Before environmental protection measures were in place, loggers cleared forests for their trees, hunters depleted wild animal populations, and companies dumped toxic waste into waterways. This glimpse into the United States reveals the devastating impact of unchecked environmental degradation. The Cuyahoga River in Cleveland, Ohio, was particularly affected, with oil, sewage, garbage, and industrial waste causing fires and killing fish. In 1952, an oil slick caught fire, but people continued to dump waste into the river. The final fire in 1969 led to the Clean Water Act and the creation of the U.S. Environmental Protection Agency. America's drinking water sources are now safer thanks to the Clean Water Act, which aims to reduce pollution and incorporates newer technologies into production processes. Smog, a visible air pollution, is caused by car exhaust, burning coal, and toxic substances, and can damage lungs and trigger allergic reactions. More recent regulations, such as those governing factory emissions, could help reduce smog. The EPA's Superfund program began in 1980 to clean up hazardous sites contaminated with toxic waste, including lead and arsenic. In many areas, these chemicals seeped into local groundwater, posing health risks to nearby residents and ecosystems. The Berkeley Pit in Montana is an example, where mining waste contaminated soil, water, and surface water with heavy metals like aluminum, arsenic, mercury, and lead. The EPA has been working on the site since the late 1980s, but it still poses health risks. Prior to regulations, the U.S. lacked oversight on logging, resulting in widespread deforestation. The National Forest Management Act of 1976 helped protect forests from excessive timber harvesting. The Endangered Species Act has protected iconic American mammals like the buffalo, which were nearly driven to extinction. Today, thanks to government intervention, these animals survive in small numbers.

Bank employees receive training to cooperate for the safety of everyone in the bank. "They're trained to cooperate with the robber because the money isn't what we're after," Earl explained. A new breakthrough in battery technology could make lithium-ion batteries cheaper, smaller, and lighter. Researchers at the University of Texas at Austin have discovered a family of anode materials that can significantly increase the charge capacity of lithium-ion battery anodes. This means that batteries used in everything from smartphones to large-scale energy storage systems could become more efficient in the future. The team, led by Professor Arumugam Manthiram, has developed a new anode material called the Interdigitated Eutectic Alloy (IdEA) that can be produced in just two simple steps. This is a major improvement over the traditional multiple-step process used to mass-produce lithium-ion battery anodes. The new material is also much thinner and lighter than the graphite and copper anodes currently used in most lithium-ion batteries. The IDEA anode is made from a foil material that is one-quarter of the thickness and half of the weight of traditional anodes. This means that a smaller, lighter rechargeable battery could be made using the new anode in the future. Professor Manthiram said, "It's exciting to have developed an inexpensive, scalable process for making electrode nanomaterials." The team's results show that the material performs well on the performance metrics needed to make a commercially viable advance in lithium-ion batteries. Recent efforts to improve lithium-ion battery electrodes have focused on building new nanomaterials atom by atom. However, the team's approach involves mechanically rolling eutectic metal alloys into nanostructured metal foils. This process is significantly simpler than the traditional method of coating graphite powder on a copper foil. The copper foil adds bulk to the electrode without improving the battery's power, and the manufacturing process is laborious and time-consuming. The team's research was published in the journal ACS Energy Letters and was funded by a grant from the U.S. Department of Energy's Office of Basic Energy Sciences, Division of Materials Sciences and Engineering. The findings have the potential to overcome a critical barrier to commercializing better batteries for use in portable electronic devices and larger applications like electric cars.

Henkel, a leading German consumer goods company, announced on Friday that it would acquire US-based laundry and home care firm The Sun Products Corp from investment fund Vestar Capital Partners for $3.6 billion, including debt. The deal, which will see Henkel expand its portfolio to include popular laundry detergents All and Sun, and fabric softener Snuggle, positions the company as the second-largest player in North America's laundry care market, behind Procter & Gamble and ahead of Church & Dwight. Analysts at Berenberg believe the acquisition will provide significant opportunities for Henkel to leverage its innovation expertise on Sun brands, driving up margins and delivering substantial returns. Henkel's shares fell 0.6% at the time of the announcement, making them the top performers on Germany's DAX index, which was down 7.1% following Britain's vote to leave the EU. The acquisition, valued at $1.6 billion in annual sales in the US and Canada, is expected to significantly boost Henkel's North American laundry and home care division, raising its sales proportion in the region to around a third from 13%. Berenberg estimates the deal will add approximately 6% to Henkel's operating profit in 2017, rising to 17% by 2019 due to revenue synergies. Baader Bank analyst Christian Weiz praised the deal, valuing Sun Products at 2.3 times sales, and reiterated his "buy" recommendation for Henkel's stock. Sun Products, with around 2,000 employees and two production sites, will become part of Henkel's laundry and home care division. Perella Weinberg Partners and Credit Suisse advised Henkel on the deal, while Cleary Gottlieb Steen & Hamilton LLP acted as its legal counsel. The acquisition is fully debt-financed and backed by Deutsche Bank, JP Morgan, and BNP Paribas.
NVIDIA waited until I left the NVISION 08 event to make a significant announcement. The big news today is that NVIDIA is enabling native support for 2 and 3-way SLI on Intel X58-based motherboards, without using any nForce 200 chips. This isn't as simple as just enabling SLI support on X58; NVIDIA wanted to ensure compatibility and generate additional revenue, so they've created a certification program. Any X58 motherboard manufacturer can submit their board for certification, which will be verified by NVIDIA. If the board passes the certification process and the manufacturer agrees to pay a certification fee (details not disclosed), the board will be certified, and NVIDIA will provide the manufacturer with a key to include in the BIOS. When you install NVIDIA drivers, they'll check for the presence of this key in the BIOS; if found, you'll be able to enable SLI, natively, on X58. Note that this support is exclusive to X58-based motherboards with Intel chips, specifically for Nehalem owners this fall. This is a huge development, as it means you can now have both CrossFire and SLI support on the right motherboard, without needing an OEM system or more exotic solutions like Skulltrail. Here are the supported configurations: you can run X58/SLI with two or three cards, including a pair of GX2s. Three-way SLI + PhysX acceleration is also supported. If you want the highest bandwidth possible, you can only use nForce 200 chips in 3 PCIe x16 slots; otherwise, you're limited to two x16s or one x16 and two x8s. The nForce 200 route may seem unnecessary due to added cost and power consumption, but it remains an option.
One of the biggest questions surrounding Formula 1 is who receives how much money. The answer is complicated because the total figure is based on the annual earnings of the Formula 1 group, but it's not entirely clear what's included and excluded. The basic breakdown is well understood, however. Ferrari gets 5% of the total, the teams collectively receive 2.5%, and the commercial rights holder (CRH) also gets 2.5%. This is justified because Ferrari is the longest-standing team. The prize fund is split in half, creating two smaller pools. One is divided equally among all teams that qualify, which requires them to be in the top 10 for two of the previous three seasons. The other is divided based on performance, with the World Champions getting 19% and the other nine teams getting varying percentages. There's also a bonus scheme, the Constructor Champions' Bonus (CCB), which rewards the top three teams based on their previous four-season performance in terms of race wins. This means Mercedes, which won 54 races, gets a smaller share than Red Bull, which won 18, and Ferrari, which won five. The current distribution is $37 million for Mercedes, $33 million for Red Bull, and $30 million for Ferrari. In other words, Mercedes gets $685,000 for each win, Red Bull $1,830,000 per win, but Ferrari gets $6 million per win. If a team wins two consecutive Constructors' Championships, it receives a one-off payment of $30 million per year. This has been a significant factor for Mercedes, which has won three championships. When you consider the entire picture, including sponsorship, merchandising, and licensing, it's clear that Ferrari is not spending much on F1. In fact, the team is likely not losing much money. The suggestion that pulling out of F1 would provide significant savings for the organization is incorrect. Ferrari would need to invest a substantial amount to promote its products, as it's currently doing so through the sport. Ferrari's marketing efforts are limited to a few theme parks and a lot of merchandise, so it's unlikely to save money by leaving. To better understand the structure, here's a chart showing the flow of money and some estimated payments.

Thankfully, there are some Catholic doctors who share this understanding.

In a briefing to the UN Security Council, Kang stated that despite Resolution 2139 calling for an end to indiscriminate weapon use, the Syrian government continues to ignore the warning. As the conflict entered its fifth year, extreme violence and brutality remained prevalent, with both sides attacking populated areas with explosives. The government still employs barrel bombs in airstrikes, while reports indicate that nearly 100 people have died and dozens more have been wounded in Damascus since January 21. This year, the UN is assisting 7.6 million internally displaced people and 3.8 million refugees. Benjamin Franklin Mitchell has been attending Philadelphia Union games since he was born, and his father, Jeffrey Mitchell, jokes that the team and his son share a birth year. Jeffrey and his wife, Rachel Mitchell, were immediately hooked after attending the first-ever Union match, and they quickly became season ticket holders and members of the Sons of Bens, a group that has become an integral part of their lives. When Benjamin was six months old, his parents brought him to the inaugural season opener and have been regulars at the River End ever since, where he's learned chants, painted tifo's, and has attended over 80 games. While they've taken Benjamin to other sports games, he's always been more engaged at Union matches, singing with the Sons of Ben and participating in tailgates. As the director of social media for the Sons of Ben, Jeffrey has been involved with the group since 2011 and chose the name Benjamin Franklin as a nod to the founding father. Middle names, he says, are usually boring, so he wanted to keep things interesting. At Sons of Ben events, Benjamin can be found collecting soda tabs for the Ronald McDonald House, earning him the nickname "the mayor" among fans. He's also well-known to the Union players, who treat him like family, and his favorite player is Zach Pfeffer, a player the Mitchell family has supported since he joined the team. Pfeffer, who was the Union's first homegrown player, has been a source of pride for fans like the Mitchells, who have grown close to him and his family. The Mitchells met Pfeffer soon after he signed with the Union, and Jeffrey was impressed by his kindness to Benjamin. As practicing Catholics, the Mitchells teach Benjamin about other faiths, and when he learned that Pfeffer celebrates Hanukkah around the same time, he was eager to learn more. Pfeffer and his family were happy to share their traditions with Benjamin, and they even surprised him with a face-time Hanukkah celebration on the first night. For Jeffrey, the experience reinforced his pride in supporting the team, which he believes are great people off the field. As for Benjamin, he may start playing organized soccer this year, but his parents are letting him choose his own path, as long as he loves the sport.

When Hinchey first joined the Rapids in 2010, he thought securing a shirt sponsor would be easier. However, after several close calls, he remains confident that he can land a deal. Hinchey acknowledges that finding a shirt sponsor is complicated, as it requires a significant investment of at least $3 million per year for three years. This is a substantial ask, and he's looking for top-tier companies based in Colorado to support the team. Despite not having a shirt sponsor, the Rapids made significant strides in 2013, including increasing attendance for the fourth consecutive year, achieving all-time high season ticket renewals, and signing their first Designated Player, Panamanian striker Gabriel Torres, in August. The Rapids are in talks with multiple potential sponsors, and Hinchey has made recent presentations to interested companies. For him, securing a shirt sponsor is a matter of great importance, and he's willing to put in the effort to make it happen. With the bike set to ship this week, Lynskey needs to act fast. Here's a closer look at the details of their fatbike. Made from 3Al-2.5V Oversize titanium, the frame features a 170mm symmetric rear end and 100mm bottom threaded bracket. Framesets will be available for $2,195, while a complete build featuring Shimano XT and Raceface components will retail for $4,342, a limited-time offer that will later increase to $5,099. Julian Assange assured reporters on Monday that Edward Snowden is "safe and healthy" and in high spirits, though he refused to disclose further details about the former NSA leaker's current whereabouts. During a conference call, Assange offered the clearest indication yet that Snowden's fate is deeply tied to the support he receives from WikiLeaks, the transparency organization that catapulted to fame three years ago when it released a massive cache of classified documents from Army Pfc. Bradley Manning. Assange revealed that WikiLeaks funded Snowden's flight from Hong Kong to Russia on Sunday and that there was no prior communication with Russian officials before his departure from Hong Kong. He also denied claims that Snowden has been debriefed by Russian intelligence agencies. Assange's tone during the call was enigmatic, leaving many questions unanswered about when Snowden and WikiLeaks first made contact and what Snowden's current status is. "The story of Snowden's travel out of Hong Kong is a fascinating one, and I'm sure it will be told one day – but not today," Assange said. According to WikiLeaks, Snowden has applied for asylum in both Iceland and Ecuador, as well as possibly other countries. Assange himself has been living in the London embassy of Ecuador for the past year, seeking asylum from Swedish authorities who have accused him of sexual assault, while he fears extradition to the U.S. in relation to the ongoing Manning case. Snowden's actions appear to mirror Assange's efforts to avoid prosecution in the U.S., with stops in Hong Kong, a part of China, and Russia. Assange said Snowden traveled from Hong Kong with a refugee document provided by the Ecuadorian government. When asked about the disconnect between the human rights records of countries like China and Russia and the transparency for which Snowden has been fighting, Assange expressed no irony. "I don't see the irony," he said. "Snowden has revealed information about mass, unlawful spying that affects us all. The U.S. administration has issued a series of threats against him and others who support his rights, which is a serious situation. Any country that assists in upholding his rights should be applauded."
Australia's National Symbols Officer recently warned Juice Media, the makers of Rap News and Honest Government Adverts, that their use of the country's coat of arms may have broken several Australian laws. This warning came despite the fact that Juice Media's videos are clearly satirical and couldn't be mistaken for official publications. In fact, the coat of arms that appeared in Honest Government Adverts didn't even have the word "Australian" spelled correctly. It's unfortunate that the Australian government struggles to distinguish between impersonation and satire, especially given their proposed legislation that could land people in jail for impersonating government agencies. Some laws against impersonating government officials are reasonable, but Australia's proposed law lacks sufficient safeguards. The recent letter to Juice Media also raises concerns about the government's judgment in applying the law. The Australian Lawyers for Human Rights has submitted to Parliament that the proposed legislation is too broad. For instance, it doesn't require any intent to deceive or actual harm caused by the impersonation. This means the law could catch conduct that doesn't necessarily involve fraud. While the proposed legislation does include an exemption for satire, critics argue that it gives the government too much leeway to attack satire that doesn't meet their standards. The limitation that conduct must be "solely" for the purpose of satire could also stifle free speech. Can a video that's created for satirical purposes be protected just because it also generates revenue? Government lawyers' struggles to understand satire are not unique to Australia. In 2005, a lawyer representing President Bush wrote to The Onion, claiming that its use of the presidential seal was a law-breaker. The Onion responded that it was "inconceivable" that anyone would misunderstand its use of the seal as anything other than parody. The White House wisely chose not to pursue the matter further. If they had, they likely would have lost on First Amendment grounds. Australia, however, doesn't have a First Amendment or a written bill of rights, which has civil libertarians worried that the proposed law could be used to attack political commentary. We hope the Australian government either kills the bill or amends the law to include a requirement of intent to deceive and a more robust exemption for satire. In response to the proposed legislation, Juice Media has created an "honest" government advert in their own style.

Delhi aims to increase its forest cover from 20.08% to 25% over the next five years, a goal that will require adding around six to seven hectares of green space to the already polluted city. To achieve this, the government plans to densify existing forests and bring new areas under green cover. A meeting of greening agencies from various departments, municipal bodies, and the Centre will be held in April to discuss an action plan. As part of this plan, the Department of Forests and Wildlife is exploring the possibility of setting aside 6 to 8 metres of green space on both sides of upcoming elevated road projects. For example, when the right of way is granted for signal-free elevated road projects, the PWD's horticulture department will need to ensure that these areas are densely planted. The government will also consider planting trees over existing drains, such as those in Najafgarh and Shahdara, as well as empty plots under the DDA. In addition, the government plans to develop 42 forests in the city by constructing pathways and using solar lights along them. This model will be evaluated by third parties, such as mohalla sabhas or eco-task forces comprising school students, to ensure that the plan remains participatory and effective.

Leon Vynehall, a UK producer, has a unique approach to beats and frequencies that sets him apart from other engineers. This unconventional style is precisely what makes his take on house music so appealing. At its core, his music features rich chords, uplifting vocal samples, and airy atmospheres. The gritty sound is a natural result of his use of worn vinyl, faulty drum machines, and other equipment, which serves as a reminder that you can't have perfection without some imperfections. Vynehall's new mini-album, Music for the Uninvited, is a tribute to the warm, fuzzy sounds of cassettes he grew up listening to in his mother's car. The record is filled with beautiful, melodic tracks, such as the string quartet "Inside the Deku Tree" and the dreamy "Christ Air," which recall the poetic sensibilities of Metro Area and Floating Points. The album also includes club tracks that are infused with light and color, making them perfect for dancing or simply enjoying the music. Vynehall's latest release, Music for the Uninvited, is set to drop on March 17 on Martyn's 3024 label.

A disturbing incident involving a neighbor shooting and killing her dogs has left one Albuquerque woman fuming, as authorities have failed to take any action. Stephanie Fonseca, the owner of the English Bulldogs, Chonchis, Chola, and Chapo, claims her neighbor took the law into his own hands, shooting the dogs without provocation. On a Saturday morning, Fonseca's children were playing outside when they discovered their pets had escaped. She immediately called Animal Welfare, and upon learning what happened, she rushed out to look for them. After searching the neighborhood, she encountered an Albuquerque Police Officer who informed her that someone had shot at her pets. Fonseca found the bodies of her dogs, including two smaller puppies, gunned down in a nearby home. Witnesses reported that 15 rounds were fired into the dogs, leaving Fonseca heartbroken. According to Animal Welfare, the neighbor shot the dogs because they were trying to attack his Chihuahuas. However, Fonseca disputes this, claiming the dogs were trapped under the fence and could have been removed without harm. Fonseca expressed her outrage and frustration, saying, "There was no mercy shown to these animals whatsoever." She also stated that her dogs were friendly and gentle, and that the neighbor's actions were inexcusable. The neighbor is not facing any charges, which has left Fonseca seeking answers and exploring her legal options. She has also reached out to animal rights groups in hopes of finding justice for her beloved pets.
A federal court judge has reaffirmed that the Navajo Nation can continue its lawsuit against Urban Outfitters Inc. under the Indian Arts and Crafts Act. The Navajo Nation filed its lawsuit in US District Court in New Mexico in February 2012, alleging trademark violations, unfair competition, and a violation of the arts and crafts act. The lawsuit was filed after Urban Outfitters sold items such as clothing, jewelry, and accessories labeled as "Navajo" on its website and in stores. Following a cease-and-desist letter from the tribe, Urban Outfitters removed the products in October 2011. On December 21, US District Judge Bruce D. Black ruled that the tribe has Article III standing, which requires an "injury in fact," according to court documents. Urban Outfitters argued that the tribe lacks standing due to a lack of injury in fact, as stated in court documents. Black issued his opinion and order in response to a set of summary judgments filed by both the Navajo Nation and Urban Outfitters in late 2014. He also denied both summary judgments in his 10-page opinion and order. The Indian Arts and Crafts Act, enacted by Congress in 1935, aims to protect Native American cultural property. The original statute created the Indian Arts and Crafts Board within the US Department of the Interior, which was tasked with promoting and protecting Indian arts and crafts and creating trademarks to safeguard such items. A 1990 amendment granted tribes the right to bring lawsuits against individuals or entities selling products that falsely suggest they are Indian-made or produced by a specific Native American tribe or arts and crafts organization. The Navajo Nation's 2012 complaint stated that the tribe registered "Navajo" as a trademark in connection with the sale of goods and services as early as 1943. At the time of the complaint, the tribe had registered 86 trademarks that include "Navajo" for a range of goods and services.

It's crucial that running your own full Bitcoin node is straightforward and accessible to everyone, especially considering the thousands of nodes already in operation worldwide. If you want to verify something for yourself, rather than relying on a few websites, running the code yourself is the most reliable approach. But do liberty and freedom truly rely on institutions and corporate actions, or are they preserved by the collective will of individuals? I find it absurd to think that a handful of companies can provide the same level of security against tampering and control as individual auditors. Bitcoin's purpose is to give individuals control over their finances, which is only necessary when it conflicts with the interests of the state or large corporations. So, why do web companies in China censor the internet, and why does the banking system restrict certain transactions? No single company can resist pressure from the state, and it's unlikely they'd be in its interest to do so. Currently, running an individual node is already cumbersome, taking hours or days to process actions, and it's only getting worse as Bitcoin grows. For Bitcoin to succeed, we need to build a culture of individual responsibility. This doesn't mean everyone needs to run a full node, but it does mean there should be plenty of people who do, and there shouldn't be significant barriers to running one if that's what you choose. What's truly important is that countless individuals, not just companies, enforce the freedom and security of Bitcoin. This isn't about blocking the possibility of raising the block size, but about being conservative and preserving this fundamental property. This is the unique aspect of Bitcoin that we shouldn't lose sight of, even if a new startup might try to compete with PayPal.
The Ledger Wallet is considered one of the most secure Bitcoin wallets available. Its CEO, Eric Larchevêque, is a prime example of this security. With over 1,000 bitcoins stored on his wallet, valued at approximately $450,000, he truly understands the importance of security. I had the opportunity to interview him at CES, where he discussed his company's approach to security. Ledger Wallet's first product was a USB key that required a PIN code to unlock. If the key were lost, the wallet's security features kicked in. The private key was needed to send bitcoins, and the wallet could even be recovered and transferred to a new one. This multi-factor approach added an extra layer of security, including a physical element. Even if a computer was infected with malware, hackers wouldn't be able to access the bitcoins, as the most critical part of the code was executed directly from the wallet. The company recently showcased its new Ledger Blue wallet, which features a touch screen, supports USB, Bluetooth, and NFC, and utilizes similar security mechanisms as its previous wallet. When asked about the current climate around bitcoin, Larchevêque expressed his continued bullishness, but noted that he doesn't expect bitcoin to become as widespread as previously thought. His personal ownership of over 1,000 bitcoins speaks to his confidence in the company's security measures.
Here's the rewritten text: The 100 Days Campaign, fought in 1815, marked the final confrontation of the Napoleonic Wars and effectively ended Napoleon Bonaparte's dreams of regaining the French throne. After escaping from his exile on the island of Elba, Bonaparte landed near Cannes with a small group of 600 guardsmen and made his way towards Grenoble, where he was confronted by men from the 5th Regiment, sent to arrest him. Undeterred, Bonaparte boldly declared: "Soldiers of the 5th, you can shoot your emperor if you dare." None did, and as he marched towards Paris, his military force grew exponentially, drawing in thousands of old soldiers and regular troops who pledged their allegiance to him. So many joined that a notice appeared in the Place Vendome: "From Napoleon to Louis XVIII, my good brother - there's no need to send more troops, I have enough." On March 19, the Bourbons fled to Belgium, and a day later, Bonaparte took control and began preparing for the impending Allied invasion. By the time he returned to France two months later, he had amassed an army of 280,000 men, with half again as many expected within another two months. However, the Allied forces, numbering nearly 1,000,000 men, would soon pose a formidable challenge. Initially, only England's Duke of Wellington (110,000 men) and Prussia's Field Marshal Blucher (120,000) were close enough to threaten France, prompting Bonaparte to strike before the numbers told against him. With incredible speed, he invaded Belgium with 125,000 men, aiming to split the armies of Wellington and Blucher and defeat them separately. His strategy worked remarkably well, and he dispatched Marshal Ney to hold off the Anglo-Allied force.
Farmers and ranchers along the Central Coast and across the country are being invited to participate in a groundbreaking government survey on organic food production. As part of the US Department of Agriculture's routine census, this is the first time questions about organics have been included. Congressman Sam Farr, who represents a significant portion of the Salinas Valley and is the ranking member on the House Agricultural Appropriations Committee, is behind this move. Farr played a key role in legitimizing the organic industry in California during his time as a state assemblyman. "I'm delighted that organic production is now part of the census," Farr stated. "This is a significant development, as it signals that organics are being treated on par with other commodities in the agricultural sector." According to Farr, it's essential that farmers and ranchers provide accurate information about their organic food production, as the USDA requires reliable data to make informed financial decisions. The USDA survey aims to assess the economic impact of organics and determine which programs and services are necessary for certified growers.

Seeking credible news? Subscribe to our free newsletters. I started my day by opening the LA Times, and the business section's front page caught my attention with a headline: Steep drop in jobless rate sparks criticism from Obama's detractors. I thought it was remarkable - one tweet from Jack Welch sparked a heated controversy that made the Times' front page. What's striking is the power these individuals wield in shaping the news agenda. However, there might be an unappreciated twist at play. Although the Bureau of Labor Statistics' data wasn't manipulated, the headline number, based on a telephone survey of households, can be volatile from month to month. In September, the employment figure spiked, but it's unclear if this was a genuine increase or a statistical anomaly. The Bureau of Labor Statistics also revealed that they had undercounted jobs by 386,000 from April 2011 to March 2012. It's possible that they've continued to undercount jobs since then. If so, the September number might not only be accurate but also compensating for the undercount over the past six months. This trend is reflected in the chart below, which suggests that the September employment number aligns with expectations for a steady but modest economic recovery. The irony lies in this: if the Bureau of Labor Statistics has indeed been undercounting, the jobs picture may have looked bleaker during the first half of the year, which is precisely when it hurt President Obama the most. This could mean that the wingnuts might be more than just misinformed - they might have their views completely reversed. It's possible that the BLS numbers were actually working against Obama, not in his favor. The September spike corrected this, but it may have been too late to make a significant difference.

A French court has recently found Monsanto guilty of chemical poisoning. The court upheld a 2012 ruling that Monsanto was liable for chemical poisoning of a French farmer, Paul Francois. Francois claims that after inhaling Monsanto's Lasso weedkiller, he suffered from neurological problems, including memory loss, headaches, and stammering. Francois says that the company failed to provide adequate warnings on the product label. The appeal court in Lyon confirmed the initial judgment, finding Monsanto criminally responsible for the intoxication and ordering the company to offer full compensation to Francois. Monsanto's lawyer has stated that the company will continue to appeal the decision. The victim's lawyer, Jean-Daniel Bretzner, believes that the compensation offered by Monsanto will be modest and likely nonexistent. This ruling is part of a growing trend of countries opting out of the GMO experiment. Greece, Latvia, Germany, and Scotland have all decided to exclude Monsanto's genetically modified crops, including MON810 GM Maize, from their markets. The decision to opt out is largely driven by concerns over the potential risks associated with GMOs. However, the passage of the TPP arrangement could change the situation. The TPP would allow giant corporations like Monsanto to force their products into the market, despite legislation aimed at preventing such a move. This has raised concerns about the potential impact on consumer rights and the environment.
The director of the upcoming film "Stonewall," Roland Emmerich, has responded to the backlash over the movie's trailer, which some queer and trans activists claim misrepresents the events of the 1969 Stonewall Riots. Released this week, the trailer has sparked intense controversy, with some activists accusing the film of whitewashing the narrative and erasing the drag queens, transgender patrons, and queer people of color who were present during the riots. Emmerich defended his film on Facebook, emphasizing that it is a "fictionalized drama" of the events at Stonewall, rather than a historically accurate depiction. He wrote that the movie does, however, honor the real-life activists who were there, including Marsha P. A proposed bill in Congress to aid homeowners affected by the mortgage crisis enjoys broad bipartisan support, despite a recent White House warning that it may be vetoed. However, a provision in the bill that has little to do with housing loans has drawn attention: a measure that would require credit card companies and electronic payment processors, such as PayPal, to submit annual reports to the IRS detailing their payments to merchants who receive more than $10,000 and process over 200 transactions per year. The provision was introduced without debate as a way to offset the costs of the housing bailout, and is expected to raise approximately $9.8 billion in revenue over 10 years by increasing tax compliance and encouraging merchants to accurately report their income. Some lawmakers, including Chair Nydia Velásquez of the House Small Business Committee, have expressed concerns that the reporting requirements would create significant technical and financial challenges for banks and small businesses, as the relationship between electronic transactions and actual income is often tenuous. Velásquez pointed out that chargebacks, merchant discounts, and other fees can significantly impact a business's profitability, and that even careful compliance could lead to costly IRS audits. Small business associations have also raised objections, citing examples of businesses that take deposits via credit card that do not constitute true income. Additionally, privacy groups have raised concerns that the reporting requirements could lead to the collection of sensitive information, such as tax ID numbers, and that an aggregate reporting requirement could easily become a demand for more detailed data. The bill is expected to be sent back to the House for a straightforward up-or-down vote next week, which critics argue is a bad process that will not allow for meaningful discussion of the provision. Despite the president's veto threat, the popular housing relief bill is expected to pass with a wide margin.
Security experts at Imperva are warning that hackers are targeting vulnerabilities in the PHP web application development platform, which is used by most websites. This is consistent with a long-standing trend of hackers focusing on widely used third-party components to maximize their returns. PHP is an alternative to Microsoft's Active Server Pages technology and is commonly used on Linux servers. According to Amichai Shulman, CTO at Imperva, "Exploits against PHP applications can have far-reaching consequences, as compromised hosts can be used to launch attacks on other servers, putting the entire web at risk." With PHP powering over 80% of all websites, including major sites like Facebook and Wikipedia, it's clear that the security community needs to pay more attention to this issue.

Security footage showed Teixeira and two other Berkeley students, Eric Cuellar and Hazhir Kargaran, laughing and chasing the bird in a wildlife garden area. The scene was interrupted when Teixeira, who was intoxicated, beheaded the bird and discarded its body, flipping its head into nearby rocks. Cuellar, now 26, and Kargaran, now 27, each pleaded guilty to reduced misdemeanor charges last year and were fined and sentenced to community service. Teixeira, of Placerville, California, pleaded guilty to a felony charge in May 2013, avoiding trial on that charge and two other felony counts that could have resulted in up to eight years in prison. He recently completed a 190-day prison boot camp instead of serving time behind bars. Defense attorney Michael Pariente said Teixeira received top honors in the program and also passed the California bar exam. However, whether Teixeira will be able to practice law depends on whether he can get his sentence reduced to a misdemeanor after completing probation. Pariente sought a lenient sentence of 18 months' probation, citing that Teixeira's name is forever linked with animal abuse and the incident will haunt him for life. Pariente acknowledged the incident occurred while Teixeira was heavily intoxicated. The judge, however, imposed a harsh sentence, stating that Teixeira's animal shelter commitment will last as long as his probation, regardless of other employment or school enrollment.

A University of British Columbia undergraduate studying geography has conducted a study on the impact of Airbnb on Vancouver's rental housing market. Iain Marjoribanks, with guidance from the B.C. Non-Profit Housing Association, investigated how many people are renting out an extra bed or two on Airbnb and how many are operating as commercial entities. Marjoribanks found that 77 per cent of Airbnb's revenue comes from hosts who do not live in the units they list on the platform, which he calls "commercial hosts" rather than the more casual hosts that Airbnb typically portrays. The top 10 per cent of hosts, or "mega hosts," generate about 27 per cent of Airbnb's revenue and list three or more listings. These hosts often rent out entire houses like hotels or multiple properties throughout the city. Airbnb disputes Marjoribanks' conclusions. In April, Aaron Zifkin, Airbnb Canada's country manager, stated that the majority of Airbnb listings worldwide are in people's primary residences, rented out occasionally. However, Marjoribanks' research suggests that only 47 per cent of hosts list their properties as primary residences, and if Airbnb were no longer available, Vancouver's rental vacancy rate would likely double from 0.8 to 2 per cent. Victoria is considering regulating Airbnb, but Marjoribanks believes this is a challenging task. He cites San Francisco and Portland as examples of cities that have successfully regulated short-term rentals by requiring hosts to register their properties and limiting the number of days a property can be rented out. However, even in these cities, compliance rates are relatively low, at around 9.5 per cent in San Francisco and 11 per cent in Portland. Marjoribanks argues that cities cannot effectively regulate hosts without knowing their identities and locations. As a solution, Marjoribanks proposes that cities or the province fine Airbnb for violating regulations, and then the company would pass those fines on to non-compliant hosts. "If you're taking a full house off the market all year round, you should be paying for the cost that transfers to society," Marjoribanks said.

Researchers have found that regular exercise is generally beneficial for health, but excessive running may actually shorten one's lifespan. A recent study published in HealthDay reported that people who don't exercise at all and those who are high-mileage runners both tend to live shorter lives compared to those who run moderately. However, the study's findings are not yet fully understood, and the researchers are unclear about what constitutes the optimal amount of running for health and longevity. Dr. Martin Matsumura, co-director of the Cardiovascular Research Institute at the Lehigh Valley Health Network, led the study, which analyzed data from over 3,800 runners aged 35 or older. The study found that nearly 70 percent of the runners reported running more than 20 miles per week, with an average age of 42 years old. The researchers also examined factors such as painkiller use, prescription medications, heart risk factors, diabetes, high blood pressure, and family history of chronic illness, but none of these factors explained the shorter lifespans associated with high-mileage runners compared to moderate runners. Interestingly, the study found that average runners were more likely to use painkillers than high-mileage runners. Dr. James O'Keefe, director of preventive cardiology at the Mid-American Heart Institute, suggested that excessive running may be related to life-shortening effects due to the "wear and tear" it causes. He recommended that the "sweet spot" for running is a slow to moderate pace for about 2.5 hours per week, which is roughly two to three times per week. Despite this, Dr. Matsumura emphasized that he does not advise people to stop running entirely, but rather to be mindful of their own limits.

Jayant Patel, an Indian-born US citizen known as "Dr Death" by Australian media, worked at a Queensland hospital between 2003 and 2005. He was found guilty at the Brisbane Supreme Court of the manslaughter of three patients and causing grievous bodily harm to a patient. Patel had denied responsibility for the deaths and harm of Gerry Kemps, James Phillips, Mervyn Morris, and Ian Vowles. Prosecutors argued that his operations fell below the standard of a competent surgeon, citing botched operations, misdiagnoses, the removal of healthy organs, and sloppy surgical techniques during his time at Bundaberg Base Hospital. It was also revealed that nurses had hidden patients from him due to their concerns about the quality of his work. The court heard that prosecutors had requested a 10-year sentence. Before arriving in Australia, Patel had been barred from conducting surgery in the US states of New York and Oregon.
MnemOdyssey, LLC is sponsoring this investigational online research study, led by principal investigator Steven de Belle. The study is open to individuals who voluntarily choose to participate. Its main goal is to assess the general population's ability to remember faces and names, with the aim of identifying people with exceptional memory who may be eligible to participate in future studies. Participants will be asked to learn a series of faces and names over two days, with each session taking no more than ten minutes. You must be at least 18 years old to participate and can withdraw at any time without penalty. The study aims to collect data from one million participants. While the risks of participating are minimal, they may include feeling bored or dissatisfied with the test performance. The study is free, but it does not offer any direct compensation. By participating, you will receive your memory test score for faces and names. To ensure your participation, please follow all instructions and answer questions truthfully. Microsoft is gearing up for a surprisingly substantial Patch Tuesday in January, which will cover seven security bulletins addressing eight separate vulnerabilities. Typically, January releases have featured just one or two bulletins. This latest batch includes a single critical fix for a remote code execution issue in Media Player, while the remaining six "important" bulletins will tackle the BEAST SSL issue and various information disclosure bugs, escalation of privilege issues, and an update to Microsoft's SEHOP technology to boost its defense-in-depth capabilities for legacy applications. Some argue that the BEAST SSL issue, classified as "important" rather than critical, is debatable, as the attack primarily affects web servers using SSLv3/TLSv1 encryption. Microsoft initially promised a patch in December but delayed it due to testing issues. Notably, security expert Paul Henry notes that despite the hype surrounding the BEAST attack, actual attacks have not materialized, and the issue remains classified as "important" by Microsoft. The upcoming month is expected to be busy with patching, as Adobe and Oracle have scheduled quarterly updates for January 10th and 17th, respectively.

Tesla CEO Elon Musk has clarified that the upcoming Model Y electric crossover will be built on a similar architecture to the Model 3, rather than being an entirely new vehicle platform. This decision was made in an effort to bring the car to market faster, after Musk had previously claimed it would be "completely different" from anything Tesla has produced before. Musk attributed the change of heart to his executive team, saying they "reined him back from the cliffs of insanity" and encouraged him to adopt a more efficient approach. This shift in strategy means that the Model Y will use substantial carryover from the Model 3, rather than the 12-volt battery architecture used in Tesla's other vehicles. By reusing existing components, the production process can be simplified and the need for lengthy electric wiring can be reduced, allowing for more automation on the production line. Musk still plans to introduce more robots into the production line to increase efficiency. In contrast, many automakers use a single vehicle architecture to manufacture different vehicles, which simplifies the manufacturing process and reduces costs. The Model Y will share many of its components with the Model 3, as well as some features from Tesla's other vehicles, such as the Model X's falcon-wing doors. The car is still expected to enter production by the end of 2019 or early 2020, and is seen as a crucial step towards Tesla's goal of delivering 1 million cars by 2020. The company has already shipped 47,000 cars in the first half of 2017, and the Model Y will be a key contributor to its production targets.
I'm often struck by the sheer length of the release notes document Unreal provides with each new release. Epic packs a multitude of features into each and every one, making it easy for new features to get overlooked. One recent addition that caught my attention in the latest release (4.17) was the ability to set up a spectator camera for VR. This feature allows you to render one image for the VR headset and a completely different image for the screen. However, the documentation for this feature seems somewhat sparse, and at the time of writing, the link to the feature in the Unreal release notes was no longer active. Fortunately, it wasn't too difficult to figure out, so I thought I'd share what I learned. To add a spectator camera, you can follow these three simple steps. First, create a Scene Capture Component 2D, which functions as the camera for the spectator. I attached mine to the character actor I was already using. Next, create a Render Target, which serves as the holding place for the image captured by the Scene Capture Component 2D. Finally, set your spectator screen mode and texture, and the 4.17 release included improvements for displaying the spectator screen with the "Set Spectator Screen Mode Node," such as showing just one eye or with or without distortion. For our purposes, we simply set it to a texture and retrieved the texture from the render target. With these steps complete, you should now be able to move the Scene Capture Component 2D anywhere and capture a good, non-head-mounted view of the gameplay. Please note, however, that this will impact your frame rate, as you'll now be rendering an additional image per frame. Use this feature at your own discretion.

A quietly released report on Christmas Eve has shown that Australia's greenhouse gas emissions rose by about 1% in 2014-15, compared to the previous year. This increase is largely attributed to Australia's international reporting of its emissions. According to the report, Australia's emissions increased by 0.8% last financial year compared to the previous one, and 1.3% when land use and deforestation were taken into account. In 2014-15, Australia generated 549.3 mega-tonnes of carbon dioxide. The Australian government had pledged to reduce emissions by 26% to 28% by 2030 at the Paris climate talks, but the current trend may put pressure on the government to do more, especially given the global agreement to limit the global temperature rise to 2C. The report highlights increases in electricity, stationary energy, transport, fugitive emissions, and industrial processes and product use. However, it notes that there was a significant decline – 3.8% – in emissions from agriculture. Emissions from electricity generation rose 3% in 2014-15, despite flat demand from consumers in the same period. Power generation from black coal increased by 1.4%, while brown coal generation increased by 9.7%. The report also shows that electricity from wind and other renewables (excluding small-scale solar) increased 12.2% on the previous 12 months, but hydroelectric generation fell by 30.3%. Electricity generation was the largest source of emissions, accounting for 34% in 2014-15. Prof. Will Steffen from the Climate Council expressed concern over the December figures, stating that Australia needed to urgently reduce its reliance on coal to meet its global commitments. "If we're putting more into the atmosphere than the year before, we're heading in the wrong direction," he said. "We've got to drop emissions fast. We've got to get out of fossil fuels very quickly, coal first – there can be no new coalmines anywhere in the world."

Industry-funded flu vaccine studies are more likely to be published in top journals and cited in the scientific community than those without such funding, according to a study of 274 studies conducted by researchers from Italy's Cochrane Vaccine Field. This was revealed by a survey published in the journal, which found that having a study partially or fully funded by the pharmaceutical industry was a key factor in boosting its prominence. In contrast, a good methodology had little impact on these factors. Furthermore, the study found that only 18% of flu vaccine studies provided evidence to support their conclusions, suggesting that many were of poor quality. The researchers also discovered that studies supporting the effectiveness of flu vaccines were often poorly designed and had lower concordance rates than those that questioned its effectiveness. Moreover, government-funded studies were significantly less likely to find the flu vaccine effective than industry-funded studies. As researcher Tom Jefferson noted, "The financial size of your sponsor is one of the levers that can access top journals." Pharmaceutical companies often order reprints of studies supporting their products, purchase publicity space in journals, and even advertise these services on their websites. Jefferson concluded, "It's time for journals to make full disclosure of their sources of funding."
During a January 10 confirmation hearing, Senator Jeff Sessions, the nominee for attorney general, was questioned by Senator John Kennedy (R-LA) about his views on the Second Amendment. Kennedy asked Sessions to share his thoughts on the issue, drawing parallels with Louisiana's approach, where love is considered the answer, but the right to own a gun is also protected under the Constitution. Sessions replied that he believed the Second Amendment was a personal right that was constitutionally protected and explicitly stated in the Constitution, alongside other fundamental rights and liberties. He emphasized that this right was not absolute and could be revoked for those who broke the law, stating that a criminal who possessed a firearm to commit a crime forfeited their right to own one. Sessions argued that this could lead to additional penalties and convictions, which he considered a legitimate and responsible restraint on the Second Amendment.
A 19-year-old man from Tennessee says he was lucky to escape severe burns after his vape mod exploded in his pocket. Now, he's sharing his terrifying experience to warn others about the potential dangers of vaping. Last September, Garrett Preston, a Dunkin' Donuts employee, was finishing his first shift when he decided to make a sandwich in the store's oven. Inside his left pocket, he had his car keys, phone, and a vape mod. Just three seconds into making the sandwich, a "firestorm" erupted from his pocket. Preston frantically ran to the back of the store, ripped off his pants, and saw his keys and vape mod smoking on the ground. A coworker rushed to his side and called 911 after seeing his severe burns. Preston was rushed to the Vanderbilt Burn Center, where he received treatment for two third-degree burns on his thigh and knee, as well as a severe burn on his stomach. After 14 hours in the hospital, Preston was released, but his recovery took a month and a week before doctors cleared him of needing a skin graft. Preston believes the vape box exploded due to a faulty design. "I just want to share my experience so that others don't make the same mistake," he said. "These vape batteries can cause serious damage if not handled properly." Preston's vape mod was missing its protective case, which exposed the batteries. He thinks his keys touching the batteries caused them to "short circuit" and ignite a fire. The teen notes that cuts in the battery wrapping can expose the batteries and increase the risk of danger. The battery case was removed by a user who was trying to adjust the wattage, but the screws had fallen off. Preston had been using the vape mod without issue for about six months before the accident. Despite his injuries, he continues to vape, which he started as a way to quit smoking regular cigarettes. While many of his friends vape without issue, Preston is the "unlucky" one who fell victim to an accident. He believes that many stories about vape explosions are caused by user error, and he hopes his story can help others avoid making the same mistake.

The Federal Reserve made a surprise announcement today when its Open Market Committee decided not to taper its $85 billion in monthly bond purchases due to concerns about a weak economy. The news sparked an immediate rally across the markets as investors rushed to invest in stocks and commodities. The Dow Jones Industrial Average surged 141 points in just one minute, from 15,485 to 15,626, and then added another 50 points over the next two and a half hours, closing up 147 points for the day. While many are celebrating the Fed's decision, I see it as a warning rather than a solution. The difference between "the Fed will not taper" and "the Fed cannot taper" is significant, as the former implies that the Fed can control the pace of tapering, while the latter acknowledges that its actions are limited by the laws of economics. If the Fed were to stop its $85 billion bond purchasing program, interest rates would skyrocket, the dollar would strengthen, deflation would become apparent, and the stock market, real estate market, and auto sales would likely collapse. The US economy would also tank. The Fed is aware of these consequences and wants to avoid them, but ultimately, the laws of physics cannot be bypassed. Despite this, the market's reaction seems somewhat muted, with the Dow Jones only rising by 150 points. This suggests that the Fed's decision may not be as effective in supporting the market as many believe. In reality, the Fed has little control over the markets and its actions are largely reactive. I worry that the market may reverse itself tomorrow and experience another downturn.

Overly aggressive medical interventions, such as angioplasty and clot-dissolving medications, are a major contributor to deaths following a heart attack. These interventions, which are intended to be administered promptly, actually increase the risk of death if they occur too late. Research conducted by the Quebec Healthcare Assessment Agency in Montreal revealed that the timing of these treatments plays a crucial role. When administered immediately, they can be lifesaving, but even brief delays can be fatal. For instance, angioplasty procedures are supposed to take place within 90 minutes of hospital admission, yet this deadline was not met in 68 percent of cases, and clot-dissolving medications were administered after the recommended 30-minute window in 54 percent of instances. Consequently, patients receiving late treatment were nearly twice as likely to perish as those receiving the same treatments within the recommended timeframe.

It wasn't until recently that making a law required a press release and accompanying photo from a PR firm. A new law in Washington state, which is the first of its kind in the US, was recently passed to curb "malicious" RFID spying. When the law takes effect in July, individuals who scan someone remotely without their knowledge or consent, for fraudulent or illegal purposes, will face charges as a Class C felony. This addresses the obvious misuse of the technology. However, the original bill included an opt-in provision, which would have required retailers and others to track activity via discount cards, implants, or ID cards only with your consent. Unfortunately, corporate lobbying led to this provision being removed from the bill, leaving it out of the final law. Contact information can be found by visiting the link below.
Physicists can't directly capture an image of a Higgs boson, but they can photograph its relatives, which helps them understand atomic physics. A groundbreaking picture has been taken: the first-ever snapshot of an atom's shadow. This is the smallest thing that can be seen in visible light. Researchers at Griffith University in Australia, led by Dave Kielpinski, successfully captured a single ytterbium ion in an electric field and held it in place for photography. By shining a specific frequency of light on it and using an ultra-high-resolution microscope, they were able to focus on the ion and capture its shadow on a CCD detector. The team's goal was to determine how many atoms it takes to cast a shadow, and they found it was just one. This achievement has practical applications, such as figuring out the types of light needed to observe certain biological processes without damaging them.

When a company is on the verge of collapse, one of the warning signs is when its CEO becomes defensive about vague claims that everything will work out. Too often, these CEOs are trying to deflect criticism by projecting confidence, often with a hint of arrogance. They'll say things like, "I've done it before, so I'll do it again," which can come across as dismissive and condescending. On the other hand, CEOs who have nothing to hide are usually transparent about their reasoning and willing to provide detailed explanations. They won't waste time trying to win over skeptics, but they will share information with analysts who have legitimate questions. For example, during the second presidential debate, moderator Candy Crowley raised concerns about Romney's budget numbers. His response reminded me of a typical CEO who's trying to cover up problems. Given the ambitious goals Romney is setting, such as cutting taxes, closing loopholes, and reducing the deficit, it's no surprise that many Americans are skeptical. So, what gives Romney confidence that his numbers add up? Why won't he share more information about his budget plan? If Romney were a public company CEO, many sophisticated investors would likely conclude that he's hiding something, because he knows his numbers don't add up.

Recently, I came across a stepped-triangular (ST) approximation that can be implemented as a series of recursive running sum (RRS) filters. This approximation was introduced by Jovanovic-Dolecek and Mitra [1] as a quantized approximation of a low-pass filter (LPF). An example of this approximation is shown in Figure 1. The original paper [1] provided a method for implementing the stepped-triangular approximation for a LPF impulse response, resulting in a transfer function that can be represented as a cascade of recursive sums. The familiar recursive running sum, H rrs (z), has a flat impulse response. Another recursive sparse sum, H rss (z), uses every s samples, as shown in Figure 2. Both of these recursive forms can be implemented with an efficient recursive structure that combines an integrator and difference combination. To generate the stepped-triangular approximation from a LPF impulse response, a simple rounding function g[n] is used. This rounding function quantizes the impulse response h[n] into one of three stepped-triangular approximation types. Figures 1 and 4 demonstrate an example of this approach. The three types determine the number of samples at the peak: less than s, equal to s, or greater than s, where s is the number of samples per step. Figure 4 shows an example of a ST-approximation frequency response, where the blue line represents the original frequency response and the red line represents the approximation using the stepped-triangular approach.
Netflix is planning to release at least 34 original movies in 2017, with a goal of 80 next year, according to its chief content officer Ted Sarandos. This is more than most movie studios release in a year. For comparison, Disney, which owns Pixar, Marvel Studios, and Lucasfilm, released 13 movies in the US in 2016, while Time Warner's Warner Bros., New Line, Fine Line, Warner Independent, and Picturehouse studios combined released 23 films. Sony, which released 38 movies in 2016, still fell short of Netflix's ambitious plans. In fact, Netflix is releasing more films than all these major studios combined. Major US movie studios have seen a decline in output since the golden age of Hollywood in the 1920s to 1940s. Prior to the rise of TV and streaming, studios often released dozens or even hundreds of films per year. However, with increased competition from TV and rising ticket prices, US studios now focus on fewer, bigger releases. Countries like India produce significantly more feature films than the US, but the US still leads in terms of box-office revenue. To support its ambitious movie-release schedule, Netflix is increasing its content budget, which will be between $7-8 billion next year, up from $6 billion in 2017. Netflix's content budget will also cover a range of films, from $1 million buys at Sundance to large-scale productions that typically get box-office releases. The company has more movies in the works, including Martin Scorsese's The Irishman, set for release in 2019. Netflix is ending 2017 with its biggest film ever, setting the stage for its aggressive film push in 2018. This year, the streaming service released movies featuring Brad Pitt, Will Smith, and films that competed at the Cannes Film Festival. According to Sarandos, some of Netflix's recent releases showed box-office potential. However, these films did not receive theatrical releases, highlighting the difference between Netflix and other studios. Netflix's release schedule is not tied to the theatrical market, giving it more freedom to experiment. While Netflix spends a significant amount on marketing, its films do not follow the same big-budget rollout plans as many major theatrical releases.

Nearly two million children will lose health coverage starting next month unless Congress renews funding for the Children's Health Insurance Program (CHIP) by Friday, according to a new report. State CHIP administrators say that parents are already panicking, and permanent damage has been done to the program, which provides health insurance to children who are not covered through other programs. CHIP covers nine million children nationwide. Congress failed to authorize funding in September, and some states have begun warning parents to get their last medical visits in while the money lasts. "Our phones are ringing off the hook," said Cathy Caldwell, director of the Alabama Bureau of Children's Health Insurance. "We have panicked families wondering what their options are. It's very stressful here in Alabama." Health policy experts, doctors, and politicians are puzzled by the crisis, as CHIP is a universally popular program that has never been used as a political pawn. Democrats in Congress urged their Republican colleagues to act by Friday, with one Democrat saying, "This is the ultimate bad Christmas carol story." CHIP has bipartisan support, but Republicans have focused on passing a tax reform bill, their first legislative victory since 2017. "Congress must get CHIP done before they leave for the holidays," said Joan Alker, executive director of the Georgetown University Research Center. "Families need the peace of mind that their child's coverage will not disappear as the new year begins." The report found that 20 states will run out of money in the first quarter of 2018. "Never before has Congress let CHIP funding lapse for this long a period of time," Alker said. A continuing resolution that Congress passed earlier this month shifts around $1.24 billion in CHIP funds to the states running out of money the fastest, but this comes at the expense of other states, which will see their share of redistribution funds reduced, accelerating their timeline for running out of money. The 20 "winner" states will only have sufficient funds to operate their programs through January, and all of them will come up short for February.

Creditors trying to collect on past debts have encountered a hurdle when attempting to seek payment through the courts, mainly due to insufficient ownership records and flawed documentation, according to a recent report by The New York Times. The National Collegiate Student Loan Trusts, one of the largest owners of private student loan debt, has reportedly been unable to present the necessary legal documents to prove it owns more than $5 billion in student loan debt. This has resulted in many debts being automatically dismissed in court. This custom ROM is specifically designed for the ASUS ZenWatch 2 and is now in its second alpha release. The best part is that it will get you Android Wear 2 on your smartwatch. Airport lounges have a lax security system that allows anyone to enter, provided they can produce a fake QR code. In 2012, computer security expert Przemek Jaroszewski discovered that he couldn't enter an airline lounge in Warsaw because the automated reader mistakenly rejected his boarding card. He wrote a 600-line JavaScript program that generated a QR code for a fictional business-class traveler, "Batholemew Simpson," and successfully entered the lounge. Jaroszewski then demonstrated at the Defcon conference how he could use this tactic to gain entry to lounges across Europe, exploiting a vulnerability in the lounges' entry systems that failed to cross-check entrants with passenger manifests. This tactic was popularized by a previous hacker, Chris Soghoian, who created a boarding-card generator that could bypass airport security. However, US aviation checkpoints now verify boarding cards by checking for a cryptographic signature from the TSA, making it unlikely that Jaroszewski's hack will work on US airport lounges. Jaroszewski has refused to release his source code, citing concerns about an FBI visit. Despite this, he claims that the hack is easy to recreate. The security of boarding passes has been repeatedly proven to be flawed, with researchers easily crafting their passes to bypass security checks and even no-fly lists. In fact, boarding passes have become increasingly bar-coded and are often checked by machines rather than humans. This makes them vulnerable to hacking, and the checks are essentially a form of security theater. In his talk, Jaroszewski will explain how boarding passes are created, encoded, and validated, and demonstrate how easy it is to craft a fake boarding pass that works at most checkpoints. He will also discuss why some passes don't work at other checkpoints.

The Simon Wiesenthal Center has accused the Lithuanian government of inadvertently fueling the glorification of Holocaust-era war criminals, specifically former pro-Nazi leader Juozas Ambrazevicius-Brazaitis. This claim follows a march in Kaunas, Lithuania's second-largest city, held earlier this month by nationalists who carried portraits of Ambrazevicius-Brazaitis. The marchers also displayed signs reading "Lithuania for Lithuanians." The event was notable, as Ambrazevicius-Brazaitis' government had helped send 30,000 Jews to their deaths. Efraim Zuroff, of the center's Israel office, stated that attendance at the annual march significantly increased after Ambrazevicius-Brazaitis' 2012 reburial in Kaunas, which was funded by the Lithuanian government. Previously, his remains were in the United States. "Last year there were 600 participants, but this year there were 1,000," Zuroff said. "This is a direct result of the government's complicity in his glorification." Zuroff was in Kaunas to protest the event alongside Dovid Katz, an American Jewish academic from Vilnius who is part of the Defending History group focused on Holocaust remembrance. Anti-fascist demonstrators also gathered to protest the march. Ambrazevicius-Brazaitis fled to the United States after the war and passed away there in 1974. He was awarded the highest state honor in Lithuania in 2009 by then-President Valdas Adamkus for his efforts to restore Lithuanian statehood following Soviet occupation. Between 1941 and 1944, up to 95 percent of Lithuania's Jewish community, numbering 200,000, perished at the hands of the Nazis and local collaborators.

SnapPea is a free utility that lets you manage all aspects of your Android phone from your desktop, offering an all-in-one solution that's comparable to iTunes for Android. If you're looking for a utility that can handle everything from apps to SMS messages, SnapPea is definitely worth considering. To use SnapPea, you can either connect your phone to your PC via USB with USB debugging enabled, or pair it with the app over Wi-Fi. Once connected, you can manage and transfer files, install apps, organize contacts, back up your phone, move photos and media, and even send and receive SMS messages from your desktop. SnapPea is clear competitor to AirDroid, which works through a web browser, offering more cross-platform compatibility. However, SnapPea provides some features that AirDroid doesn't, so it ultimately depends on your specific needs. SnapPea is available for Windows users for free, although there is no version for Mac or Linux. For those who need to sideload apps to their Android phone, a Mac version of the APK installer is available.
Stephen Paddock's arsenal of weapons before the Las Vegas massacre brought gun control back to the forefront of the US national debate. The scale of Paddock's weaponry and the widespread gun ownership in America have sparked a renewed discussion about gun laws. Just how common is it for Americans to own multiple guns? And who owns them? To understand the distribution of gun ownership in America, let's look at the facts. The US has the highest number of guns, owning roughly half of the world's guns while making up only 5% of the global population. In relative terms, the US has the highest number of guns per capita, with an estimated 89 to 100 guns for every 100 Americans in 2013, or one firearm per person. Serbia comes second, with 75.6 guns per 100 people. But how many people actually own guns in America? According to numbers from the General Social Survey and Gallup in 2013, only about a quarter of Americans own guns. This means that many gun owners have multiple firearms. So, how many guns does the average American gun owner possess? A 2015 survey by Harvard and Northwestern University found that the average American gun owner owns three guns. While many own just one or two, around 14% of gun owners – 7.7 million people, or 3% of the US population – own eight to 140 guns. These 3% of the population own half of the civilian guns in the US. Stephen Paddock's 47 guns, found in three locations, put him among the 3%. It's worth noting that gun ownership surveys are not always accurate. As long as Americans can still legally purchase guns without a permit in many states, there's no systematic method for tracking the US population of gun owners.

As is typical of Google, however, this isn't just a simple customization tool. Instead, the company's approach leverages machine learning and neural networks to map your facial features to animated versions designed by artist Lamar Abrams. These cartoon avatars can then be further customized. The end result is a diverse range of stickers featuring your newly created digital doppelgänger, which can be used in future Allo chats. According to Google's research blog, the company has taken significant steps to avoid the uncanny valley and ensure that people of all races, genders, and ages are represented across the generated illustrations. It appears that many people in the media, especially those who focus on big cars, are fixated on whether hybrid or plug-in hybrid vehicles will "break even" in terms of fuel savings. A recent example is the misleading claim that plug-in hybrids will pay for themselves in 95 years. This phenomenon has taken on a life of its own, with some people claiming that certain cars will save them money and expecting to be held accountable for making such a financial claim. We're not opposed to scrutinizing these claims, but the obsession with financial savings has become an unrealistic expectation. It's absurd to assume that cars should pay for themselves. Shouldn't people be free to make choices about their vehicles based on personal preferences, such as engine size, features, or performance, without being asked to justify their decisions financially? Meanwhile, those who choose to buy cleaner cars for reasons like lower emissions, electric mode, or to encourage new technology are expected to make a financial case. On the other hand, the money spent on more efficient vehicles can help offset their costs, especially if they're more fuel-efficient. Let's establish a more balanced approach to evaluating green cars, one that recognizes the value of both financial and non-financial considerations.
De Nederlandsche Bank's head of research, Jakob de Haan, recently responded to pointed questions about the role of bitcoin in global finance at a conference. De Haan's candid remarks were notable, given his position at the Dutch central bank. During a speech, he was asked several questions about bitcoin, including some that referred to the euro as a "bullshit fiat currency." A video surfaced showing de Haan responding to a question about whether his institution fears being replaced by decentralized financial technologies like bitcoin. De Haan said he wasn't opposed to the idea of digital currencies being the future, but rather, he was open-minded about it. He acknowledged that the central bank is following the emerging technology closely, with researchers delving into the topic. De Haan clarified that his organization isn't fundamentally opposed to alternatives, but rather, it's essential to note that digital currencies don't come with the same consumer protections as traditional alternatives. He highlighted the importance of this guidance, which was issued in May 2014. De Haan also addressed concerns about the potential benefits of private currencies competing with public alternatives, suggesting that bitcoin's volatility against fiat currencies is a significant issue. In conclusion, de Haan emphasized the importance of price stability, which is the central bank's most critical mandate.
Seeking reliable news? Subscribe to our free newsletters. I've mentioned this before, but it deserves a dedicated post. Contrary to the widespread discussion of Obamacare's enrollment shortfalls, the Congressional Budget Office (CBO) published several projections in 2010. One of these projections predicted a loss of 8 million people from private insurance coverage by 2016. However, this didn't occur. The individual mandate under Obamacare led to a surge in individual coverage purchases, while the employer mandate prompted an increase in employer coverage. By the mid-2015, this resulted in a total of over 16 million people gaining private coverage. The chart on the right illustrates this. After four years of private coverage averaging around 61% of the population, it jumped to 66% within a single year. Was this solely due to the economic recovery, or was there another factor at play? The economy has been steadily recovering since 2012. The only significant change in the fourth quarter of 2013 was the introduction of Obamacare. In conclusion, while Obamacare missed the CBO's target for exchange enrollment by approximately 7 million, it far exceeded their target for private insurance by 24 million. This outcome is a significant success, as employer-sponsored coverage is generally preferred over individual coverage through the exchange, providing better coverage and lower costs for taxpayers.
