Marine Sgt. Andrew Biggio of Winthrop and Marine platoon Sgt. Peter Boisvert of Nashua, N.H., are urging Congress to allow more Afghans to earn visas. Biggio, who worked closely with his interpreter, described him as a hero who "saved lives." Boisvert echoed his sentiments, saying that without his protection, his interpreter is now vulnerable to the Taliban's wrath. With a December 31 deadline looming for interpreters to apply for 1,000 new visas, US Rep. James McGovern (D-Worcester) is pushing to extend the deadline and add more visas. To date, about 13,000 visas have been handed out to Afghan nationals since 2009, but McGovern believes more needs to be done. He argues that the US has a moral obligation to protect these people, who are being targeted by the Taliban. McGovern's amendment to the National Defense Authorization Act for 2015 is now before the US Senate. The move comes as American and NATO troops closed their operational command in Afghanistan. One Afghan interpreter, who has been in Boston since Sunday, was one of the lucky ones. He was helped by both Marines, who enabled him to escape. "Every interpreter's life is in danger," he said. "Enemies believe we betrayed our country by helping and supporting coalition forces. If they catch interpreters, they're going to kill them in a painful way, and they have done it." The interpreter told the Herald that he spent three years securing a visa and sold his car to buy a plane ticket to a new life. Biggio also spoke about his interpreter, saying that he dedicated his life to saving US lives in Afghanistan. He believes extending the deadline and adding more visas is a step in the right direction. "I think we know what the alternative will be," McGovern said. "They will be killed and their families will be killed."

The shares dropped as much as 5% in early trading before recovering somewhat. Weekend reports by Germany's Bild newspaper stated that the country's regulators had uncovered evidence of emissions-cheating software in Fiat cars. Rival Volkswagen has already faced a similar scandal. According to Bild's Sunday edition, the German Federal Motor Vehicle Office (KBA) discovered software in a Fiat car that disabled emissions control technology after just 22 minutes - two minutes after the standard test period ended. The newspaper claimed that the KBA had reported its findings to the European Commission and the Italian authorities. Fiat Chrysler has yet to comment on the allegations, and it has cancelled a meeting with the German authorities that was scheduled to take place last week, citing Italy's responsibility for testing. German Transport Minister Alexander Dobrindt has criticized Fiat's "uncooperative attitude," describing it as "completely incomprehensible".

The Royal College of Nursing (RCN) has called for a specific law to prevent verbal and physical abuse against nurses treating the public. There are 193 reported attacks on UK healthcare workers every day, with a 4% rise in physical assaults against healthcare workers in England from 67,864 in 2014/15 to 70,555 in 2015/16. A survey of RCN members revealed that 56% had experienced physical or verbal abuse from patients, and an additional 63% from patients' relatives or the general public. Following a petition signed by over 116,000 people, a parliamentary debate will take place to discuss making it a specific criminal offence to attack a public healthcare professional. The campaign, led by LBC radio presenter Nick Ferrari, aims to create a greater deterrent for those willing to verbally or physically assault nurses on the job. Each year, the incidents result in an estimated £69m in costs for the health service, including staff absence, loss of productivity, and additional security measures. In response to the petition, the Government stated that assaults against NHS staff are unacceptable and already criminalised, and that a new offence would not provide additional protection. The RCN is also raising concerns about the safety of lone-working nurses in the community, with Kathryn Yates, RCN Professional Lead for Primary and Community Care, expressing her ongoing concern. She noted that little has changed since 2015, when half of all community nurses surveyed felt vulnerable at work, and that the risks had worsened due to NHS cost-cutting, increasing caseloads, and a lack of staff, with some local councils cutting community nursing budgets by 50%.

Romania's Prime Minister Sorin Grindeanu announced on Saturday that the government plans to withdraw the corruption decree that sparked the country's biggest protests since the fall of dictator Nicolae Ceausescu in 1989. In a news conference, Grindeanu stated that the government will hold a meeting on Sunday to repeal the decree, adding that he does not want to divide Romania. The prime minister emphasized that the decree's repeal will ensure it does not take effect. An estimated 120,000 people took to the streets on Saturday for the fifth consecutive day to protest the decree, which decriminalized official misconduct. Demonstrators marched through Bucharest to the parliament building, forming a human chain. On Friday, between 200,000 and 250,000 people demonstrated across the country. The decision was not unexpected, as the leader of Grindeanu's Social Democrats said earlier on Saturday that the government might withdraw the decree. Grindeanu stressed that the decree will not be implemented. Calin Tariceanu, the head of the ALDE party, a junior partner of the ruling coalition, stated that the move came because the government had nothing to hide. The Constitutional Court is expected to rule on the decree's legality next week. The decree allowed for the decriminalization of abuse-of-power offenses for sums below 200,000 lei ($47,500; 44,200 euros) and narrowed the definition of conflict of interest, making it legal for a public official to favor a business partner when deciding who should win a contract. Grindeanu's left-wing government, in office for a month, also aims to free around 2,500 people from prison who are serving sentences of less than five years. The prime minister stated that the measures are necessary to bring penal law into line with Romania's constitution and reduce overcrowding in prisons. However, critics argue that the real goal of the decree was to release thousands of politicians and officials caught in anti-corruption drives, many of whom are members of the PSD. The PSD returned to power after winning elections in December, promising to raise salaries and pensions in the European Union's second-poorest country.

How to profit from misprinted money By James Allum, August 26th, 2016, Editorial In the world of numismatics, a thriving market for misprinted banknotes has emerged, showcasing the complexity and potential for error in the minting process. This variety of mistakes can significantly impact the value of a note, with the more errors present, the rarer and more valuable it becomes. Let's examine some notable examples of misprinted USD bills and calculate their increased value. $1 bill with an inverted obverse This $1 bill features the back printed upside down and misaligned, resulting in nearly 25% of the iconic imagery being distorted. The value of this note has increased by 105,200%. $1 bill with a foldover error The top right corner of this bill was folded before being cut, causing the right-hand serial number, treasury seal, and two of the four District numbers to appear on the bent-over corner. The value of this note has risen by 75950%. $20 bill with a star shift error A subtle error can be seen in the star to the left of 7th US President Andrew Jackson's portrait, which appears to be moving towards his head. The value of this note has increased by 3115%. $1 bill with multiple gutter folds The creases and crimps on this single $1 bill resemble random lines, possibly due to George Washington's alleged interest in Pink Floyd's music. The value of this note has risen by 17450%. $10 bill with a missing face printing error A mistake in the minting process resulted in a faceless $10 bill. However, the serial numbers, district star, and federal seal are still intact. The value of this note has increased by 3050%. $20 bill with a missing back printing error This $20 bill lacks the back printing, leading to a significant decrease in value. The value of this note has dropped by 2678.75%. $10 bill with an ink smear error A disgruntled employee's frustration has resulted in an unfortunate smudge on the $10 bill, resembling a squid's ink. The value of this note has increased by 4287.5%. Images and prices provided courtesy of www.stacksbowers.com

First Minister Nicola Sturgeon has stated that China can do more to promote gender equality and women's rights during her recent visit to Beijing and Hong Kong. Speaking to a gathering of senior women in government, academia, and business, Ms Sturgeon highlighted the potential benefits of gender equality in transforming the global economy. She referenced Hillary Clinton's famous "women's rights are human rights" speech, which commemorates the 20th anniversary of her address at the United Nations' fourth world conference on women in Beijing this September. The visit aims to strengthen Scotland's ties with China, following the Chinese government's crackdown on human rights activists and women's groups in recent months. Amnesty International has accused China of suppressing human rights activists and women's groups. The organization's Scotland programme director, Naomi McAuliffe, welcomed the First Minister's statement of support for equality and human rights. Ms Sturgeon emphasized that gender equality is a fundamental human rights issue and a great economic opportunity of the 21st century, noting that there are over two billion working-age women worldwide who face unequal economic opportunities. Ms Sturgeon stated that empowering women is a key way for countries to increase their productivity and that gender equality can transform the global economy. She also acknowledged that China has made progress in promoting gender equality, but noted that there is still more work to be done. Women account for 45% of China's workforce, and the government recognizes the importance of women's involvement in decision-making. However, women remain under-represented in key decision-making roles in business and government. Ms Sturgeon concluded that no country has achieved complete gender equality, and that learning from each other is essential for progress. She expressed her hope that her visit would continue a dialogue on equality and human rights throughout her time in China.

The global company announced that it will establish its first U.S. factory in Wisconsin, promising 400 new jobs. According to Wisconsin Governor Scott Walker, the Kenosha County plant is set to open in 2020 and marks one of the largest foreign investments in the state's history. Walker expressed his gratitude to Haribo for choosing Wisconsin, praising the state's business-friendly environment and fiscal management. Construction of the plant is expected to begin next year, with a capital investment of $242 million in the first phase. The facility, spanning 500,000 square feet, will be the third-largest confectionery plant in the U.S. Haribo, famous for its Gold-Bears and fruit-flavored gummy bears, is also known for producing gummy coca cola bottles, licorice, and other treats. The family-run company currently employs nearly 7,000 people globally, with 16 manufacturing facilities in 10 countries. Kenosha County Executive Jim Kreuser hailed the project as a significant boost for the county, calling it a "sweet day" for Kenosha County.

Volkswagen's woes continue to worsen as the company reveals it has discovered "irregularities" in carbon dioxide emissions levels of approximately 800,000 cars in Europe. This figure is likely just the tip of the iceberg, with the total number of affected vehicles potentially much higher worldwide. The original VW scandal revolved around nitrous oxide emissions, a toxic pollutant produced by diesel engines. While there are methods to reduce NOx emissions, such as catalytic reduction with urea, VW has shown that cheating on emissions testing can be more lucrative in the short term, albeit a more precarious option in the long run. Now, VW is reporting that around 800,000 cars, mainly equipped with 1.4-litre diesel engines, may also have issues with their carbon dioxide emissions. The two primary models affected are the VW Polo and Golf, but some Audi, Seat, and Skoda cars are also included. VW notes that some 1.4-litre petrol engines may also be affected, but provided no further details. It appears that while investigating the "defeat device" that artificially reduced NOx emissions, VW stumbled upon similar inconsistencies in CO2 testing. The company has yet to release many details, but it seems that the cars were producing significantly less CO2 during certification testing than in real-world use. "Unexplained inconsistencies were found when determining Type Approval CO2 levels," a statement from VW said. "Based on present knowledge, around 800,000 vehicles from the Volkswagen Group are affected." While cheating on NOx emissions was one thing, these CO2 irregularities could be far more damaging for VW. CO2 emissions are closely tied to fuel consumption, so if VW was falsifying mileage and lower emissions, it could face massive regulatory fines. The company has already set aside €2 billion for the 800,000 cars that may have CO2 issues, approximately four times as much per vehicle as what was set aside for the NOx defeat device issue. Volkswagen has so far written off €6.5 billion for the 11 million vehicles affected by the NOx emissions issue. If the CO2 issue involves more than 800,000 cars, VW could be looking at a further write-off of tens of billions of euros. Consequently, VW's shares have plummeted about 10 percent since the company announced the CO2 irregularities late last night.
To identify a succubus or incubus, start by recognizing that they often appear as a person. The first step is to sever any connection with this entity. Disappear from their life, delete their photos and contact information, and cut ties with anything that keeps you linked to them. Next, use protective spells or blessings to sever your connection on the astral plane. However, some succubi and incubi have a personal preference for their victims, and they won't easily let go once they've chosen you. Since we exchange energy with everyone we interact with, they may target your close friends and family to drain their energy, which shares your energy signature, or to maintain a connection to you. They do this by constantly talking about you, especially with mothers, who are naturally inclined to compliment and praise. This flattery stirs emotions and begins the feeding process. The person you like must then spend more time with you to 'refuel,' and eventually, they become the go-between, a type of energy vampire or succubus themselves. For some, this middle ground can lead to them becoming a succubus or energy vampire. Breaking free from a succubus or incubus is extremely challenging, as they usually don't want to leave an unfinished meal. The best approach is to make them associate you with negative experiences, which requires the assistance of a highly skilled spiritual sage, such as a Native American shaman, high Wiccan priestess, or voodoo doctor. Destroying a succubus or incubus is nearly impossible and can leave a person feeling depressed, empty, and unfulfilled, as that's all they've become. It's rare to restore someone to their original state before the succubus took over. Only if you intervene when the person is being drained can you hope to save them; after they've been drained, it's too late.
You can now book a wine-tasting trip to Washington state, but if you wait a few decades, the Pacific Northwest could gain around 80,000 square miles of suitable vineyard space due to rising temperatures. However, this expansion comes with a sad reality - much of this new land is currently inhabited by grizzly bears and wolves. In a superheated future, California's grape-growing regions will face challenges, with Lee Hannah, a biologist at Conservation International, estimating that 60 percent of the state's regions will find it harder to grow varietals. This assessment, which combined three methods, also predicts that areas like Mediterranean Europe, South Africa, and Australia will struggle to grow wine grapes, while higher latitudes and elevations, including parts of New Zealand and northern Europe, will experience an advantage. While vineyards will adapt to the changing climate, techniques like shadier trellising and planting heat-tolerant varietals may help. In the long run, growing good grapes will become easier in certain regions, such as those with higher latitudes and elevations.

Chanel Dubofsky, a 37-year-old writer, believes she's had a lifelong pattern of overachieving. This started when her mother was diagnosed with cancer when Dubofsky was just 7 years old. When her mother relapsed twice, at ages 13 and 17, Dubofsky's stress levels escalated. She recalls struggling with debilitating headaches in middle school, which she would only manage to shake off after completing her homework. Her high school friends even fueled this habit, competing to see who could get by on the least sleep. Dubofsky says her workaholism became even more intense during her college semester when her mother passed away. Even after her mother's death, Dubofsky continued to take on an incredibly heavy course load of 20 credits, staying up all night to finish her assignments and sleeping in until noon. She managed to earn As in her classes, even the ones that were notoriously early, often missing because she was too exhausted, and only later did she reveal her mother's passing to her professors. Dubofsky later realized that she wasn't meeting the expected standards of grief, and that she was struggling to cope with her emotions. Mental health research suggests that grief can manifest in many different ways, including anger, guilt, depression, or a lack of productivity at work. However, some people may respond to grief in unhealthy ways, such as making significant life changes or trying to avoid their feelings altogether. On the other hand, using work as a way to calm down can be beneficial in moderation, especially if it gives you a sense of purpose. However, people with trauma or PTSD may be more prone to using work as a way to avoid their emotions, and may become so absorbed in their work that they overlook physical pain or other discomforts. A 29-year-old nonprofit worker in New York, who asked to remain anonymous, identifies as a trauma survivor and workaholic. For her, working all the time and being constantly busy is a way to avoid thinking about her feelings. Researchers Rebekah Reysen and Bryan Robinson agree that overworking can be a way to escape. People may try to run away from their problems by focusing on something else, says Reysen. Nancy, a journalist and documentary filmmaker based in New York City, saw her overwork tendencies most clearly when working with an abusive partner on a project. She would channel her anger and frustration into the project itself, as a way of coping with the emotional pain she wasn't addressing.
Texas Motor Speedway track president Eddie Gossage is once again being territorial about the rest of Texas, specifically Austin's Circuit of the Americas, after COTA expressed interest in hosting a NASCAR series. It seems Gossage has a map, as Fort Worth and Austin would typically be in different states in any other state. COTA chairman Bobby Epstein recently expressed interest in hosting NASCAR, which is a road course, unlike the oval at TMS. However, NASCAR dates in Texas fall under a regional protection clause in TMS's five-year contract with the series. Gossage reminded everyone that TMS was the first to call dibs on NASCAR in the Fort Worth Star-Telegram: while COTA wants NASCAR races, they're 20 years too late. Gossage's comments were seen as condescending and asinine, given that TMS and COTA are 221 miles apart, most of which is on the congested I-35. This spat over Texas race dates is like Homestead-Miami complaining about Daytona being 287 miles away or Martinsville objecting to Bristol's races. If NASCAR only races at TMS twice a year, allowing for road course dates that would be a welcome addition for fans, it's not a concern for TMS, which has a long history with the series and is one of its biggest clients.
Three years after Japan was ravaged by a devastating earthquake, tsunami, and nuclear disaster, over 2,600 people remain unaccounted for, according to the latest police figures. The search for the missing continues across the northeast coastline, where most of the fatalities occurred when the tsunami swept through in March 2011. As Japan marks the third anniversary of the disaster, the nation's worst peacetime loss of life, claiming 15,884 lives, the missing remain a haunting mystery. In the worst-hit prefectures of Miyagi, Iwate, and Fukushima, 2,636 people are officially missing, with the remains of 98 others unidentified. Despite the appearance of normalcy in many disaster-stricken communities, life remains challenging for those left behind, particularly bereaved relatives determined to find their loved ones. Monthly searches are conducted on the 11th of each month, with police officers, Maritime Safety Agency personnel, and local volunteers participating, at the request of relatives. For some, the quest is not just about closure but also about finding a way to lay their loved ones to rest. One such individual is Yasuo Takamatsu, a 57-year-old from Onagawa, Miyagi, whose wife Yuko disappeared in the tsunami. Her disappearance was one of the many tragedies that struck the town, which was hit hard by the disaster, with over 800 people killed by the 65-foot tsunami. Takamatsu, a bus driver, recently took a drastic step by learning how to scuba dive to search the Pacific Ocean seabed for signs of his wife. The last text message he received from her was a simple "I want to go home," while another unsent message on her phone read "Tsunami huge." The emotional weight of not knowing her fate weighs heavily on Takamatsu, who feels compelled to bring her home as soon as possible.
Drivers have witnessed a significant decline in gas prices, with analysts predicting that prices could drop below $3 by the fall. Over the past month, gas prices have plummeted by 6 percent, and since hitting their peak in April, they have decreased by $0.49. Oil speculators, including Tom Kloza, expect this trend to continue, with Kloza stating that gas prices are likely to fall to $3 by Halloween and certainly by Thanksgiving. This drop in prices coincides with the peak driving season of summer, when Americans hit the road in large numbers. According to AAA's latest projections, nearly 42.3 million Americans plan to travel over 50 miles, representing a 5 percent increase in travel from last year, with 35 million opting for road trips. The current national average gas price stands at $3.45 per gallon, and crude oil prices have fallen to $78 a barrel, the lowest level since late winter, according to market reports from West Texas Intermediate.

Marvel Comics has announced that it will be bringing an end to the Luke Cage series with the release of issue #170 in February. Writer David F. Walker confirmed the news on social media, stating that the comic book series, not to be confused with the popular TV show, has been cancelled. Walker expressed his gratitude to fans who supported the series, while also acknowledging that the comic industry faces significant challenges. According to Walker, Luke Cage was cancelled due to poor sales, which is a common issue affecting many comic book series. He noted that the success of superheroes in other media does not necessarily translate to comic book sales, highlighting the reality that few people purchase comics. In fact, Walker pointed out that even the top-selling comics only manage to sell around 100,000 units, a far cry from the massive success of other media. This has led to the cancellation of several other Marvel series, including Generation X, Hawkeye, Iceman, Jean Grey, and The Unbelievable Gwenpool. However, not all affected series have been confirmed as cancelled, with some writers, such as Matthew Rosenberg and Margeret Stohl, assuring fans that their books are still in production.
Fewer than half of Scots feel well-informed about the key issues surrounding independence and the upcoming referendum, according to a recent survey by pro-independence group Yes Scotland. The survey, which polled over 1,000 people in April, found that 46% of respondents believe they have enough knowledge ahead of the 2014 vote. Of these, 47% said they plan to vote yes, while 1% planned to vote no. Yes Scotland's chief executive, Blair Jenkins, noted that the survey is part of a broader research program and found that 74% of people are interested in the independence debate, while 10% are not. The results also showed that 75% of those who are well-informed are very likely to vote, with an additional 13% who are somewhat likely to vote. Jenkins stated that the surveys indicate that the more people learn about independence, the more they are drawn to it. He also emphasized that with over a year to go before the referendum, Yes Scotland is currently running neck-and-neck with the No campaign among those who already feel well-informed.

AMD has shifted its focus away from pirate-themed branding, changing the codename for its next-generation high-end Radeon GPU family from "Pirate Islands" to "Caribbean Islands" at the last minute. According to rumors, the new cards are expected to arrive by summer. These changes come from Fudzilla, an online publication that claims its sources are always accurate. However, release dates are notoriously difficult to predict, so the "June to August" window should be viewed with skepticism. AMD might be under pressure from rival Nvidia to accelerate production of the Caribbean Islands GPUs if Nvidia successfully launches its GeForce GTX 990 in early 2015. This could widen the performance gap between Nvidia and AMD's top graphical solutions, forcing AMD to play catch-up. The development process for Radeon R9 290 and 295X2 follow-ups is uncertain, with AMD likely to shrink the nanometer count from 28 to 20, but a direct jump to 16 FinFET die is also possible. If AMD needs to expedite R&D, it may need to cut corners. Typically, AMD puts on a show when introducing new graphics processing units, and this time is no exception. The company has a history of using Caribbean Islands names for its Radeon products, including the HD 6970 and HD 6990, so there are still plenty of options available.
Almost a year after unveiling its self-driving bubble car prototype, Google is set to put the high-tech vehicle on California's public roads. The company has been testing the prototype in private settings, and now plans to deploy a few vehicles in Mountain View this summer. Those concerned about potential safety issues will find reassurance in the fact that a human will always be inside the car, and the vehicle will have a removable steering wheel, accelerator pedal, and brake pedal for manual control. The cars will also be limited to a speed of 25mph. Google's experience with its self-driving Lexus RX450h SUV fleet, which has been roaming California's streets since last September, has logged nearly a million autonomous miles and averages around 10,000 miles per week. The company claims that its total logged autonomous miles are equivalent to "75 years of typical American adult driving experience." The prototype bubble cars will build on the software and expertise from the Lexus fleet. Google views this public testing as a necessary step towards autonomous driving and a crucial moment for public perception and acceptance. With concerns about the safety of self-driving cars persisting, public tests allowing the public to interact with the vehicles may help alleviate these fears. Following reports of four self-driving cars in California involved in accidents, Google responded by highlighting its own safety record. In a recent Medium post, Chris Urmson stated that, over six years, the company had been involved in 11 minor accidents during 1.7 million miles of autonomous and manual driving, with none of these accidents caused by the self-driving car.

The divorce rate in Israel decreased by 2% in 2014, according to a report released by the Chief Rabbinate of Israel. The report showed that there were 11,249 divorces in 2013 and 11,023 in 2014, a drop attributed to the cases handled by the batei din around the country. Despite this decrease, the number of divorces increased in Jerusalem and Tel Aviv, which have consistently ranked first and second in recent years. Jerusalem led the way with 874 divorces in 2014, followed by 733 in 2013, while Tel Aviv came in second with 779 divorces in 2014 and 678 in 2013. Haifa and Petach Tikvah ranked third and fourth, respectively, with 629 and 439 divorces in 2014. Azor had the lowest number with 29 divorces. In 2014, a total of 89,551 divorce cases were opened, up from 90,237 the previous year. The majority of these cases - 7,541 - involved a couple seeking divorce. Of these, 969 were classified as "shalom bayis," but only 2.6% were approved. The Rabbanut system also handled 3,437 cases of alimony, 3,341 inheritance matters, and 2,501 child custody cases. The unit responsible for freeing agunos reported a significant increase in securing gets for 184 agunos in 2014, up from 159 in 2013. The batei din imposed sanctions on 40 recalcitrant husbands, with 191 decisions to impose sanctions, and 184 cases involving husbands who fled the country. These sanctions can include a lien on bank accounts and assets, preventing the husband from leaving the country, and restricting employment and driving privileges. The batei din issued 16 arrest warrants in 2014, down from 19 the previous year, and protective orders in 152 cases to protect wives from domestic violence. The Chief Rabbinate reported a significant decrease in the time it took to address cases, with an average of 56 days compared to 96 in 2013. There was also a reported drop in civil judicial system interference with the batei din. In 2014, only 4 out of 45 petitions filed with the Supreme Court were accepted. The batei din administration pointed out a critical shortage of dayanim, with 20% fewer than required
In a bid to slash energy bills and lower emissions, a small German village has turned its mobile phones into remote controls for streetlights. The innovative scheme, known as Dial4Light, allows residents to switch on and off the village lamps as needed, simply by dialing a code displayed on a lamp post. The system, which has been trialled on several streets for a year, has been deemed a success and is now set to be rolled out across the entire village, as well as in neighboring towns. With over 9,000 residents eligible to register, the free service enables users to control the lights for up to 15 minutes, after which they automatically switch off. Mayor Friedrich Ehlert attributes the initiative to both cost savings and environmental awareness, stating, "We're doing this to save money, but also to raise our community's awareness of energy usage and protect the environment." The idea was born out of a village council's decision to turn off the lights at night due to financial constraints. A local resident, frustrated by the lack of control, suggested the Dial4Light system, which was taken seriously by the county council. With the help of experts, the system was developed, featuring a special modem and software that allows registered users to control the lights. Data collected by the council shows a significant reduction in carbon dioxide emissions, equivalent to the emissions of 11 four-person households. The pilot project generated international interest, with inquiries from European countries, Saudi Arabia, Japan, India, and the US. The Dial4Light system is set to be launched internationally later this month.

Catch.com, a popular note-taking service, is shutting down on August 30th. The company informed its users today that they can export their notes before the service goes offline. The Android app, Catch Notes, is likely to stop working on the same day. Catch had millions of users and its Android app had between 5 and 10 million downloads on the Play Store. The company hasn't provided much explanation for its decision, stating only that it's moving in a "different direction". The note-taking market is becoming increasingly crowded with Google's Keep and Evernote still being widely used. It's possible that Catch felt it couldn't compete with the likes of Google and Evernote, who have already established themselves in the space.
In an interview set to air on ARD public television on Sunday, German President Joachim Gauck expressed discomfort with the prospect of Left party candidate Bodo Ramelow becoming the premier of Thuringia. Gauck questioned whether the party, which emerged from the East German communist party, had fully shed its past of repressing people. "Can we really trust this party after everything it once did?" he asked during the interview. Gauck acknowledged that he had problems trusting parts of the party, but stressed that he respected the results of the recent Thuringia state election. As a Lutheran pastor and human rights activist in former East Germany, Gauck had a unique perspective on the country's past. He headed the government agency that oversaw the files of East Germany's Stasi secret police after reunification in 1990. Gauck's comments sparked criticism, with Left co-chairperson Katja Kipping telling the Bild am Sonntag newspaper that his doubts about the party's democratic attitude were "not right for a president." However, some people understood Gauck's concerns, noting that many shared his experiences of injustice in East Germany. Left co-chairperson Cem Özdemir said the president was simply expressing a sentiment shared by many who lived through that period. The party's other co-chair, Simone Peter, advised Gauck to stay out of party politics. Ramelow, who is set to become the Left's first state premier, declined to comment. The Left party, which finished second in the Thuringia state election, is in talks with the Social Democrats and Greens to form a coalition government.
The US military has admitted to using depleted uranium ammunition in Syria, sparking controversy over its potential health risks. In 2015, thousands of rounds containing this toxic material were fired during strikes against Daesh positions, according to reports. The use of depleted uranium is widely regarded as a war crime, as it can cause serious health problems, including genetic damage, birth defects, cancer, and immune system damage. According to US Central Command, depleted uranium was used in combination with high-explosive rounds to target Daesh's oil transportation fleet. Maj. Josh Jacques confirmed the use of depleted uranium, stating that around 5,265 armor-piercing rounds were fired, destroying approximately 250 vehicles. The US had previously vowed not to use depleted uranium in Iraq and Syria, but it appears that this commitment was not upheld. This raises questions about the military's adherence to its own policies, particularly regarding the use of depleted uranium. In 2015, John Moore, the US-led coalition's spokesman, stated that depleted uranium munitions would not be used in Iraq or Syria. However, the Pentagon later claimed that the substance was not used because Daesh did not possess the tanks it was designed to penetrate. Historically, the US military has faced criticism for its use of depleted uranium in Iraq during the 2003 invasion. The Iraqi government expressed deep concern over the material's harmful effects, and a UN report released in 2014 described it as a danger to human beings and the environment. Iraqi doctors and international health scientists have linked the use of DU to outbreaks of previously unknown diseases, including kidney and liver problems, as well as a sharp rise in leukemia and anemia cases, particularly among children.

President Trump has a history of admiring strongmen leaders, including Vladimir Putin, Saddam Hussein, Bashar al-Assad, and China's authoritarian government. As a private citizen, Trump often praised these leaders for their firm handling of dissent, saying they showed the power of strength. Now as the leader of the free world, Trump's enthusiasm for authoritarian leaders remains unchanged. In recent weeks, he has congratulated Turkish President Recep Tayyip Erdoğan for effectively ending his nation's democracy, expressed approval of Rodrigo Duterte's program of extrajudicial killings, and reiterated his admiration for Kim Jong-un's success in securing the title of Supreme Leader at a young age. Trump also stated that he would be "honored" to meet with Kim Jong-un if circumstances allowed it. Trump acknowledged that most politicians would never say they would meet with the North Korean dictator, but he said he would do so under the right circumstances. Given Trump's affinity for the "strength" of human rights abusers, his eagerness to meet with Kim Jong-un may seem shocking. However, on the level policy, Trump's stance toward Pyongyang is arguably more hostile than that of Barack Obama during his presidential campaign. Obama suggested negotiating "without preconditions" with America's enemies, including North Korea and Iran. Trump's stance is more confrontational, with the US preconditions for direct engagement being a pause in Pyongyang's nuclear program and a declaration that the regime is open to negotiating over denuclearization. A critical examination of the BioShock videogame franchise offers a unique lens through which to explore philosophical, ideological, and computational perspectives on systems, decisions, and propaganda. The New Materialism's use of digital cultural artifacts, such as video games, as a catalyst for philosophical inquiry is exemplified by Robert Jackson's book Bioshock: Decision, Forced Choice, and Propaganda. In this concise volume, Jackson utilizes the BioShock franchise as a tool for pondering questions of choice, causality, and decision-making both within and outside the game itself. Unlike many studies of videogames' aesthetic and cultural achievements, Jackson provides a rigorous critique of the ideology of decision in contemporary society, arguing that it is a capacity inherent to systems and collectives, rather than individual agency. Ian Bogost, Ivan Allen College Distinguished Chair in Media Studies and Professor of Interactive Computing, commends Jackson's work for avoiding the superficiality often associated with videogame analysis, instead offering a nuanced exploration of the complex, algorithmic, and systemic nature of decision-making. Jackson's book is not merely a study of the BioShock franchise, but rather an examination of the world of forces and forced choice, decision-making over choice, and the intricate, nested structures that define our contemporary political landscape. By applying the BioShock franchise to this broader context, Jackson illustrates how any inquiry into the so-called self is inherently predicated upon decisions made outside of it, and how these decisions are automated, algorithmic, and systemic, forming a constant cascade of choices. Jackson's book, which provocatively explores this moment through the lens of BioShock, is a testament to his intellectual acuity.

The Puya raimondii, also known as the queen of the Andes, is a massive species of bromeliad that can reach up to 15 meters in height. Native to the high Andes in Bolivia and Peru, it is the largest species of its kind. The first scientific description of the species was made by French scientist Alcide d'Orbigny in 1830 after he encountered it in the region of Vacas, Bolivia, at an altitude of 3960 meters. However, the plants he saw were immature and not yet flowering, so he couldn't classify them taxonomically. The species name raimondii commemorates the 19th-century Italian scientist Antonio Raimondi, who discovered the plant in Peru and described it as new to science under the name Pourretia gigantea in his 1874 book El Perú. The queen of the Andes has a distinctive trunk that can grow up to 5 meters tall, with a rosette of leaves that can reach 1.25 meters long, and leaf spines that can be as short as 1 centimeter. The inflorescence can grow between 4-8 meters tall, and the entire plant can reach up to 15 meters tall. A single plant can produce between 8,000 and 20,000 flowers in just three months. The reproductive cycle of the plant can last up to 80 years, although one individual planted at the University of California Botanical Garden bloomed after only 28 years. The Puya raimondii is native to the Andes of Bolivia and Peru, where it grows on shrubby and rocky slopes between 3000-4800 meters of elevation. This species is highly specialized to its site conditions and tends to grow in small areas, even if the surrounding terrain seems equally suitable. It has also been found to thrive at near sea level in a temperate climate. Despite its impressive size and adaptability, the Puya raimondii is considered an endangered species by the IUCN. The main threats to its survival are human-caused fires, climate change, and a declining genetic diversity.

This process allowed for some amazing details that were lost during the original theatrical projection. The bonus feature on the restoration process and conversion to 4K explains this in more depth. Interestingly, the original footage was shot in 6K, making the transition to 4K a breeze. Watching the film on Blu-ray brings back memories of seeing it in theaters. While I have some reservations about certain creative decisions made by Hasbro and the writers, the opening 30 minutes and the ending are truly standout. If I'm being honest, I think Optimus Prime should have been saved and simply repaired to become Ultra Magnus. Hot Rod becoming Rodimus was an interesting twist, but the resulting character design didn't quite live up to my expectations - it looked more like a futuristic RV than I would have liked. Overall, while it could have been improved, the Blu-ray version is a great way to experience the film, and it includes a digital copy that works with iTunes, PC, and Mac.

The event takes place in Rosebud Park and promises an immersive experience with dozens of historical reenactors, sword fights, and live combat. Visitors can explore the reenactment village, featuring the Sons of Fenrir Vikings, Dragons Own Medieval Combat Group, and other groups, and witness battles throughout the day. The festival also includes traditional music, sing-a-longs, and storytelling. Artisan Alley offers a chance to shop for heritage arts and traditional crafts that complement the festival's character. Verizon has been vocal about its push for Voice over LTE (VoLTE) as a cutting-edge alternative to traditional circuit-switched voice. The company started testing VoLTE back in 2011 using the LG Revolution, with a planned 2012 launch. Now, an analyst with Northland Capital Markets claims that Verizon has already activated two trial VoLTE markets and plans a national rollout for 2013. However, this doesn't rule out a potential commercial launch this year, as Verizon may be rolling out the service in smaller markets first. Interestingly, the timing of the national rollout aligns with Verizon's plans to upgrade its entire 3G network to LTE in 2013, allowing the carrier to eventually phase out 2G and 3G networks by refarming spectrum. The transition is crucial because LTE is an IP-based technology, meaning VoLTE is the only way to deploy legacy calling services without falling back to older technologies like CDMA or GSM, ultimately paving the way for the elimination of 2G and 3G networks.

Blind gamers often feel invisible, and visually impaired players aren't far behind. For example, Brandon Cole, a gamer, wrote to THQ about making its Smackdown series of WWE wrestling games more accessible to the blind. He received a generic response thanking him for his appreciation of the game's graphics. This lack of understanding doesn't help visually impaired gamers, as some characters in EverQuest II are actually invisible. However, one visually impaired gamer, Alexander Stern, has taken a different approach. He's not just requesting accessibility features, but is demanding them through a lawsuit against Sony, Sony Online Entertainment, and Sony Computer Entertainment America in the US District Court for the Central District of California. Stern alleges that Sony is violating the Americans with Disabilities Act by failing to implement features that make its games accessible to visually impaired gamers. According to the Act, no individual should be discriminated against on the basis of disability in accessing goods, services, or accommodations. Stern claims that Sony ignored repeated requests through postal mail and e-mail to make its games more accessible. The lawsuit specifically requests the addition of visual cues to help gamers with impaired visual processing find their destinations. The lawsuit also cites examples of other companies making their games more accessible, such as Blizzard Entertainment's World of Warcraft, which allows third-party mods, and Pin Interactive's Terraformers, which includes high-contrast graphics, an audio compass, and voice-over detailing items. The suit also claims that Sony's actions have caused visually impaired gamers a financial loss, as they're unable to participate in the official auction site where gamers can sell their in-game items for real money. The lawsuit states that Sony has constructed its products in an inaccessible way, maintained them in this state, and failed to correct the barriers despite repeated notifications. Representatives for Sony and Sony Online Entertainment declined to comment on the pending litigation.
Microsoft's decision to switch to new AMD chips for its next Xbox console suggests that the upcoming system won't support Xbox 360 games, according to a recent report. The unannounced console, reportedly simply called "Xbox", will utilize a variant of AMD Jaguar technology. This switch to x86 PC-style architecture aims to make game development more cost-effective and accessible for developers familiar with the standard. However, this also means that backwards compatibility with current-gen games is unlikely, as Bloomberg reported. It's best to keep your old 360 handy. Sony faced a similar issue with the PlayStation 4 but has already found a solution by streaming older games using its Gaikai technology. Analyst Paul Thurrot has previously mentioned that Microsoft plans to launch a budget-friendly $99 (£65) Xbox 360 model alongside the new console, which points to the new console not supporting current-gen games. Given the shift to new hardware, can Microsoft still somehow achieve backwards compatibility in some form? "Entirely different hardware configurations don't make backwards-compatibility easy," said Rich Leadbetter, Digital Foundry's expert. "That said, the transition from Xbox to Xbox 360 shows that Microsoft can make efforts to ensure older games work well on the new console, even improving some titles with added anti-aliasing. "Given enough time and resources, a similar form of backwards compatibility could be implemented on Durango, but the time and effort required to provide flawless compatibility with a handful of titles, let alone a whole library, makes this rather unlikely." Microsoft is set to announce the new Xbox on May 21st, according to multiple sources, with the machine reportedly launching in November and costing $500, or $300 with some form of subscription.

Slovenia held a referendum today on homosexual marriage and related issues, with voters deciding whether to overturn a law that aligns the country with more progressive EU member states. The referendum was seen as unlikely to succeed, due to strict conditions imposed on it. However, in a surprise turn of events, Slovenians turned out in large numbers, voting down the law to meet the required threshold. Our Slovenian correspondent Andrej Turjaški reports: It's been a remarkable day. At today's referendum on December 20, 2015, the Slovenian people overwhelmingly rejected the law proposed by the parliament majority, which would have granted marriage equality to homosexuals, allowed them to adopt children, and introduced gender theory in schools. The turnout at polling stations was just over 36%, with 63% of voters casting their ballots against the law. This exceeded the 20% threshold needed for the law to be rejected, a feat made all the more remarkable given that the usual turnout for referendums is around 10%.

Augusto Pinochet in Chile - The US-backed coup that ousted socialist leader Salvador Allende in 1973 led to 17 years of state-sanctioned torture and brutal repression under Pinochet's dictatorship. This decision was a masterclass in how to turn a policy failure into a success. 2. The Right-Wing Junta in El Salvador - Funding right-wing paramilitary groups led to widespread violence and human rights abuses, resulting in thousands of deaths and widespread terrorization. The US's involvement in this policy was a textbook example of how to create a massive failure. 3. The US-backed government in Iraq - The war in Iraq was a disaster, and its aftermath has been marked by the rise of extremist militants. Despite a $14 billion US investment, the country's new government has ceded power and strategic interests to these groups, making the US's decision to invade a colossal failure. 4. Manuel Noriega in Panama - The US's decision to support Noriega, who initially worked as a CIA operative, ultimately backfired. His regime was marked by extrajudicial killings and large-scale drug trafficking, leading to a complete collapse of the US's influence in the country. 5. The Mujahideen in Afghanistan - The US's decision to aid Afghan insurgents against the Soviet Union led to a decade of war that left over a million people dead and 6 million displaced. The chaos created by this policy ultimately allowed the Taliban to rise to power, making this one of the US's biggest policy failures.

He challenged legacy media organizations to poll the public on the issue.

For nearly eight centuries, scientists have blamed black rats for spreading the bubonic plague, but new evidence suggests they may have been unfairly maligned. In fact, gerbils could be the real culprits behind the devastating epidemic. It's often the cute ones that turn out to be the troublemakers. Researchers have found compelling proof that gerbils are the actual cause of the plague, contradicting the long-held notion that European outbreaks were triggered by fleas on rats. According to a study published in the Proceedings of the National Academy of Sciences, climate data from the 14th century reveals that the plague was not linked to the weather in Europe. Instead, it was Asia's climate that seemed to trigger the outbreaks. Analysis of 15 tree-ring records showed that Europe experienced plague outbreaks after central Asia had a wet spring followed by a warm summer – conditions that were ideal for gerbils but disastrous for black rats. The gerbils and their fleas then hitched a ride to Europe via the Silk Road, arriving a few years later to wreak havoc. This means that Europe's black rats were not responsible for the deaths of over 100 million people in the "second plague pandemic." The findings also explain why the disease recurred intermittently over the centuries, rather than lingering on the continent as long as rats were present to carry it. This is not the first time scientists have challenged a popular understanding of the plague. Last year, researchers found evidence that the disease was airborne, rather than spread through flea bites. Now, Nils Christian Stenseth, an author of the study, plans to fact-check their findings by analyzing DNA from ancient European skeletons. If the samples show significant genetic variation across time, it would indicate that successive outbreaks were caused by newly arrived waves of the disease, rather than a resurgence from the continent's rat reservoir. The implications of this research are significant, and Stenseth says that if the findings are confirmed, they would have to rewrite the history books. And for many students, this means that their pet gerbil might not be as harmless as they thought.

Where does your city rank for the best place to sleep? Contrary to popular opinion, Canada's most sleepless city isn't in the major cities. Find Your Forecast. A new study by Sleep-Centre.ca and IPG Mediabrands reveals that 62 per cent of Canadians struggle with sleep. The top reasons for staying awake at night are pain, followed by room temperature, with 31 per cent of respondents citing it as a problem. This finding is surprising, as room temperature is something we can easily control. Loraine Cordery, insights and marketing manager for the study, notes, "It's eye-opening to see that room temperature is a major issue, especially when you consider it's one of the most controllable factors. We hope to repeat the survey, perhaps timed around the seasons, to gain a better understanding." Other common reasons for lack of sleep include work stress, illness, snoring, and excessive caffeine consumption. The study also found that the most effective solutions for sleep deprivation include changing sleeping positions, using the bathroom, turning to a screen, and taking medication. The study ranked the Top 20 most sleepless cities in Canada, with Kitchener, Ontario, at the top and Saskatoon tied with Vancouver for second worst. Interestingly, Toronto residents get the best sleep, likely due to the city's high number of singles. Cities at the top of the list tend to have more children living at home. "Kitchener is a tech-savvy community with young families, which contributes to its high ranking," Cordery said. Sleep deprivation costs companies an estimated $75 million annually due to lost productivity, according to the study. In fact, over a quarter of Canada's workforce takes sick days to catch up on sleep. Cordery emphasizes the importance of addressing sleep deprivation, stating, "It's an issue that can't be ignored. People often downplay the impact of a bad night's sleep, but it's crucial to prioritize getting a good night's sleep." To conduct the study, over 3,000 people were surveyed across Canada in April. Researchers aimed to match the Census data by surveying 1,000 people based on demographics, with the remaining 2,000 surveyed in the top 20 largest cities. Citizens aged 18 and older were asked 10 online questions. Sources: The Weather Network | Sleep-Centre.ca
Williams Advanced Engineering, the tech division of the Williams Formula 1 team, is taking a more serious approach to its electric car efforts. The company recently unveiled a lightweight electric car platform, showcasing its capabilities in this area. Although Williams is better known for its involvement in Formula One and engineering work for other car companies, it has also been associated with the all-electric Formula E. Although McLaren won the new supply contract for Formula E last year, Williams continued to invest in electric vehicle development. The company raised $125 million to build an EV battery factory in the UK, where it will produce battery packs for Aston Martin's new RapidE. Now, Williams aims to expand its services to build other electric vehicles, and today unveiled the FW-EVX, a new lightweight EV platform concept. The platform features a skateboard-like design, similar to Tesla's, which has become a standard for EVs in recent years. While details about the platform are limited, Williams highlighted several innovations, including advancements in battery pack design, cooling systems, and lightweight structures. These innovations have been integrated into a single, scalable platform, which includes a highly automated process for creating fibre reinforced suspension components that can reduce weight by up to 40%. Another notable innovation is the process of forming high-strength 3D structures from 2D materials, which has created an exoskeleton for a battery module that also contributes to the structural performance of the battery. According to Paul McNamara, Williams Advanced Engineering Technical Director, vehicle efficiency has always been a core focus for Williams, whether in Formula One or with customer projects. These technologies have the potential to greatly increase the competitiveness of electric vehicles, making them more attractive to consumers and accelerating their adoption. Craig Wilson, Managing Director of Williams Advanced Engineering, views this new concept as a new direction for the company's advanced engineering business, recognizing that the transition to a fully electric future is underway and that Williams aims to facilitate this transition as a supplier.

As I began exploring the depths of the Internet, I was struck by how many of its protocols, such as HTTP and SMTP, were based entirely on text. At first, this seemed peculiar; text is inefficient, and machines are better suited to interpret protocols. A binary setup would indeed save bytes and be more manageable for software. However, it didn't take long for me to appreciate the genius behind this decision. When everything is text-based, all you need to interact with a server is plain telnet, making it easy to explore the Net's infrastructure, which relies heavily on the Robustness Principle. This ability to understand how things work is crucial, as it allows us to use tools effectively. When we don't comprehend the tools we use, we're forced to rely on other tools to simplify complex issues, sacrificing the ability to create solutions tailored to the actual problem. In contrast, the HTTP 2.0 draft, which introduces unnecessary complexity to the HTTP protocol, is a prime example of this principle in action. By examining the current state of Web development, we can see how this principle affects the development of web-based APIs, such as REST, which is simple yet powerful when used correctly. On the other hand, SOAP, despite being text-based, is orders of magnitude more complex due to its design assumption that humans won't truly need to read or understand it. As someone with two decades of experience, I've seen firsthand the problems caused by obscurity and excessive abstraction, which can lead to a form of wilful ignorance that results in subpar code.

Throughout your life, you'll be told what to do, time and time again. Some of this advice comes from a place of kindness, while others are motivated by a desire to fit in. The conventional plan goes like this: study hard, earn good grades, attend a reputable university, find a stable job, work your way up the corporate ladder, get married, and upgrade your home every five to ten years. However, this is just one of many paths to success. What if you chose a different route? What if you dropped out of school and embarked on a solo journey across the country? What if you decided grades were unnecessary and instead pursued your passions, reading about the things that interest you? What if you volunteered in a developing country and met someone who lived a completely different life? What if you traveled the world, faced your fears, and got a few scars in the process? You have one life to live, and it's up to you to decide how you want to spend it. Consider that there are countless ways to navigate existence, and most people choose a narrow path. If you fail, at least you'll have some great stories to tell. Ultimately, it's your decision, and I'm not here to tell you what to do. However, I want to remind you that within 100 years, you and everyone you love will be gone, and the stress you're putting into your life will be forgotten. The pressure to conform is overwhelming, and it can be all-consuming. But until you decide to break free, you're at the mercy of societal expectations.

With the ongoing market turmoil and volatility in Europe and other areas, Canadian Finance Minister Joe Oliver has warned that Canada's economy is vulnerable to external factors. However, Oliver also expressed confidence that European authorities will address the situation positively, while still acknowledging that the crisis overseas could harm Canada. Interestingly, Oliver stated that Canada will continue to pursue policies to maintain its economy's strength, but Canada's current fiat currency system is detrimental to its citizens and economy. If Oliver truly wanted to strengthen the Canadian economy, he should consider the reality of this policy and work to change it. Currency competition would be a better solution than the current centralized currency system, which perpetuates debt for citizens and the nation.

The parliamentary secretary to the prime minister has entered the fray in the Ontario election. Conservative MP Dean Mastro ordered a poll over the weekend after he felt a local newspaper had downplayed the popularity of the local Progressive Conservative candidate. According to a report in The Globe and Mail, a poll by Peterborough This Week showed Liberal Jeff Leal with a 45.5-per-cent share of the vote compared to 28 per cent for his PC candidate Alan Wilson. Mastro disagreed with the poll, so he decided to conduct his own. In an interview with The Globe and Mail, he expressed concerns that the way the numbers were presented could suppress voter participation. "I was deeply concerned that publishing numbers in the fashion as presented on the cover of a weekly large circulation paper could significantly suppress voter participation," he said. "The methodology was not released, and the responses could have just as easily been provided by six-year-olds as registered voters." Mastro's poll, which he shared on Twitter, showed the Liberal and PC candidates in a dead heat at 34 per cent each. His poll numbers matched province-wide ones that had the Liberals and Conservatives virtually tied. Mastro claims his intentions are not political, stating that he is motivated by democracy. "I dispute that I'm heavily involved in the provincial election, I want people to vote in each and every election," he said. "When information is published that suggests the election is decided based on flawed methodology, it runs contrary to one of my core democratic values, which is that people need to exercise their right to vote." Despite a directive from the Prime Minister's Office to avoid becoming "the story" in provincial elections, Mastro has become a focal point. However, he is not alone. Many of Harper's MPs, including senior cabinet ministers, have made their presence known in the Ontario campaign. Citizenship and Immigration Minister Jason Kenney, Foreign Affairs Minister John Baird, Treasury Board President Tony Clement, and Labour Minister Lisa Raitt have all been active in the Ontario hustings. It's not unusual for provincial candidates to get endorsements from their local sitting MPs, but it's rare to see federal ministers making such visible forays into an Ontario election. York University political scientist Robert Drummond noted that federal cabinet ministers often try to stay out of provincial campaigns due to concerns about turning off voters. However, in this case, it seems that the Conservatives are in a good position to challenge for government, and the federal party may be willing to encourage that.

Earlier this year, a brave CRPF officer, Chetan Cheeta, was injured in an encounter in Bandipora. This incident is the latest in a series of infiltration attempts in North Kashmir. The situation has been further complicated by a recent terror attack on an army convoy in Quazigund. In response, extra forces have been deployed along the Line of Control and borders, anticipating trouble from Pakistan, which provides cover fire during infiltration bids, and an increased threat from Border Action Teams.

A full-sized replica of the San Salvador, which Juan Rodriguez Cabrillo sailed into San Diego Bay in 1542, was transported to a shipyard in Chula Vista on Wednesday in preparation for its launch next month. The vessel was built by around 500 volunteers from the Maritime Museum of San Diego over a four-year period at Spanish Landing Park, across from Lindbergh Field. A plan to transfer the vessel onto a barge was scrapped due to bad weather, but it was successfully moved to Marine Group Boat Works today. Dr. Ray Ashley, CEO of the Maritime Museum of San Diego, hailed the move as a "truly monumental day" for the museum, its volunteers, donors, and the San Diego region and state of California. Recreating the San Salvador has taken years, and the finished ship matches the original in size, weighing 150 tons and measuring 92 feet long by 24 feet wide. The materials used to construct the San Salvador came from across the globe, including Angelique wood from Suriname, Sapele wood from Nigeria, Southern live oak from Georgia, and Douglas fir from the Pacific Northwest. San Diego Mayor Kevin Faulconer praised the dedication that went into building the San Salvador, saying "You can see it in every bit of the ship, from the keel to the deck." He thanked the Maritime Museum for its efforts and expressed gratitude to the region and state for the opportunity to explore its origin story. The San Salvador will make its public debut over Labor Day weekend during the museum's Festival of Sail.
Inmates in Georgia come to the rescue of guard who collapses during work detail A guard working security at a job site in Polk County, Georgia, collapsed and the inmates jumped into action, saving his life. According to Sheriff Johnny Moats, the inmates quickly called 911 using the guard's phone, removed his gun holster and bulletproof vest, and helped him breathe. "We turned him over to make sure he was okay," said inmate Landon Ware. Fortunately, the officer is expected to recover, but Moats praised the inmates for their heroic actions, saying they "really stepped up in a time of crisis and they showed it." The sheriff expressed his gratitude to the inmates, saying they "really care about my officers." As a token of appreciation, the guard's family treated the inmates to a free lunch at the park.
Pune: Union human resource development minister Smriti Irani on Friday dispelled the notion that less funding results in poor research, countering the claims that the BJP-led government has drastically cut funding for basic science research at the Centre. Speaking at the inaugural function of the Indian Institute of Science Education and Research (IISER) in Pune to celebrate its 10-year milestone, Irani addressed two misconceptions. Firstly, she argued that no Indian institute has yet achieved a top spot in the world ranking list. Secondly, she claimed that substantial funding is not necessary for good research. Irani stated, "More money does not necessarily mean better science. For instance, millions of dollars are being spent on a fusion experiment in Orange County, but the same experiment is being carried out in Bengaluru with just Rs 2 lakh. Youngsters should focus on producing better science with limited resources." CNR Rao was among several scientists who had publicly criticized the government's alleged reduction in funds for fundamental research during the 81st annual meeting of the Indian Academy of Sciences at IISER last year. On the global ranking of Indian institutes, Irani pointed out that the myth that Indian institutes do not measure up needs to be dispelled. The world ranking only considers research in English and not in Indian languages. She also announced that an Indian ranking system was being developed to counter biases and that over 3500 higher education institutions had already participated in the framework. Irani also launched a common website for all six IISERs in the country, which will serve as a single point for information on all six institutes, including subjects available, faculty, and ongoing research projects. In her speech, Irani remembered Kaustubh Deshpande, an alumnus of IISER-Pune, who had started remedial teaching for underprivileged children in the nearby areas. Now, one of those children is a student at IISER-Pune. Irani appealed to students to encourage children from disadvantaged backgrounds to pursue higher education, as language barriers often hinder their progress. The Global Initiative of Academic Networks (GIAN) launched by the Union ministry of human resource development has now partnered with 22 state universities and features 400 foreign professors. This year, Irani announced that 1,000 foreign academics will be teaching in government institutions.
Spot prices for marijuana are gradually increasing after a sharp decline over the past year. Cannabis Benchmarks has been monitoring wholesale cannabis prices since 2015 and observed a significant drop from an average of $1,953 to a low average of $1,487 in 2016. By the first quarter of 2017, the average price had risen to $1,613. According to Jonathan Rubin of Cannabis Benchmarks, the price of wholesale marijuana is exhibiting characteristics typical of a commodity, with prices influenced by supply, which in turn is linked to harvest schedules. The market is supplied by three different sources, each with distinct harvest patterns. Indoor growers can produce multiple harvests throughout the year, while greenhouse growers typically yield four harvests annually, and outdoor growers are limited to one harvest. The outdoor harvest season often coincides with the fall, resulting in a surge of supply and subsequently lower prices. This chart illustrates the fluctuations in wholesale cannabis prices since 2015, provided by Cannabis Benchmarks. Rubin attributed the recent price spike in August to the depletion of outdoor harvests, which outdoor growers tend to sell quickly to recoup losses and then reduce sales, allowing prices to rise as they gradually increase their supply. Each year presents unique weather conditions and challenges, affecting marijuana prices. The market experienced its largest decline at the beginning of 2016, with prices not recovering until the holiday season. Rubin noted that increased supplies, improved cultivation techniques, and better supply chain management contributed to the price drop of $365 per pound or 19.7%. Prices tend to rise during holiday sales and 420 celebrations, increasing from an average of $1,487 in the fourth quarter of 2016 to $1,613 in the first quarter of 2017, despite a year-over-year decline of 17.4%. Oregon's market faced specific challenges, including a testing bottleneck at the end of 2016 and a spike ahead of full adult use markets beginning in January. Currently, there are no traditional commodity options for farmers to lock in prices. Tradiv operates a wholesale marketplace, but Rubin believes the market is experiencing an overall bust cycle. As prices decline, larger and more efficient players can sell close to their production costs, waiting for weaker growers to exit the market. This reduction in supply leads to higher prices. Rubin expects that lower commodity prices and the influx of new players will disrupt the growers, potentially leading to a period of market instability.

A senior NATO naval officer warns that ISIS is aiming to build a terrorist navy to target cruise ships in the Mediterranean. According to Vice-Admiral Clive Johnstone, the group's growing presence in Libya is a worrying sign of its plans. With control of the lawless country comes access to the sea and maritime weaponry. Johnstone believes ISIS has long ambitions to develop a maritime arm, which would pose a significant threat to shipping lanes through the Mediterranean. He suggests that an attack might not be premeditated but could occur by chance, when ships unknowingly cross paths. Johnstone describes the potential attack as more of a "mistake" or "random terrorism" that could have far-reaching implications for the Western world. Some NATO members are considering redeploying submarines from the Mediterranean to the North Atlantic to counter growing Russian influence. This move could inadvertently create opportunities for an ISIS sea attack.
The Wall Street Journal has finally joined the ranks of other major publications by signing up for Apple's subscription service, "Newsstand". This move marks a significant shift for the Dow Jones-owned business newspaper, as it now sells digital subscriptions through Apple's iTunes store in exchange for a cut of sales and control over its relationship with customers. Interestingly, this decision comes after Time Inc., the previous most prominent holdout, also signed on earlier this year. As part of this deal, Apple will retain 30% of all subscription revenue generated from "in-app" sales, and it will also control customer billing information such as credit card numbers and addresses. Like other publications, The Journal will still be able to collect subscribers' email addresses, a strategy that publishers have found effective. Notably, former Dow Jones president Todd Larsen, who left the company last summer, had reportedly opposed selling subscriptions via Newsstand. However, The Journal will continue to sell digital subscriptions through its own website, retaining all revenue and customer data. This move makes it easier for Apple customers to subscribe to The Wall Street Journal, as they can use their existing iTunes account to pay for the paper. Additionally, Newsstand allows developers to update their publications automatically via "background" downloads, making it simpler for readers to access The Journal.
Fictitious and Symbolic Creatures in Art, by John Vinycomb, [1909], at sacred-texts.com p. 91 The Crocodile as the Prototype of the Dragon The ancient saurians, including the crocodile and alligator, are the origins of the mythical dragons and hydras. The crocodile is a well-known, massive reptile that resembles a large lizard covered in large, hard scales that are difficult to penetrate, except underneath. It can grow up to twenty-five to thirty feet in length. The crocodile held a special place in the culture of ancient Egypt, its habitat being the Nile River, which is still its best-known environment. It is also found in the rivers of the Indian seas. Although it is awkward on land, it is incredibly fast in the water, chasing fish, which is its primary source of food. However, it is also a threat to dogs, other creatures, and humans who enter the water or linger too close to the bank. The crocodile is referred to as Lacerta crocodilus by Linnæus, a name derived from the Greek word κροκοδειλος (krokodeilos), of uncertain origin. The American crocodile is named after the Spanish El Legarto, the lizard. Miss Millington, in her book "Heraldry in History, Poetry and Romance," states that both the dragon and crocodile were once considered to be the same entity in ancient times. According to Philip de Thaun, in his book "Bestiarus," the word "crocodille" means "devil" in this context. Guillim, an old heraldic writer, says that the dragons are so hot that they cannot be cooled by drinking water, but they still need air to refresh themselves, as depicted in Jeremiah xiv. 6. Young, author of "Night Thoughts," in a footnote describing the leviathan (crocodile) in his paraphrase of the book of Job says: "The crocodile, being forced to hold its breath underwater, has its breath repressed for a long time, which is then released so violently when it emerges that it resembles fire and smoke. The horse does not suppress its breath for as long, nor is it as fierce and animated as the crocodile."
The City of Calgary has lost its court appeal against street preacher Artur Pawlowski. In April 2007, Pawlowski was fined after he used an amplifier to preach in a public park without a permit. Despite being ticketed, Pawlowski initially won his case, with the judge ruling that his freedoms of religion and expression had been unfairly restricted. The city then appealed, and the earlier acquittal was overturned. Calgary Court of Queen's Bench Justice R.J. Hall noted that the city had a responsibility to balance the public's concerns with Pawlowski's wishes. However, Hall also stated that citizens have a right to a healthy environment, including noise control in city parks. Pawlowski expressed that this decision is not the end of the matter, stating that he and his team will continue to fight for their freedom of expression. "We firmly believe that our freedom of expression has been unfairly taken away and that this decision infringes on our fundamental rights," Pawlowski said.

The goal is to find a company whose name will be prominently displayed throughout the stadium by December. The club is eager to move forward with this idea without delay. The Americans have a strong track record in this area, having partnered with stadium sponsors for nearly 100 years. Almost all stadiums in the US have a sponsor's name.
