The remedy for speech you disagree with is not silencing others, but rather exercising your own right to free speech to object to theirs. This is how the system works as intended. In Oregon, the Portland Mayor wanted to ban a group from assembling because he disagreed with their speech, an action that undermines the constitutional protections that safeguard us from the state's overreach. The government should never silence a citizen for their beliefs, but we, the people, have the power of speech to respond to others. This includes civil debates when they're appropriate, as well as protest speech when necessary. For instance, in Idaho, a decade ago, Fred Phelps and his "God hates F**s" group tried to protest a funeral at a Catholic church. I don't recall the specifics of their grievances, but it's clear that they sought to inflict emotional distress on the family and attendees. The community responded by rallying together. Non-religious groups joined forces with local Christian, Muslim, Jewish, and Buddhist communities to create a human shield around the church, shielding people from the protesters. Inside the shield, they held signs of love, hope, and support for the grieving family. This is how the First Amendment is used effectively. It's perfectly fine for a community to reject someone's values and organize in opposition. In this case, the protesters eventually left, their plans foiled by the community's united response.
Canada's government is responsible for ensuring the safety of its citizens and securing its borders. As such, any policy that weakens the country's security screening measures for asylum seekers is unacceptable. Last week, Sun Media columnist Candice Malcolm reported that the Trudeau government plans to expedite the process for asylum seekers from countries with a high number of approved refugee claims. This could potentially mean that some claims are approved without a hearing, or with a shortened hearing process, or even rubber-stamped without a thorough review. While many asylum seekers from the Middle East and Africa, countries where Canada has historically recognized large numbers of refugee claimants, are legitimate, some pose serious security threats, including terrorists, war criminals, and thugs. The government has responded to Malcolm's column by stating that security and criminality screening practices will remain unchanged, but some claims will still be approved without a face-to-face hearing. These concerns have been raised before. In 2015, the Senate Standing Committee on National Security and Defence reported that concerns existed about the screening process, citing a lack of face-to-face interviews with visa officers, with only 9-15% of immigrants being interviewed before arriving in Canada. Conservative leadership candidate Kellie Leitch has been criticized for suggesting face-to-face interviews to screen immigrant and refugee applicants for anti-Canadian values. However, former ambassador Martin Collacott testified that such interviews are crucial for assessing potential security threats and ensuring that applicants align with Canadian values and objectives. Canada is a generous country, but it cannot afford to be naive about who it lets in. Its procedures for making such decisions must be thorough and not haphazard.
In recent years, the increasing importance of intellectual property law has been driven by the rapid growth of globalization, digitalization, and the widespread adoption of the Internet. This shift has sparked a lively public debate around the world, with a broad range of stakeholders, including non-legal experts, now contributing to the discussion. As intellectual property law intersects with various fields such as economics, culture, health, commerce, creativity, and intellectual freedom, it is not surprising that a thriving body of literature on intellectual property issues has emerged from diverse disciplines. In 2012, the Centre for Law, Technology, and Society at the University of Ottawa organized a workshop that brought together academics from different fields to foster interdisciplinary dialogue, encourage collaborative research, and produce research that explores intellectual property law issues from unique perspectives. The papers in this book are a result of this workshop, and paperback and ebook editions are available from IRWIN LAW.

Unfortunately, the eligible age for voting has left these young supporters unable to participate. The "Babies for Bernie" phenomenon has taken over Twitter and Instagram, with mothers across the US posting pictures of their children dressed as the Democratic presidential candidate. Typically, these images feature a young child wearing glasses and sporting Photoshopped gray hair. The trend began when a Los Angeles mom, Ashia Siegel, was unable to attend a Sanders rally because she had to care for her newborn son, Natan. In an interview with CNN, Siegel explained that she didn't have a specific agenda in mind when she created the meme. Siegel launched a website, BabiesForBernie2016.com, to sell Bernie Sanders merchandise and plans to donate all proceeds to the campaign. Supporters have also gathered on an official Facebook group to share articles and news about their favorite candidate. The US military's C-27A Spartan planes, a version of the Italian-made Aeritalia G222, were seen taking off from Howard Air Force Base in Panama. The Defense Logistics Agency, working under the Defense Department, destroyed the planes after the Afghan military proved unable to keep them running. This decision came after spending nearly half a billion dollars on 20 planes for the Afghan Air Force. However, the department then scrapped 16 of the aircraft for a mere $32,000 per pound, or $6 cents, according to the Special Inspector General for Afghanistan Reconstruction. The destruction of the planes took place at Kabul International Airport, while the SIGAR was investigating the Defense Department's failed program to supply the Afghans with a fleet of twin-engine military transport aircraft. The Italian-made planes had proved too difficult for the Afghan military to maintain, leading the Pentagon to terminate the program in March 2013. By then, the department had spent at least $486 million on the aircraft, with the planes only flying for 234 hours out of 4,500 required hours between January and September 2012. In a letter to Air Force Secretary Deborah James, John Sopko requested all documentation related to the decision to scrap the planes, including an explanation for why they weren't flown out of the country to be sold and what steps were taken to obtain a refund. He also asked for information on the end use of the scrap metal sold to an Afghan company and the fate of parts that weren't scrapped, such as engines and brass components. This is particularly concerning given the potential for such materials to end up in US-made weapons used against US troops. The remaining four planes from the original 20 are currently stored at Ramstein Air Force Base in Germany. In a separate letter to Defense Secretary Chuck Hagel, Sopko requested advance notice before any of the remaining planes are moved or destroyed.
A man who broke into a supermarket wearing just a sheet and Ugg boots has been jailed for four years. David Ball, 32, from Birmingham, was drunk when he smashed into the store and stole cigarettes. He then led police on a high-speed car chase through Warwickshire before being arrested after the incident in April. Ball was sentenced at Birmingham Crown Court for aggravated vehicle taking, burglary, driving with excess alcohol, and other driving offenses. In addition to the four-year sentence, he was also given an extra 51 weeks from an earlier suspended sentence. When he was arrested on April 16, Ball had been twice and a half times over the drink-driving limit and claimed he had no recollection of the incident and had been asleep in the car. However, when shown CCTV footage, he admitted to the crime. His guilty plea was taken into consideration during sentencing. The judge noted that Ball had over 50 convictions for theft dating back to 1990 and had spent several short periods in jail. He was arrested after crashing into two parked cars in Birmingham, following a police chase. Ball pleaded guilty the next day at Solihull Magistrates' Court, where the court heard that he had gotten up in the middle of the night and stolen his girlfriend's Ugg boots and Renault Clio before the crime. The prosecution said Ball had "very little recollection" of the night. The court was shown CCTV footage of him repeatedly ram-raiding a Co-op in Balsall Common in the West Midlands. Ball's defense barrister, Tom Kenning, admitted that the offense was "extremely unusual" and that his client had demonstrated "extraordinary behavior." He said the death of Ball's grandmother had a significant impact on him, contributing to his recent crimes, and that his girlfriend was expecting their baby, which should be taken into consideration during sentencing. Ball was given three years for burglary and a 12-month sentence for aggravated vehicle taking. He was also disqualified from driving for three years. As Ball was led from the dock, he gave a thumbs-up sign to his family and friends who had come to support him.
As the clock ticked down to the end of 2011, several high-profile business stories attempted to fly under the radar, but one controversy - the Stop Online Piracy Act (SOPA) - had the potential to have a lasting impact in 2012. Introduced in October by Texas Republican Lamar Smith, SOPA aimed to tighten restrictions on the sharing and duplication of copyrighted materials online. However, opinions on the bill were far from clear-cut, and no organization felt its sting more than Internet domain registrar Go Daddy. Unlike tech giants like Google and Facebook, which publicly opposed SOPA as too restrictive, Go Daddy surprisingly backed the legislation, prompting a grassroots backlash. On Reddit, users jokingly suggested transferring their domains to competitors and calling for a "move your domain day," a notion that quickly gained traction and spread anti-Go Daddy sentiment across the internet. The issue at hand was the broad latitude SOPA gave law enforcement agencies, particularly the US Department of Justice, to punish those accused of copyright infringement. The intense backlash, including a proposed boycott, was significant enough for Go Daddy CEO Warren Adelman to reverse the company's support for SOPA. In a statement released on December 23, Adelman acknowledged the importance of fighting online piracy but emphasized the need for a collaborative approach. "We can clearly do better," he said, noting that Go Daddy will support SOPA only if the internet community does the same.

Cleveland, Ohio -- Browns coach Hue Jackson is launching a foundation in his name to tackle human trafficking in Cleveland and its surrounding areas, with a formal announcement set for July 20 at the Browns facility in Berea. This marks the first time Jackson, who recently joined Jay Z's Roc Nation, has had a foundation of his own. According to a statement, the Hue Jackson Foundation aims to support children, teens, and adults who have been exploited through human trafficking by funding organizations that advocate for their rights and provide essential services. Jackson and his wife Michelle, who have three daughters, have established the foundation out of personal conviction, and they are committed to backing non-profit organizations that empower and offer hope to victims of sexual exploitation and coercion. Ohio has been grappling with a significant number of human trafficking cases, according to statistics from the National Human Trafficking Resource Center in Washington, DC. The foundation's primary goal is to increase awareness, prevent new cases, and provide healing for those affected by this crime. Human trafficking victims are often stripped of their autonomy and self-respect through various means, including violence, physical and emotional coercion, debt bondage, and other forms of exploitation. These victims can come from any background, including age, gender, race, or socioeconomic status, and can be found in urban, suburban, and rural areas, as well as in lower-income and upper-income neighborhoods. In addition to Jackson, the event will feature speakers such as Ohio Attorney General Mike DeWine, Renee Jones of the Renee Jones Empowerment Center, and Major Lurlene-Kay M. Johnson from The Salvation Army.

If you caught our recent Volca Vibes episode where we explored the Retrokits RK002 active MIDI cable and the Volca Sample, you might have been struck by the clever microprocessor within the Retrokits MIDI cable. This processor allowed you to play Volca Sample samples chromatically and also enabled you to play all 10 samples from a regular keyboard. The cable achieved this by processing incoming MIDI signals from a regular MIDI keyboard in real-time through its clever firmware. However, we were disappointed when we reviewed the Volca FM and found it didn't respond to velocity over MIDI, which is a key aspect of the DX FM sound. The sensitivity to velocity is crucial. You can still adjust velocity using the velocity fader and record it as part of a Motion Sequence, but Retrokits have now released a new firmware for their cable that maps this in real-time. The firmware update involves dumping a .MID file into the cable and then editing it via Chrome's Web MIDI in the browser, all you need is an attached MIDI interface with the cable plugged in. Fortunately, this new firmware works as intended.
Northampton, Massachusetts, has suspended its "High Five Friday" program, which involved police officers greeting students at elementary schools every morning. The initiative was launched last December as a collaboration between the Northampton Police Department and the school district. The goal was for officers to visit four schools monthly, but the program was met with mixed reactions from the community. Police Chief Jody Kasper attended a school committee meeting where concerns were raised that some students may feel uncomfortable with a police presence at the start of their day. The department held a follow-up meeting with community members, who expressed concerns about minority students, undocumented children, and those who had negative experiences with police. After the meeting, Chief Kasper met with Superintendent Dr. John Provost, who decided to cancel the program. The department stated that while officers enjoyed greeting the kids, they couldn't ignore the possibility that some community members may view the program differently. They want students to arrive at school enthusiastic and ready to learn. Reactions to the cancellation have been mixed, with some praising the police department's efforts, while others expressed concerns about the impact on students' mental well-being. While a replacement program has not been announced, the police department is reportedly working with the school district to create a new initiative.

On Monday, the Center for American Progress hosted "In Search of Secure Borders," an event that assessed the federal government's efforts to control illegal migration. State Rep. Kyrsten Sinema, a vocal critic of Arizona's new immigration law, sat down with the Wonk Room to discuss some of Governor Jan Brewer's recent statements about undocumented immigrants. Brewer claimed that the majority of undocumented immigrants who cross into the US are drug mules, committing crimes like extortion and terrorizing families. However, Sinema disputed these claims, stating that they are false. She joined a growing chorus of voices, including Senator John McCain and T.J. Bonner of the National Border Patrol Council, who have all said that Brewer's remarks are inaccurate. Sinema emphasized the importance of fact-based debate in the political process. "It's not true, and I think that's unfortunate," she said. "In politics, we should have a variety of opinions, but we shouldn't be loose with the facts or make unfounded allegations." Sinema pointed out that Brewer's comments, like her previous ones, are not supported by evidence. When asked why she felt more comfortable stating her opinion, Sinema replied, "I believe in facts." Sinema also clarified that it's not true that the majority of undocumented immigrants are drug mules or that the majority of people in US jails are undocumented immigrants. While border crime and interior crime are serious problems, Sinema believes that they can be addressed without resorting to false information. In contrast, Brewer has defended her decision to sign off on SB-1070, claiming that Arizona is under siege by Mexican drug cartels and undocumented immigrants. However, the law only deals with people who have already crossed the border into Arizona and does not address the flow of undocumented immigrants and drug cartels. Recent reports have shown that the border has become safer in recent years. Brewer has also been accused of fabricating her claim that her father "died fighting" the Nazis, as his death occurred 10 years after World War II had ended.
Focus Features has acquired the rights to 'Won’t You Be My Neighbor?', a documentary about the life of beloved television personality Fred Rogers. The film, directed by Morgan Neville, is set to be released on June 8, 2018. This intimate portrait of Rogers reveals a creative genius who inspired countless children with his kindness and boundless imagination. As the host of Mister Rogers’ Neighborhood, Rogers was a staple on PBS for 31 seasons, with reruns continuing to air until 2008. Rogers passed away in 2003 at the age of 74 due to stomach cancer. According to Neville, the film offers a fresh perspective on Rogers, who is both familiar and surprising. "I believe Mister Rogers is the kind of voice we need to hear right now," he stated. Neville expressed his excitement about collaborating with Focus Features to share the film with the world. "Morgan once again avoids making a traditional biodoc and instead takes us behind the curtain to see how Fred Rogers navigated the cultural and social issues of the second half of the twentieth century with his own brand of forward-thinking, compassionate wisdom far beyond his time," said Focus chairman Peter Kujawski. "Mister Rogers makes us all want to be better people, and we couldn’t be more proud to be a part of telling his story today." The film was produced by Neville, Caryn Capotosto, and Nicholas Ma, and is a production of Tremolo Productions in association with Impact Partners and Independent Lens/PBS. The deal was negotiated by Endeavor Content and Judith Karfiol on behalf of the filmmakers.
Today's hearing on free speech on America's public college campuses will be streamed live on the House Judiciary Committee's website. The event, scheduled for tomorrow, will bring together several experts to discuss the state of free speech on campus. Foundation for Individual Rights in Education (FIRE) President and CEO Greg Lukianoff will lead the discussion alongside attorney and author Wendy Kaminer, Kim Colby, Director of the Christian Legal Society's Center for Law and Religious Freedom, and Professor Jamie Raskin of American University Washington College of Law. The hearing will begin at 2 p.m. ET in room 2141 of the Rayburn House Office Building. Lukianoff praised the Subcommittee on the Constitution and Civil Justice for organizing the hearing, stating, "I applaud the Subcommittee for shedding light on the serious problem of censorship on today's public college campuses and am honored to testify." In his testimony, Lukianoff will examine why many public colleges and universities continue to maintain unconstitutional speech codes and the chilling effect these codes have on students, who increasingly seek to silence speech they disagree with. Lukianoff's testimony will also explore threats to faculty speech and how the Supreme Court's 2006 decision in Garcetti v. Ceballos has left protections for faculty expression unclear. This is not the first time Lukianoff has testified about free speech on campus. In July last year, he spoke before the United States Commission on Civil Rights about how the federal government's interpretation of Title IX threatens student and faculty expression. In 2003, Lukianoff testified before the U.S. Senate's Health, Education, Labor, and Pensions Committee about university speech codes and free speech zones and their impact on campus discourse. Lukianoff emphasized that free speech on campus is an issue that needs to remain on Congress's radar, saying, "I'm hoping Congress will take meaningful action to protect the First Amendment rights of students and faculty at our nation's public institutions." FIRE, a nonprofit educational foundation, stands ready to work with legislators to ensure that campuses remain true marketplaces of ideas. FIRE's efforts to preserve liberty on campuses across America can be viewed at thefire.org.

The Saudi government has warned that it will sell off hundreds of billions of dollars' worth of US assets if the US Congress passes a bill that could hold Saudi Arabia responsible for its alleged role in the 9/11 attacks. According to the New York Times, Saudi Foreign Minister Adel al-Jubeir told US politicians last month that the kingdom would be forced to sell up to $750 billion in US Treasury securities and other assets before they could be frozen by American courts. A bill that recently passed the Senate Judiciary Committee would strip foreign governments of immunity in cases involving terrorist attacks that result in the death of an American on American soil. The New York Times reported that the Saudi threats have been a subject of intense discussions between politicians and officials from the State Department and the Pentagon. Administration officials and congressional aides have confirmed that the Obama administration has been lobbying to block the passage of the bill. The State Department stated that it stands firmly with the families of the victims and their loved ones, with Secretary of State spokesman John Kirby saying, "We remain committed to bringing to justice those who use terrorism to advance their depraved ideology." In September, a US judge dismissed claims against Saudi Arabia by the families of the 9/11 victims, ruling that the kingdom has sovereign immunity from damage claims and from insurers covering losses suffered by building owners and businesses.
The Trump administration is reportedly seeking to utilize a United Nations climate change adaptation fund to finance the construction of coal-fired power plants, according to a Bloomberg News report. An official stated that the White House is pushing to use the Green Climate Fund, to which the US has contributed $1 billion, for the development of "clean coal" power plants worldwide. The Green Climate Fund is a UN initiative aimed at redistributing contributions from affluent countries to developing nations facing the most severe impacts of climate change. The fund has supported projects such as hydropower initiatives in the Solomon Islands and Tajikistan, as well as a renewable energy push in Egypt. A Trump administration official told Bloomberg that the US plans to allocate future funds to "clean coal" and other power plants designed to produce fewer carbon emissions than existing facilities. This stance aligns with one of President Trump's primary criticisms of the Paris climate agreement, which involved a $3 billion pledge to the GCF. In contrast, former President Barack Obama was able to contribute $1 billion to the fund before leaving office earlier this year, and Trump has vowed to halt future payments. Despite this, the US still holds a seat on the GCF's governing board due to its previous contributions. The GCF's consensus-based funding approach makes it challenging for the US to push a coal project through without the support of other nations.
A 1969 Lotus Europa that had been stored in a garage for 30 years was purchased by the seller. Over their two years of ownership, the car underwent a complete overhaul, with the seller claiming nearly $1,000 was spent on penetrating oil. The vehicle is said to be nearly complete, except for various fasteners, carpet, and a few interior components that are not visible in the photos. It can be found on Craigslist in Rocky Top, Tennessee, for $3,500 or in exchange for listed vehicles. The car's body angles are limited, but the fiberglass and paint appear to be in good condition, with no visible cracks. The trim, wheels, and windows are all present, although some interior accessories, including the seats, steering wheel, and dash, are not visible. The seller notes that the dashboard may need to be replaced, and the engine details are limited, but the seller claims the Renault four-cylinder was seized and has been partially disassembled. The chassis has been stripped and painted in primer, and the suspension parts have been media blasted and powder-coated. All new bushings are included. This particular Europa is notable for its completed work, making it an attractive option for someone looking to restore a project car. With its current state, it may be possible to get it back on the road again, potentially for an additional $3,500.

Glide 0.11.0 brings several new features to simplify working with dependencies and versions. In this release, we'll explore some of these enhancements and how to utilize them effectively. As always, all changes are documented in the release notes. One notable feature is test imports, which automatically resolves test imports for your project. Any additional test imports not already listed in the glide.yaml file can be managed manually or via a flag on the glide get command. The configuration wizard, accessible standalone or following the initial glide setup, helps determine semantic versions and suitable version ranges in glide.yaml files, making this process more streamlined. While optional, the wizard provides a user-friendly interface to guide you through the process. For easier release installations, two new options have been added to the existing methods. A shell script on the Glide website can be used to install the binary in your Go binaries directory ($GOPATH/bin or $GOBIN), and a Glide PPA, maintained by Francisco Guimarães, offers a straightforward installation process via the command $ sudo add-apt-repository ppa:masterminds/glide && sudo apt-get update $ sudo apt-get install glide. Future versions will continue to improve release options. In Glide 0.11.0, there are numerous additions, including 10 new features and 12 changes. For more details, please refer to the release notes, which are more comprehensive than ever.
Excessive internet use is a key contributor to the development of school burnout. In turn, school burnout can lead to excessive internet use or digital addiction. A Finnish longitudinal research project, funded by the Academy of Finland, has identified a link between digital addiction and school burnout in both comprehensive school and upper secondary school students. The study, published in 2016, found that excessive internet use among adolescents can ultimately lead to depression via school burnout. Adolescents who lose interest in school and feel cynicism towards it are more likely to develop digital addiction. Fostering enthusiasm for learning is essential in preventing this problem. Research suggests that the most critical stage for addressing digital addiction and school burnout is around age 13-15. To support adolescents' mental health and prevent excessive internet use, promoting school engagement, building students' motivation to learn, and preventing school burnout are crucial. Depressive symptoms and school burnout are more common among girls than boys, and boys are more likely to suffer from excessive internet use. The study, which involved over 3,000 Helsinki adolescents, explored the reciprocal associations between excessive internet use, school engagement, school burnout, and depression among adolescents. The findings highlight the importance of addressing digital addiction and school burnout in young people, who are often referred to as 'digital natives' due to their exposure to mobile devices and social media from a young age. While the internet provides many benefits, including social experiences and engagement in science and technology, excessive use can also lead to burnout and depression.
While Nevada isn't expected to start selling recreational marijuana until mid 2018, a forward-thinking state lawmaker is planning to introduce legislation that could speed up the process. According to a report from CBS affiliate KLAS-TV, State Senator Tick Segerblom is drafting a bill that would allow Nevada's medical-marijuana dispensaries to temporarily sell cannabis products to the recreational sector while the retail trade is being established. This move comes after Question 2 was passed, making marijuana use and possession legal across the state as of January 3. However, consumers still face the challenge of purchasing marijuana from the black market until state officials have a handle on the regulatory affairs of the recreational sector, a process that's expected to take at least another year. In an effort to mitigate this issue, Segerblom is advocating for Nevada to follow Oregon's lead in passing legislation that would give consumers early access to legal weed through the state's medical-marijuana market. "They allowed the existing medical dispensaries to sell recreational marijuana and, based on what we've seen, there's no reason we can't do that in Nevada," Segerblom explained to KLAS-TV. The primary goal of this plan is to weaken the black market and ensure that Nevada reaps the full financial benefits of its recently passed ballot initiative, which is expected to generate around $100 million in taxes, particularly in the tourist economy. Notably, only Oregon has implemented a plan to allow consumers to purchase legal weed before retail sales are ready, largely due to the state's lawmakers prioritizing policies that discourage consumers from seeking out black-market sources for weed. If successful, Nevada's medical-marijuana dispensaries could begin selling marijuana to the general public by fall 2017.

As the organizers of HeroesHearth, I'd like to extend an invitation to all professionals, semi-professionals, and free agents to find each other using our LFG tool. Our tool is quite user-friendly. Players, teams, and organizations simply need to visit our page, navigate to the filter menu under the header, and select the criteria they're looking for. For instance, if you're seeking Master-Level Support from Europe, you can filter by region, HL/TL level, preferred role, and intensity. Click the Beacon icon in your header. 2. When you click on your listing, you'll see a window to set how long your Beacon will last. US President Donald Trump is set to issue a sweeping executive order to roll back major climate regulations introduced by the Obama administration, according to the Washington Post. The order will instruct the Cabinet to rewrite rules limiting carbon emissions from new and existing power plants, lift a moratorium on federal coal leasing, and alter how climate change is considered in federal decision-making – key elements of the Obama administration's efforts to combat climate change. The Post reports that the administration is under pressure to release the order soon, as a lawsuit challenging the Environmental Protection Agency's carbon emissions limits is currently pending. The suit was launched by conservative attorneys general, including now-EPA chief Scott Pruitt, although he is no longer a plaintiff. The EPA rule aimed to cut emissions by a third by 2030. Climate scientists have warned that the issue cannot be ignored, just as gravity cannot be escaped when one steps off a cliff. The situation is urgent, with carbon dioxide levels in the atmosphere reaching record-breaking highs. The latest measurements from the National Oceanic and Atmospheric Administration show that carbon dioxide concentrations have surpassed 405 parts per million. This milestone was predicted by scientists last year, who warned that atmospheric levels of CO2 would soon permanently exceed 400ppm. The rapid increase in carbon levels is already having a profound impact on the planet. Various studies published this week have revealed alarming trends, including the Amazon rainforest's "death spiral of drought and deforestation," the Great Barrier Reef's second major bleaching event in two years, and the devastating effects of drought on Australia's mangrove forests. The situation is dire, with massive swaths of the Middle East facing shrinking water levels, making it potentially uninhabitable for humans in just a few decades. A climate-linked late-season blizzard is barreling up the East Coast of the US, while polar heat waves have led to global sea ice melt to its lowest levels in millennia. New research also suggests that even if countries adhere to international climate commitments, the Arctic may soon be entirely ice-free in the summers. Climate scientists have repeatedly warned that human activities are to blame for the rapidly changing planet. In a letter to Pruitt, 30 prominent climate scientists reminded him that rising CO2 levels are linked to climate change. They wrote: "Just as there is no escaping gravity when one steps off a cliff, there is no escaping the warming that follows when we add extra carbon dioxide and other greenhouse gases to the atmosphere."
I've recently discovered a bug I've been aware of for over a year and have finally published a pastebin detailing it. The pastebin, available at http://pastebin.com/hMW4nH6x, shows the results of my experimentation with the issue. It appears that certain savefile hacks, similar to those used by ironhax, could potentially be used to create a homebrew exploit. The bug seems to be related to the adjectives applied to the scorpion in the game, as the crash occurs when these adjectives are present. However, I've found that the number of adjectives does not seem to affect the crash. By editing the scorpion's adjectives using a hex editor, I've discovered that the homebrew launcher could potentially be used as a loader. However, in order for this to work, it would need to execute on render. If it were to fail to execute on render, it might be possible to immobilize the player and scorpion, but this would require additional adjectives to avoid disrupting the payload. President Duterte has given the green light for the construction of temporary military facilities, as agreed upon by the US and Philippines under the Enhanced Defense Cooperation Agreement (Edca). The Supreme Court's upholding of Edca's constitutionality last year further solidified the military pact between the two countries, which was initially established in 1951 under the Mutual Defense Treaty. During a press briefing in Malacañang, Defense Secretary Delfin Lorenzana clarified that President Duterte was misinformed when he claimed American soldiers were bringing in war armaments in various locations, including Palawan, Pampanga, and Cagayan de Oro City. Lorenzana stated, "I corrected the President. I told him that no construction of Edca camps had taken place yet, and it wouldn't be allowed under our agreement." He recalled telling the President, "The equipment the US will bring in is primarily for humanitarian assistance and disaster response, such as rubber boats. It will be strategically located around the country to enable US forces to quickly provide aid in case of disasters." Lorenzana also mentioned that he had inquired about the President's decision regarding the Edca construction. The President agreed, saying, "Let's allow it, but ensure there's no stockpiling of ammunition."
As the year began, concerns about a potential trade war between China and the US, sparked by President Trump's stance on the US trade deficit with Beijing, started to fade. However, just one year later, the situation may be deteriorating. According to the Financial Times, the Trump administration has put its plans to strengthen economic ties with China on hold, citing the swollen trade imbalance and Beijing's efforts to liberalize its economy as stalled. This decision comes after the two countries' dialogue in July failed to make progress. The White House has taken a more confrontational approach in its economic relations with China, including opposing Beijing's bid to be recognized as a "market economy" at the World Trade Organization. The US Treasury Department's undersecretary for international affairs, David Malpass, stated that the Comprehensive Economic Dialogue (CED) with China is "stalled" and there are no plans to revive talks. Malpass explained that the CED was intended to promote market liberalization and economic dialogue between the two countries. However, he noted that China is not moving in a market-oriented direction, and therefore the CED remains stalled. Malpass emphasized the need for China to change its economic practices to align with global rules and market liberalization techniques. For now, China is unlikely to respond formally to the US's latest trade escalation, except for occasional angry op-eds in Global Times. However, if Trump proceeds with tariffs on Chinese imports, the situation could change. The impact of a potential trade war on the already volatile markets is increasingly uncertain.
The US's largest medical cannabis dispensary, Harborside Health Center, has won a four-year battle against federal authorities over medical marijuana. The government withdrew its action to close down Harborside's Oakland and San Jose pot shops, effectively ending the tug of war between local and federal authorities. Harborside's executive director, Steve DeAngelo, hailed the move as a significant step towards ending federal prohibition on marijuana. Harborside, which has been operating since 2006, is a model for compassionately distributing medical marijuana to patients while generating tax revenue for the city of Oakland. The center serves over 1,000 people daily in Oakland and San Jose, employing 150 staff members. Harborside was initially targeted by the US attorney, Melinda Haag, in 2012 as part of a crackdown on illegal marijuana suppliers. However, officials and customers praised the center for its role in providing medical marijuana to patients while contributing to the local economy. In 2016, Haag filed a legal action to close down Harborside, describing it as a "marijuana superstore." The Oakland store generated $28m in revenue last year, making it the second-largest retail taxpayer in Oakland. The city had chosen to regulate and tax the cannabis industry rather than allowing it to flow onto the streets. Harborside's operations were also seen as a way to fund the city's police department, which had struggled to find funding. The government's decision to dismiss its action against Harborside does not mean that federal authorities will no longer enforce the Controlled Substances Act. However, the move marks a significant shift towards recognizing the legitimacy of the medical cannabis industry in California.
Israel is on the verge of becoming the world's first cashless society under a three-phase plan recommended by Prime Minister Benjamin Netanyahu's chief of staff, Harel Locker. The plan aims to curb tax evasion and money laundering, which cost Israel around 20% of its national budget every year. According to Reuters, Locker led a government panel that unveiled its interim recommendations on Monday. The plan involves limiting businesses to cash or cash-equivalent transactions of up to 7,500 shekels initially, and 5,000 shekels after a year. Private citizens will be allowed cash transactions of up to 15,000 shekels. The new rules will also restrict the use of checks. To reduce cash usage, the panel suggested that banks issue debit cards, as this would eliminate the need for credit. Locker expressed confidence that most citizens would comply, as violating the rules would be a crime and most everyday transactions are below 5,000 shekels. The short-term guidelines set out by the committee include: - Limiting business transactions to cash or checks to 7,500 shekels immediately, and reducing it to 5,000 shekels a year from the date of legislation; - Limiting private transactions to cash or checks to 15,000 shekels; - Any violation of these limits would be a criminal offense carrying a stiff fine. The new law is expected to take effect by the end of 2014.

Mojang's upcoming game Scrolls, the next title from the creators of Minecraft, will be free to play after an initial payment. According to Markus "Notch" Persson, Minecraft's creator, the initial cost will grant a lifetime subscription to new scrolls, with additional free scrolls available through participating in ranked tournaments and other activities. Notch made this statement in a Gamasutra interview, saying that Scrolls will embody aspects of 'free-to-play' but won't be labeled as such, as it's not a 'free' game. Scrolls, a fantasy-based interactive collectible card game, has been at the center of a high-profile trademark battle between Notch and Bethesda, the makers of Elder Scrolls. In a blog post titled "I hate 'free to play'," Notch elaborated on his skepticism towards the free-to-play trend. He attributed its popularity to the success of social gaming on Facebook and the wealth generated by companies like Zynga. Notch believes that free-to-play games are designed to encourage players to spend more money, exploiting psychological traps such as sunk costs. Notch prefers a different approach, stating that he likes paying for games after purchasing them, allowing for customization and extra features. He believes that the 'free to play' model is geared towards game developers, not players. In a tweet, Notch humorously said he'd rather play Team Fortress 2 until Dota 2 is released.

South Carolina has become the latest state to rule against allowing towns to set up their own broadband networks. A recent bill makes it much more challenging for cities to establish their own ISPs that offer speeds of 190Kbps or higher. Critics argue that the bill, now awaiting the governor's signature, is an attempt by carriers like AT&T to stifle competition. In North Carolina, for instance, Time Warner Cable launched a lengthy legal battle to shut down a municipal network that provided faster speeds than its own service. Some claim that the bill was drafted and lobbied for by ALEC, a conservative think tank with ties to AT&T, although the organization disputes this, saying that private companies may struggle to compete with municipal broadband, which can rely on tax revenue, and that local solutions have "negative impacts on free markets and limited government." The US has made limited progress in expanding broadband access, and recent stimulus programs have focused on increasing availability through partnerships or independent efforts. However, cutting off a potential source of faster internet may not necessarily improve the country's overall situation.

Speaker John Boehner believes the legislation, which would take effect 15 days after adoption, could harm the NATO mission to overthrow Muammar Qaddafi. Meanwhile, a bipartisan group of lawmakers is growing increasingly frustrated with the ongoing intervention in Libya, which has been ongoing for three months. However, they are facing a challenge: the Kucinich proposal is considered privileged under House rules, allowing Kucinich to force a floor vote even if Democratic and Republican leaders oppose it. The resolution is set to "ripen" next week, potentially paving the way for Kucinich to bring it to a vote when Congress returns from its upcoming recess. The House GOP will hold a special meeting on Thursday to discuss their next move, with one option being to reschedule the vote for a later date when it is more likely to fail.
House Speaker Paul Ryan has hinted to close confidants that he plans to leave Congress after the 2018 midterm elections, Politico reported on Thursday. The 48-year-old Republican has represented Wisconsin's 1st Congressional District since 1999. In recent interviews with approximately 40 lawmakers, congressional aides, conservative intellectuals, and Republican lobbyists who are close to Ryan, not a single person believed he would serve beyond 2018. When asked if he was planning to leave "any time soon" at a press conference on Thursday, Ryan chuckled and replied, "No." Politico noted that Ryan has grown tired of Washington even before reluctantly accepting the speakership in 2015. In 2012, Ryan was Mitt Romney's running mate during the presidential election. The publication reported that Ryan has had conversations with his close advisers about his intentions, making it clear that he would like to serve through the upcoming midterm elections and retire before the next Congress convenes in January 2019. Despite still serving as House speaker, Ryan plans to focus on entitlement reform in his final legislative push. His current major push, tax reform, appears to be on the verge of passing into law.

A four-year-old boy has died after being attacked by three pit bulls outside a home in Felton, Delaware. The boy's mother was visiting a friend in the 900 block of Edwardsville Road when she saw the dogs attacking her son through a window. The mother immediately rushed outside to save her son, but was bitten on the arms several times while trying to intervene. Two repairmen who were working at the home also tried to help by fighting off the dogs with PVC piping. Despite the efforts of emergency crews, the boy was pronounced dead at the scene. The mother was taken to Kent General Hospital for treatment of her injuries. Police are not releasing the boy's name until his family has been notified. Captain Sherri Warburton, head of the Delaware Animal Care and Control, described the scene as "horrendous." The three pit bulls - two females and one male - were related and had not been spayed or neutered. Warburton believes that the pregnant female and the dog in heat may have triggered a pack mentality among the dogs, leading them to attack the child. "It's just not a good situation to leave a child unattended with dogs of this type," she said. The dogs were removed and euthanized by the First State Animal Center and SPCA, and their remains will be tested for rabies because they were not vaccinated. Warburton also revealed that the oldest female dog is believed to be the mother of the younger female and the male dog involved in the attack. She suspects that the dogs may have interbred, but does not believe they were used for fighting. Animal control officers first visited the home on Wednesday morning after receiving reports of two loose pit bulls, but were unable to find them. When they returned later, they confirmed that the attacking dogs were the same ones that had escaped earlier.

A chilly Friday afternoon about a month before the White House was set to welcome its 45th president, I visited the other residence in Washington where several presidents had once resided during the warmer months. Once known as the Soldiers' Home, it's now called President Lincoln's Cottage, a stucco villa that sits on a 250-acre tract that's been a retirement community for war veterans since the 1850s. Perched high above the city, the living memorial feels like a hushed and evocative refuge from the hustle and bustle of Washington. The cottage itself has been open to the public since 2008. Its austere beauty is reminiscent of Lincoln, who wrote the first drafts of the Emancipation Proclamation here as the evening sky crackled with cannon fire. In the morning, the president would ride his gray horse or take a carriage to his office at the White House, passing by the capital city's wartime scenes - wounded Union soldiers, grave-digging laborers at the first national cemetery, and escaped slaves singing spirituals from their makeshift camps. These harrowing images had a profound impact on America's 16th president, underscoring the stakes of his stewardship. Though Lincoln's Cottage is just a couple of miles from my home, I only recently learned of its existence. Having spent most of my life in Texas, I moved to Washington in 2005 to write a book about George W. Bush, the 43rd president. My first visit to the White House, however, left me struck by the ahistorical plainness of the West Wing.

A tanker that Libya's rival eastern government had been using to try to export oil in defiance of the Western-backed administration in Tripoli returned to the country on Saturday after being blacklisted by the United Nations. The eastern government's parallel oil company had hoped to sell the cargo of 650,000 barrels, but the UN measure required states to ban it from entering any port. The ongoing struggle for control of North Africa's OPEC state, which began in 2014, has seen two competing governments, one in Tripoli and one in the east, backed by armed factions, vying for authority. The eastern administration has set up its own National Oil Corporation in parallel to the Tripoli-based NOC. Western powers fear that any attempt by the eastern NOC to export crude independently would undermine the Tripoli government and further divide the country along regional lines. In a statement, NOC Chairman Mustafa Sanalla warned that oil exports from Libya by any entity other than the National Oil Corporation are illegal and will be stopped. He also cautioned that the Libyan individuals behind this attempt risked splitting the country with their reckless actions. The Distya Ameya tanker left Marsa el-Hariga port on Monday and was blacklisted on Wednesday before heading to Zawiya port in western Libya. Sanalla said the tanker would unload its crude in the coming days, although bad weather is currently preventing it from discharging the vessel. The eastern NOC claims legitimacy from the government and parliament based in eastern Libya, which received international recognition after the 2014 uprising against Muammar Gaddafi. The new UN-backed unity government, aimed at ending the conflict, faces resistance from hardliners in both factions, whose rivalries have steadily emerged since the 2011 uprising. A UN Security Council resolution last month emphasized that the new unity government has primary responsibility for preventing illicit oil sales and urged it to communicate any such attempts to the UN committee overseeing Libya-related sanctions.

Two men in a van were speeding along the H-1 freeway viaduct near Honolulu Airport on Thursday night, causing a three-car collision that sent an SUV crashing 30 feet down onto North Nimitz Highway, resulting in the driver's death, according to police. Traffic investigators say speed was likely a factor in the 9:10 p.m. crash, but there was no evidence of drugs or alcohol being involved. The drivers involved were identified as a 65-year-old Waipahu man and his 30-year-old passenger, also from Waipahu, who were traveling at a high speed when they rear-ended a vehicle being driven by a 26-year-old Honolulu woman. Her 10-month-old daughter and a 25-year-old man from California were also in the vehicle. The collision forced the woman to swerve into an SUV, which crashed into a guardrail and fell off the viaduct, landing on North Nimitz Highway, where the 48-year-old Aiea man, the SUV's driver, was pronounced dead at the scene. The crash marked Honolulu's 54th traffic fatality of the year, compared to 47 at the same time last year.

A tunnel dug to drain a lake blocked by the 1980 Mount St. Helens eruption is narrowing, experts warn. If it fails, Interstate 5 in Washington state could be flooded. The Spirit Lake Tunnel was built after the eruption, which blocked the lake's natural outlet into a local creek. As lake levels rose, the U.S. Army Corps of Engineers dug a 1.6-mile tunnel to provide the lake with a new outlet. The tunnel opened in 1985. However, inspections last fall and spring found that the tunnel floor was rising, causing concerns. Geologists say shifting rock formations under the surface are to blame. The tunnel's opening has been shrinking over the past few years. In 2013, it was eight feet six inches, but by 2014, it had narrowed to seven feet one inch. By 2015, the opening had reduced to six feet eight inches. The U.S. Army Corps wrote that a worst-case scenario could destroy all transportation routes to the west of the lake, including Interstate 5 and the main North-South rail lines. The tunnel still has a long way to go before it can no longer drain the lake, according to project engineer Chris Budai. "I don't think that's imminent," he said. "We have time." The Army Corps is now working on designs to fix the problem and hopes to make emergency repairs by the end of the year. A price tag for the fix has not been determined yet. Two Washington senators and a congresswoman have expressed serious concerns about the issue. They wrote that a complete failure of the tunnel could be catastrophic to Washington state on multiple levels.

Singapore is facing a shortage of rail engineering talent as its rail system expands and becomes increasingly complex. Representatives from SMRT's technical advisory panel, which was formed in 2013 to boost the company's engineering capabilities, say more rail professionals are needed. The nine-member panel, which includes experts from the Hong Kong MTR and London Underground, has discussed ways to improve SMRT's engineering capabilities. Training more rail professionals and elevating the rail engineering profession within SMRT were among the topics discussed. Professor Alfred Huan, chair of the panel since its inception, noted that the company has made significant strides in growing its in-house engineering expertise, with over 400 engineers currently employed. SMRT Trains' growth in engineers is a 96% increase since 2014, and the company believes that nurturing its people to their full potential is crucial for its future success. According to SMRT's chief technology officer, Ng Bor Kiat, the ability to solve problems as they arise is essential for the company's continued growth. Experts emphasize that SMRT needs engineers with deep engineering expertise in various areas, such as trains, signalling, and power systems, as well as those with system-wide knowledge of all aspects of rail engineering. National University of Singapore engineering dean Chua Kee Chaing highlighted the importance of developing this expertise, particularly for a complex system like the rail network. The universities in Singapore need to direct engineering students to join SMRT and SBS Transit, as well as the Land Transport Authority, to ensure that knowledge is applied to the rail network. Other topics discussed by the panel included the use of condition monitoring devices and data analytics to improve predictive maintenance processes. Noting commuter dissatisfaction with delays following the testing of the new signalling system, Professor Lee Kang Kuen noted that "teething issues" are a common experience among rail systems undergoing similar upgrades. Shutting down a line to allow for engineering and maintenance could be a solution, as seen in the case of the Victoria Line on the London Underground, but it is not currently feasible in Singapore due to the importance of its major transport links.

However, he was initially removed from the ballot for failing to comply with campaign disclosure laws, which led to a write-in campaign. According to a federal magistrate's ruling in 2015, Crawford and the commission began a "systematic purging of white county employees" after his election, including Galbreath's termination. Galbreath was not given prior notice of her termination and was instead called into a meeting under the guise of discussing performance issues. The reasons given for her firing were absenteeism, failure to carry out duties, and use of foul language, but none of the commissioners had ever raised these issues, the judge stated. Hale County has appealed the verdict, which was handed down just days after a similar verdict in the case of Ann Langford. The county has a population of 14,952, with a majority of residents identifying as black, according to the 2016 U.S. Census data.

Seeking trustworthy news? Sign up for our free newsletters. In the US, a significant obstacle to prosecuting rape and sexual assault cases often lies in the law itself. Thirty-four states, Washington, DC, and several others have statutes of limitations, ranging from 3 to 30 years, after which the perpetrator can no longer be charged. For example, in New Hampshire, the deadline to file charges is just six years after the crime, while in Connecticut, it's five years. However, some states link their statutes of limitations to reporting deadlines. In Illinois, if a survivor reports the crime within three years, the state has 10 years to file charges; if it takes longer, the case expires. Twenty-seven states waive or suspend their statutes of limitations in cases where DNA evidence identifies a suspect, but these exemptions vary widely. In Georgia, there is no time limit for rape cases where a DNA match has been made, whereas in Indiana, prosecutors must charge a suspect within one year of a DNA match. In Connecticut, the initial report must be made within five years for any future DNA match to be considered.
North Dakota's largest high-value crop farm, McMartin Farms, has filed for Chapter 7 bankruptcy for the fourth consecutive year. The farm, headquartered near the US-Canada border, voluntarily filed its bankruptcy in U.S. Bankruptcy Court in Fargo on February 10. McMartin Farms, also known as McM Inc, was a significant player in the region's sugar beet, potato, and dry bean farming operations, employing over 50 full-time staff and 100 seasonal workers. In 2016, the farm cultivated approximately 39,000 acres, including 2,000 acres of sugar beets and over 4,000 acres of potatoes, as reported by Mikkel Pates in Agweek magazine. This was down from 59,000 acres in 2012. There had been concerns about the farm's financial situation last year, prompting Agweek to feature farm president Ron McMartin Jr. in a cover story, in which he acknowledged the challenges of farming in a difficult time. The first meeting of creditors is scheduled for March 16 in Grand Forks. Despite the growing trend of larger farms in Canada and the US, farm profitability appears to be more complex than just acreage.

The Department of Justice has backed former Maricopa County Sheriff Joe Arpaio's bid to have his conviction for contempt dismissed, weeks after President Trump granted him a pardon. According to BuzzFeed News, the DOJ filed court papers on Monday that support Arpaio's request to dismiss the case, labeling it "moot." The agency claims that the pardon eliminates any potential punitive consequences from the conviction, rendering the case "moot," as DOJ lawyers wrote in the filing. The Department argues that Arpaio's conviction became "moot" due to the unpredictable nature of a presidential pardon, stating that it would be "just and appropriate" to vacate the conviction. The White House announced Trump's pardon of Arpaio in late August, praising Arpaio's decades of service to the nation. "Throughout his time as Sheriff, Arpaio continued his life's work of protecting the public from crime and illegal immigration," the statement read. The controversial former sheriff, who was an early supporter of Trump's presidential campaign, was found guilty of contempt in July for willfully violating a federal judge's order to stop racial profiling. Arpaio immediately asked U.S. District Judge Susan Bolton to reverse his conviction after Trump pardoned him, and the former sheriff launched a campaign to raise money for his legal defense fund on the night Trump granted the pardon.
China is set to make history by landing the first-ever probe on the moon's dark side in 2018, marking a significant milestone in its ambitious space program. This achievement comes two years after China successfully landed a probe on the moon's surface in 1976, with the Chang'e-3 mission and its Jade Rabbit rover. Despite several spacecraft having imaged the far side of the moon, which is never visible from Earth, no probe has ever landed on this side. China's latest mission, the Chang'e-4, is similar to its previous Chang'e-3 probe but is equipped to carry a larger payload. According to Liu Jizhong, head of the lunar exploration centre, the Chang'e-4 will focus on studying geological conditions on the far side of the moon. This research could potentially pave the way for the placement of a radio telescope on the moon's surface, which would be an ideal location for sensitive astronomical instruments. The far side of the moon is inaccessible to radio transmissions from Earth, making it an ideal location for sensitive instruments. China first announced its plans to land on the moon's dark side in September, aiming to launch a mission before 2020. However, setting a launch date of 2018 highlights the importance of China's space program to its leaders, with President Xi Jinping calling for China to establish itself as a space power. While China insists that its space program is for peaceful purposes, the US Defence Department has raised concerns about China's increasing space capabilities, citing its efforts to prevent adversaries from using space-based assets during a crisis. China's space program has sparked fears of a new arms race in space, as seen in its 2007 use of a ballistic missile to destroy an orbiting satellite. In March, the Chinese government announced plans to open up its lunar exploration program to private companies, in an effort to boost technological breakthroughs. China had previously sent a "letter of intent of cooperation" to foreign countries on its latest mission in 2015, although it is unclear if any have signed up since then.

At the nine Thane call centres, more than 2,000 people worked, duping Americans by claiming to be IRS officials and threatening them with tax investigations if they didn't pay. In a surprising study, a new, historically accurate portrait of Mr. Darcy from Jane Austen's "Pride and Prejudice" has been unveiled, vastly differing from the popular image of Colin Firth. Rather than the dashing, dark-haired hero we know, the actual Mr. Darcy would have been pale, with a pointed chin, long nose, and beardless face. His hair was surprisingly powdered white, and he was slightly undernourished, with sloping shoulders. This image of Mr. Darcy is far removed from the typical romantic hero, and ahead of the 200th anniversary of Austen's death, a study published Thursday has given fans an unflattering makeover of the beloved character. Conducted by John Sutherland and Amanda Vickery, the study, published at University College London, is the first historically accurate portrayal of the fictional character.
Netflix has finally delivered on its long-awaited promise of offline playback, a feature that users have been clamoring for since the company's inception. Starting Wednesday, users will be able to download select Netflix content, including TV shows and movies, for viewing without an internet connection - whether that's on a plane, in a car, or stuck in a crowded subway car. To make the most of this feature, users can simply click a "download" button on the titles they want to keep, with the option to select from two quality settings that balance video quality with device storage space. The initial rollout of offline playback will focus on Netflix's original content, with titles like Orange is the New Black, Narcos, and The Crown available for download. Users can expect a significant amount of content to be available offline, with the selection varying by region. Using research, customer data, and feedback from previous meetings, Metro has created a new service plan for MetroBus routes in North St. Louis County, which will become effective after the transit center opens next year. These open-house style meetings will give Metro commuters a chance to provide feedback on the proposed service plan, allowing them to interact with Metro employees and discuss how the new plan will meet their transit needs. The meetings will take place at the following locations and times: Tuesday, November 10, 6 a.m. - 9 a.m. at the Riverview Transit Center, Wednesday, November 11, 2 p.m. - 6 p.m. at the North Hanley Transit Center, Thursday, November 12, 11 a.m. - 2 p.m. at St. Louis Community College - Florissant Valley, and Tuesday, November 17, 4 p.m. - 6:30 p.m. at Trinity Catholic High School. Metro successfully competed for federal funding to plan and build the North County Transit Center, which will feature an indoor waiting area, public restrooms, concessions, and a free Park-Ride lot. The new transit hub will transform bus service in North St. Louis County by providing connections to more destinations, faster transfers, more direct and frequent service, and enhanced passenger amenities. North St. Louis County is one of Metro's fastest growing public transit markets, accounting for 19 percent of total MetroBus and MetroLink ridership.
A major breakthrough for Substratum users is on the horizon. Nicholas Chum from the Substratum team recently shared some screenshots that showcased an upcoming feature allowing users to install custom ROMs without the need for root access. The screenshots provided a glimpse into a future version of the popular theme engine, which appears to be integrated into a custom ROM with its own customization options within the main settings application. What caught our attention was the fourth screenshot, which was from a root checker application. The result showed that a Nexus 6P running Android 7.1.1 Nougat was not rooted at all. This is significant, as Substratum traditionally requires root access to function properly. However, it seems that this may soon change with a future update. According to Mr. Chum, the team thanks Ivan Iskandar and Randall Rushing for their contributions to the new feature. This development is a welcome change for users who have been hesitant to use Substratum due to its requirement for root access. Working without root should be possible on all custom ROMs that have integrated the special masquerade job engine and the new version of Substratum. While some users may be skeptical about the transition from root access to a rootless system, Mr. Chum acknowledges that this process may not be smooth at first. To address this, the Substratum team plans to release a statement to custom ROM developers, providing instructions on how to build the new version into their custom ROMs and test them before making them public. If everything goes smoothly, users can simply perform a dirty flash to update their current custom ROM.
A severe water shortage in Cibecue, Arizona, has prompted the White Mountain Apache Tribe to declare a state of emergency for the town. The shortage is attributed to the failure of pumps supplying the community with water, leaving it to operate on just 5% of its normal capacity. Navajo County is accepting donations of hand sanitizer and bottled water, which can be dropped off at the Navajo County Board of Supervisors' offices in Holbrook or the Reception Desk in Show Low. In nearby Yavapai County, Cordes Lake has also issued a state of emergency, citing issues with the water system's ability to keep up with demand due to the extreme heat. The county has asked residents to reduce their daily water consumption by 50% through voluntary conservation measures. Water is being trucked in from Prescott Valley to fill tanks, and the town has implemented a nightly shutdown of the system from 11 pm to 3 am to allow for recovery. A limited supply of bottled water is available at the Mayer Fire Station in Cordes Lakes.
The Astros are searching for a backup catcher, according to Evan Drellich of the Houston Chronicle. Drellich estimates that there's a 60% chance they'll find a suitable replacement outside the organization. Recently, the Astros have been linked to Brewers catcher Jonathan Lucroy in trade rumors, although he would likely be a starter if they acquired him. At the moment, their focus is on catchers to back up Jason Castro. The Astros have expressed interest in Bryan Holaday of the Tigers and A.J. Jimenez of the Blue Jays, both of whom are out-of-options candidates. Ryan Hanigan, a veteran from the Red Sox, could be another potential option, although his likelihood has decreased after Sox manager John Farrell expressed doubts about Christian Vazquez starting the year with the big club.
UPDATE: Lawyers for Honeywell retirees have filed an injunction to stop the company from ending medical coverage for its retirees. The request was made before a US District Court in Michigan. Earlier, David Lymangrover, a retired Honeywell employee, was looking forward to his golden years, knowing he had a pension, supplemental insurance, and prescription coverage. "I thought we should be able to live on this," he said from Mishawaka. However, he received a letter from Honeywell on April 3 stating that the company would end health coverage on June 30, 2017. The letter cited the "maturity" of the Medigap market, allowing Medicare-eligible retirees to find affordable and comprehensive health insurance and prescription coverage. A Honeywell vice president said the market has matured, making it easier for retirees to find alternative coverage. The letter also told retirees to contact the Honeywell Retirement Service Center with any questions. David's wife, Charlotte, called the number, but was told that it was being handled by a call center. "It's not like we can just go to another corporation and get our insurance back," she said. Their health coverage had paid for David's blood pressure medicine and inhalers. The supplement insurance had previously covered the rest of his expenses. "Cutting our medical and prescription coverage now is just devastating for people's lives," Charlotte added. "I'm scared because I don't know what the costs will be for us." David said his friends also received the same letter. The UAW Local 9 President, Todd Treder, is investigating the matter and says retirees can contact him at 574-209-2201 if they have questions. Honeywell released a statement saying they have decided to terminate medical and prescription coverage for certain retirees, effective June 30, 2017. They claim to be providing time for retirees to review the individual marketplace and identify alternative health insurance options. The company also mentioned that federal subsidies may be available, depending on household income, to help with the cost of coverage. Additionally, the individual Medicare supplemental insurance market has matured, offering Medicare-eligible individuals access to affordable and comprehensive health insurance and prescription coverage.
In 1978, the Supreme Court ruled in the Bakke case that racial quotas were unconstitutional in higher education. Justice Lewis Powell, in a summary, praised Harvard's approach, noting it was an "illuminating example" of a better method. Unlike the other elite Ivy League institutions, Harvard did not reserve a specific number of places for poor minority candidates; instead, it considered race as one of several "plus" factors in a student's file. Thirty-six years later, Harvard's admissions policy is being challenged in a federal district court in Boston. The plaintiffs claim that the policy is a quota system in disguise that discriminates against Asian-Americans. This is the latest and first challenge to affirmative action at a private university, initiated by activist Edward Blum, who aims to dismantle the Bakke ruling. Blum's organization, the Project on Fair Representation, recruits students who claim to have been unfairly rejected from universities that use racial preferences. The complaint against Harvard comes from Students for Fair Admissions, a group established by Blum's campaign. The group has a prominent case: a student who graduated first in their class, captained the tennis team, and volunteered for National Public Radio, was rejected by Harvard due to the university's alleged intention to limit the number of Asian-Americans on campus. Blum argues that Harvard's admissions policy is a "figleaf" to conceal racial balancing and quotas. Despite Asian-Americans making up over 27% of the applicant pool at the most selective Ivy League colleges from 2008 to 2012, they comprised only 17-20% of the students admitted. The SFFA claims this constitutes "intentional discrimination" under the Civil Rights Act of 1964. Blum hopes that this case will go to the Supreme Court, where he plans to argue that the use of race in college admissions must end.
Photos of the latest smartphones have been flooding the market, including the iPhone 5, a device that has been highly anticipated since its release. This sixth-generation phone boasts a larger screen, a more powerful processor, improved camera capabilities, and the ability to connect to faster 4G LTE wireless networks. In its first 24 hours, Apple took a staggering 2 million pre-orders. Another stylish Android option is the HTC One X, which has been praised for its beauty and performance. However, Nokia's recent decline has been evident in its Lumia 920 and 820 phones, which feature new camera technology and run the Windows Phone 8 operating system. Motorola has released a new line of Razr phones this summer, with the Razr M available for $100 with a contract. It runs the Android OS. Meanwhile, the Samsung Galaxy Nexus, a collaboration between Samsung and Google, is set to run the latest version of the Android operating system, offering face-recognition technology for unlocking the phone with a smile. The Sony Xperia Ion is notable for its 12-megapixel camera, available for just $100 with a contract. On the other hand, the Motorola Photon Q stands out for its tactile keyboard, which has been praised as one of the finest physical keyboards available on an Android device. The Samsung Galaxy Note II, with its massive 5.5-inch screen, is a "phablet," or a phone-tablet hybrid that doesn't fit in your pocket but comes with a stylus. The LG Optimus Vu is another example of a device with a large screen, designed to promote doodle scribbling and free-hand note-taking.
The Indianapolis Colts are returning to their West 56th Street complex today to kick off their eight-week offseason program, but this year, the team will feel a different dynamic when it comes to the wide receiver position. Reggie Wayne, a veteran star, is no longer with the team after opting to leave in free agency. Wayne is still weighing his options and hopes to play another season in the NFL, but it won't be with the Colts, which will be a strange sensation for the team. For the first time since before the 2001 season, Wayne won't be a part of the Colts, and for the first time since before the 1996 season, neither Marvin Harrison nor Wayne will be on the team. The Colts have a promising young receiver, T.Y. Hilton, who will take Wayne's place on the field. However, in 2015, Hilton will also be assuming a more significant leadership role, one that he's "got no choice" but to take on now that Wayne, a mentor and close friend, is gone. Hilton has spoken to Kevin Bowen, saying he's talked to Wayne just yesterday. The team's roster is stacked with experienced wide receivers, with only T.Y. Hilton having more experience than Andre Johnson and Vincent Brown, who were signed this offseason. Despite Brown fighting for a roster spot, Hilton is coming off a Pro Bowl season and is clearly the team's number one receiver, making him the natural choice to step up into a leadership role. This is good news for younger receivers like Donte Moncrief and Duron Carter. Another player taking on a bigger leadership role is safety Mike Adams, who's looking to fill the void left by the departure of Reggie Wayne and Cory Redding. Adams told Kevin Bowen that he showed leadership in 2014 but aims to step it up a notch this year. While the Colts will miss some of their top leaders, they have other players like Hilton and Adams looking to take their place, and the team still has a solid group of leaders, including Andre Johnson and Frank Gore, who were added to the mix. This doesn't mean the Colts will be lacking leaders in 2015, and that's a positive sign.
