The European Commission has given the Czech Republic, Hungary, and Poland one month to reconsider their decision to participate in the EU's migrant relocation scheme. Brussels initiated legal proceedings against the countries last month and warned Poland that if they don't comply, it will lose its voting rights. EU migration commissioner Dimitris Avramopoulos stated, "There's still time to change everything and return to normalcy." Notably, the Commission is not pursuing a case against Austria, which has also refused to accept migrants, possibly due to Austria's net contribution to the EU's finances compared to the Czech Republic, Hungary, and Poland, which receive more funds than they pay in. None of the countries appears willing to budge, and their cases could end up in the European Court of Justice after the relocation scheme ends. Avramopoulos remains resolute that migrants will still need to be relocated even after the scheme concludes, and it will be intriguing to see which country caves first.
A gunman, believed to be a white male, killed nine people at a historic black church in Charleston, South Carolina, sparking renewed scrutiny of hate crimes in the state. The state's list of active hate groups, which does not have a hate crimes law, includes 19 associations, including two Ku Klux Klan chapters. According to the Hate Crime Statistics Act, a hate crime is one that is committed with evidence of prejudice based on race, gender, or ethnicity, among other factors. Federal authorities have confirmed that the attack will be investigated as a hate crime. The list of active hate groups in South Carolina includes white nationalist, Ku Klux Klan, Neo-Nazi, black separatist, and neo-confederate groups, among others. Nationally, the number of hate groups has declined in recent years, from 784 in 2014 to 1,018 in 2011, according to the Southern Poverty Law Center. In 2013, the number of hate crimes in South Carolina dropped to 51, following a rise to 153 in 2012. The KKK had distributed leaflets in a South Carolina neighborhood in July 2014, calling for the preservation of the land and the joining of the Klan. Nearly half of the 5,922 hate crimes reported nationally in 2013 were racially motivated, with sexual orientation being the next most common, accounting for 20.8 percent of reported incidents. Rev. Al Sharpton expressed outrage, saying, "What has our society come to when people in a prayer meeting can be shot in what is deemed a possible hate crime?" The shooter, who remains at large, reportedly made a statement saying, "You rape our women and are taking over our country and you have to go," according to a survivor.
Parents outraged by a 10-year-old boy who pleaded guilty to raping an 8-year-old boy are refusing to send their children to school in a northern suburbs primary school. The boy admitted to one count of sexual penetration of a child and one count of threatening to kill. Under court orders, the boy must attend school under constant supervision while his case is being heard. The alleged victim, who is 8, does not attend the same school as the offender. On Monday morning, around 50 parents gathered outside the school to discuss their concerns about their children's safety. They expressed anger that the school had not informed them about the incident, which occurred in December. Some parents have withdrawn their children from school, fearing the boy is not always supervised. In response, the school increased security by posting two guards at the gates and displaying an electronic sign saying "all students are safe." An Education Department representative said the court set bail conditions that allowed the child to continue attending school. "The student is being constantly supervised by two staff members while at school," she stated. The representative emphasized that the Education Act guarantees every child the right to an education, even if they face criminal charges. The school is taking reasonable steps to ensure student safety. The boy is due to appear in Perth Children's Court on Tuesday.

Huntsville, Alabama – Despite the collapse of the world's largest cupcake company, Huntsville's first and only cupcake truck, Sugar Belle, is thriving and is set to unveil a second vehicle next week in Madison and at Redstone Arsenal. The company, founded by Andrew and Lee Judge in February 2013, recently purchased a 2000 Ford high-top van that is better suited for long-distance driving than its previous gas-guzzling commercial RV. The bright-teal van will feature Sugar Belle's iconic smiling cupcake design, along with hot pink rims to match. "After a year of driving our old RV, we wanted something smaller, more fuel-efficient, and easier to maintain – perfect for events," Andrew explained. The company's silver flagship truck, which has operated in front of Below the Radar in downtown Huntsville for the past seven months, is currently undergoing maintenance and kitchen upgrades to become a fully functional mobile bakery. However, downtown construction and parking challenges have posed difficulties for Sugar Belle. "It was fun at first, but it was noisy and dusty, and that didn't make it appealing to customers," Andrew said. Until the truck returns to service later this month, Andrew and Lee are searching for a permanent parking space with utilities and a small patio area. The company is accepting recommendations for a new location through its Facebook page or by emailing info@sugarbelletruck.com. In the past, a business owner had proposed a centralized food truck park in Cummings Research Park, but the idea was never pursued due to zoning issues. However, Sugar Belle is in talks with the city of Huntsville to develop a food truck park, where residents can find their favorite mobile eateries in one spot. Interestingly, NPD Group reports that cupcake servings declined by 1 percent in the year leading up to April 2014, compared to an 8 percent increase during the same period in 2011. Despite this, cupcakes remain a beloved American dessert for Sugar Belle customers. When the flagship truck is upgraded, Andrew and Lee plan to expand their menu to include other food options. "I wasn't sure if we could sustain a food truck cupcake business on its own, but it's been better than I anticipated," Andrew said. Paul Lester, who recently purchased Something Sweet Bake Shop in Daphne, believes that Crumbs' decision to close is not indicative of all cupcake businesses. He emphasizes that location can greatly impact a business's success, and the type of economic market can also play a significant role.
An Indian Mujahideen operative, Ashraf Ali, 40, was arrested in Tamil Nadu in connection with the sleeper cell in Rajasthan, which was dismantled by the state's Anti-Terrorism Squad and Delhi Police's special cell in March. The main suspect in setting up and coordinating the sleeper cell in Jodhpur is believed to be Ali, who initially operated a footwear shop in the city before the cell was discovered. Born in Uttar Pradesh, Ali had fled Jodhpur for Delhi after terrorists Saquib and Barkat were apprehended there with explosives. From Delhi, he traveled to Tamil Nadu by train, where he was staying at the time of his arrest. Simultaneously, arrests were made in Jaipur and Sikar in connection with the IM module. The arrested terrorists were questioned, leading to Ali's name emerging as a suspect, which prompted the Rajasthan ATS to apprehend him in Tamil Nadu's Cuddalore district and make him a formal arrest on Thursday.
A woman named Robyn Benson, from Victoria, was declared brain-dead in December after suffering a cerebral hemorrhage. Five months pregnant at the time, she underwent surgery on Saturday to deliver a healthy baby boy before her death the following day. Benson was on life-support at a hospital until doctors deemed the fetus viable enough to perform a caesarean section. On Monday, her husband Dylan announced the birth of their son, Iver Cohen Benson, and her passing on Facebook. He described Iver as "healthy" and "the cutest and most precious person I've ever met." Dylan also shared his grief, saying he would "miss Robyn more than words can explain" and praising her strength and love for their son. Iver will remain under hospital care until he is healthy enough to go home. After her passing, Dylan started a "Baby Iver" fund on the crowd-funding website YouCaring.com to cover some of the costs associated with caring for the newborn and planning a funeral. He initially aimed to raise $36,000 but was overwhelmed by donations, which soon exceeded $144,000. The additional funds will go towards Iver's care, education, and a new living space. Dylan expressed his gratitude to those who supported him and his family during this difficult time, thanking them for their love, kind words, and financial help.
We're living in a vastly different era for pesticides, one that's hard to remember: the pre-Silent Spring years, before the Clean Water Act and the Environmental Protection Agency. In those days, chemicals could be used almost freely, with little regulation. The creation of our safer chemical reality was largely thanks to the CWA and EPA, but pesticides still persist, contaminating our waterways and drinking supplies. A recent study by the US Geological Survey, published in Environmental Science and Technology, highlights the issue. The study analyzed water samples from over 200 points along rivers and streams across the US between 1992 and 2011, and found that insecticides and herbicides were ubiquitous. However, the levels of these chemicals are now much lower, thanks to newer, safer pesticides and increased regulation. The study reports that human-health benchmarks were rarely exceeded, with only one agricultural stream and no urban or mixed-land-use streams breaking the limits during 2002-2011. But there's a downside: the levels of pesticide pollutants in urban streams increased during the same period, largely due to two pesticides, fipronil and dichlorvos. Both of these chemicals, as well as their byproducts, are toxic to aquatic life at the levels detected. It's worth noting that the study didn't monitor for some popular pesticides, including Roundup, due to testing costs. The study's co-author, Karen Ryberg, notes that there's a lag time between developing a pesticide and having enough data to analyze it, making it a challenge to stay on top of new pesticides.

Last week's wintry weekend, with temperatures plummeting to below zero after a 4 inch snowfall, was just a warm-up for the next winter storm forecasted to hit the Adirondacks tonight. Meteorologists are predicting a significant snowfall, although they're being cautious, saying it could reach up to a foot. The current natural snow cover is just enough for sledding, but conditions are expected to be ideal after this week's Thanksgiving snow storm. However, the winter storm is likely to cause hazardous travel conditions throughout the Adirondacks and east coast, making it a good idea to reconsider plans to visit Lake Placid this Thanksgiving weekend. If you do decide to go, be sure to leave immediately and pack warm clothing, including a snowsuit.

After the devastating Orlando tragedy, 22-year-old gay Arizonan Nano Rodriguez started flying a rainbow flag at his Tempe rental to show solidarity with the victims of the worst mass shooting in modern U.S. history. In an interview with KPNX 12 News, Rodriguez explained that the flag brought him comfort, saying, "I don't really know how, but it just gave me some sort of comfort to be able to show my support." However, his landlord disagreed, expressing safety concerns in a letter to Rodriguez. The landlord cited the lease agreement, stating that the property must be "kept clean, safe, and carefully maintained." They clarified that this includes not displaying flags that could potentially promote negative reactions and harm to tenants and property. Despite his landlord's concerns, Rodriguez plans to continue flying the flag, stating, "I'm not looking to press charges or do anything drastic." However, he's aware that the City of Tempe's anti-discrimination ordinance may not provide sufficient protection, as it primarily applies to business owners' discrimination against customers. Rodriguez can, however, file a complaint with the city manager. Interestingly, a Roman Catholic neighbor who lives nearby views the flag as a form of free expression, saying, "I have Blackhawks gear hanging up, and I mean, if they want to fly it, that's fine – it doesn't bother me either way."
Caracas, Venezuela - Venezuela's public employees will only work on Mondays and Tuesdays as the country struggles with an electricity crisis. President Nicolas Maduro announced the plan to reduce working hours for at least two weeks in an effort to conserve energy. He stated that the water level behind the nation's largest dam has plummeted to near its minimum operating level due to a severe drought. Experts attribute the issue to a lack of planning and maintenance. Venezuela's socialist administration initially gave nearly 3 million public workers Fridays off earlier this month and implemented four-hour daily blackouts nationwide on Monday. The government is now extending the Friday holiday to grade school teachers, although public hospital and state-run supermarket employees will still be required to work. Venezuelans expressed disbelief at the news, which would leave most public workers at home. Those sent home will still receive pay. Some have been using their Fridays off to wait in lines to purchase essential goods, while others have been staying home to watch TV and cool their homes with air conditioning. Critics argue that this measure is not effective in saving energy. Power outages have been a persistent issue in this oil-rich nation. Maduro's predecessor, President Hugo Chavez, had promised to solve the problem in 2010, but progress has been limited.
Former Trump campaign foreign policy adviser Carter Page suggested sending then-candidate Donald Trump to meetings with Russian officials in May 2016, according to emails read during Page's testimony to the House Intelligence Committee. In a May 16, 2016, email to fellow campaign staffers, Page expressed his desire for Trump to take his place in meetings with Russian officials, stating he'd be willing to step aside if Trump was interested. During Page's testimony, he confirmed that these meetings were intended for Russia. Page revealed he was unaware that fellow foreign policy adviser George Papadopoulos was also lobbying the Trump campaign to send Trump to Russia around the same time. Papadopoulos, who was in close communication with Russian officials, sent at least 11 emails between March and August 2016 proposing a Trump trip to meet with Russian officials. In other emails read during Page's testimony, Papadopoulos boasted of receiving "incredible insights and outreach" from Russian legislators and senior members of President Vladimir Putin's administration, claiming they were just observations from a speech he attended.

A severe Ebola outbreak has struck, making it the worst on record. Liberia has taken drastic measures to contain it, closing its borders, banning public gatherings, and imposing quarantines on some communities. The virus has claimed 1,201 lives so far this year in West Africa, with over 670 fatalities reported. Unfortunately, Ebola remains a deadly disease with no effective vaccine or cure, killing up to 90% of those infected. The virus is transmitted through contact with wild animals or infected patients, and fruit bats, commonly consumed in West Africa, are believed to be a host. Symptoms begin with flu-like illness but can progress to damage kidney and liver function, impairing blood cells and leading to severe internal bleeding. The outbreak, which started in Guinea in February, has spread to Liberia and Sierra Leone, with Nigeria reporting its first case last week after a Liberian man died in Lagos. Outbreaks are only declared over after 42 days without new confirmed cases, meaning the current outbreak will persist until at least September.
Pakistani climbers have become the first team from their country to reach the summit of the world's second-highest mountain, K2. This achievement took place on Friday at 02:30 and marks the 60th anniversary of the first successful ascent of K2. Located near Pakistan's northern border with China, K2 is considered a far more formidable challenge than Mount Everest, the world's highest peak. Climbers believe that descending from the summit is significantly more perilous. A team of six Pakistani climbers, including Hassan Jan, Ali Durani, and Muhammad Sadiq, along with three Italian climbers, made it to the top as part of the K2 60 Years Later expedition. Although individual Pakistanis have previously reached the summit, this is the country's first successful team attempt. The descent, however, is notoriously tricky, and many have lost their lives attempting to return to base camp. In June, the Italian organisation supporting the Pakistani effort announced the installation of a weather station that provides vital data to the climbers and researchers monitoring high-altitude activity.

At a Kentucky Walmart, Sydney Gholston stood in line with a cart full of Thanksgiving turkeys when a friendly, white-haired man struck up a conversation behind her. As she turned around, her jaw dropped in surprise: it was actor Jon Voight. Voight, who was in town filming a movie on Friday, couldn't resist inquiring about the six frozen turkeys in her cart. He jokingly remarked that she must have a big family, but Gholston explained that the turkeys were intended for a local nonprofit shelter and pediatric convalescent center, Home of the Innocents. The center cares for about 196 residents, many of whom have limited mobility or medical conditions that prevent them from enjoying traditional Thanksgiving meals. After Gholston finished explaining, Voight pulled out his wallet and offered to pay for her groceries. With his help, she was able to transport the six turkeys back to the Home, where they will feed approximately 70 residents this Thanksgiving holiday. Meredith Pack, the marketing manager for Home of the Innocents, told CBS News that some medically fragile children don't eat the same Thanksgiving meal as others, and residents often make arrangements with family for the holiday. Voight's act of kindness was widely appreciated, and his story was shared on the Home's Facebook page, where it quickly went viral with over 4,200 shares and 11,000 likes. The nonprofit praised Voight for bringing joy to dozens of hungry children on Thanksgiving.

A Canadian case of whirling disease has been discovered in Johnson Lake in Banff National Park. To prevent its spread, access to the lake, located just east of the townsite, is being restricted. While the disease is not harmful to humans or other mammals, it can severely impact certain fish populations. As a result, fishing and recreational activities have been banned. Whirling disease primarily affects trout and salmon, causing infected fish to exhibit erratic swimming patterns before dying prematurely. The disease is caused by the microscopic parasite Myxobolus cerebralis, which can be transmitted through contaminated gear and equipment used in water sports. Conservation manager Bill Hunt from Parks Canada emphasizes the difficulty in containing the outbreak due to the parasite's tiny size, which can persist in mud for decades. "It's several times smaller than the diameter of a human hair," he notes. To prevent the spread of the disease, Hunt advises against moving fish between water bodies and proper disposal of fish entrails. He also recommends cleaning, draining, and drying boats and equipment. The Canadian Food Inspection Agency has confirmed the first case of whirling disease in Canada, and is working closely with Parks Canada and Alberta Environment and Parks to coordinate a response. Fish from other bodies of water in Banff National Park are being tested, and AEP is sampling fish downstream from the park. If you suspect a case, report it to 1-855-336-BOAT.
A bizarre standoff has erupted between a property developer and the relatives of a deceased villager whose grave sits atop a construction site in Britain. In an effort to relocate the remains, villagers were initially paid to move the graves from the cemetery to make way for construction. However, one family refused the compensation, and as a result, the sole grave remains in its current location, precariously perched 10 meters above the ground. According to reports, the grave is that of an ancestor from nearby Longbao, and its current state has drawn significant attention. The Daily Mail has revealed that the developers are now offering nearly £100,000 to relocate the grave, complete with headstone. This incident is part of a growing trend of resistance among Chinese people against land dispossession, with other notable examples including a freeway built around a single house and a lone house standing atop a mound in the middle of a construction site.
Performance Comparison of Core i3-8100, Ryzen 3 1300X, and Ryzen 5 1500X with Different Memory Configurations We conducted our testing using the Vega 64 Liquid Cooled graphics card, as we're primarily focusing on DX12 performance. In our upcoming review of the Core i5-8400, we'll also be comparing the GTX 1080 and Vega 64 graphics cards. Our testing with Battlefield 1 revealed some interesting results. The Core i3-8100 with faster DDR4-3200 memory outperformed the stock R5 1500X, thanks to a 10% boost in average frame rate. Even with DDR4-2400 memory, the 8100 was still 8% faster than the Ryzen 3 1300X. The Ryzen 3 1300X struggled to keep up with the Core i3-8100 in Ashes of the Singularity: Escalation, with the 8100 outperforming it by 21% even with slower DDR4-2400 memory. However, when paired with DDR4-3200, the 8100 matched the R5 1500X's performance. The Ryzen 5 1600 and Ryzen 7 1700 CPUs were also matched by the 8350K, demonstrating its impressive capabilities. In Civilization, the Core i3-8100 with DDR4-3200 memory was 5% faster on average and 9% faster on minimum frame rates compared to the Ryzen 3 1200. Using DDR4-2400 memory limited the 8100's performance to that of the Ryzen 3 1300X. The Ryzen 3 1300X was still faster than the 8350K in this game, while the R5 1500X was significantly faster. In F1 2017, the Core i3-8100 with DDR4-2400 memory was 13% faster than the Ryzen 3 1300X and 3% slower than the R5 1500X. Increasing the memory speed allowed the 8100 to beat the R5 1500X by 7% margin. The 8350K was 5% faster and matched the 7600K's performance, but was still 9% slower than the Core i5-8400.

Today is Indonesia's National Music Day, so we're excited to introduce you to a fascinating aspect of our nation's musical history – Indonesian psychedelic rock from the 1970s. You might not have known that Indonesia had a thriving progressive music scene back then, but don't worry if you missed it. During the 1970s, this kind of music was forced to stay underground due to strict censorship by Suharto's regime. As a result, many great songs from that era were lost and forgotten until recently. In the new millennium, after Suharto's fall, archives were uncovered, old records were rediscovered, and a wealth of old-school cool Indonesian rock is now available to everyone. The best compilation we've come across is an album called "Those Shocking Shaking Days," released by Now-Again Records. The album's official description reads: "The progressive music scene in 1970s Indonesia was largely underground due to censorship by Suharto. Performers who created music with intense fuzz, provocative lyrics, powerful rhythms, and low-fidelity sound were true rebels." This music has largely been ignored or overlooked, with Indonesia being known more for its coffee exports than its music. However, thanks to the tireless efforts of Canadian producer Jason Moss Connoy and the trust of Indonesian rock legend Benny Soebardja, Now-Again Records has secured the rights to share this anthology with you. We feel honored to shine a light on some of the most impressive bands that explored psychedelic and progressive rock, funk, and other sounds during the early days of Suharto's regime.
War inevitably throws up unexpected challenges. From Germany's use of mustard gas in World War I to North Vietnam's effective use of its air defense system in the Vietnam War, the US has consistently faced unanticipated threats in combat that require quick responses. On the ground, US troops continually adjust to changing enemy tactics with the equipment they have available. However, the part of the Defense Department responsible for training and equipping these troops has rarely been as flexible. This is a paradox that would surprise most people outside the Pentagon: the Department of Defense is ill-equipped to address urgent needs that arise during wartime. The Department has a good track record of making smart, long-term acquisitions, which has given the US a substantial qualitative advantage over potential adversaries. While the department still struggles to contain the costs of military systems, it has made significant progress in providing better value for taxpayers. The Pentagon has also pioneered advances in medical technology, particularly in areas such as prosthetic limbs and treating traumatic brain injuries and post-traumatic stress disorder. However, the same system that excels at anticipating future needs has struggled to quickly provide technology and equipment to troops on the battlefield. Over the past five years, I have worked to address this shortfall, first as undersecretary of defense for acquisition, technology, and logistics and then as deputy secretary of defense. Now that the Iraq war is over and the war in Afghanistan is drawing to a close, it is essential to understand what prevented the Pentagon from rapidly meeting immediate demands during those wars, what enduring lessons can be learned from its efforts to become more responsive, and how to put in place the right institutions to ensure success against future threats when agility is crucial.

I firmly believe that no one would argue that we live in a natural system, and this system is undoubtedly global, interconnected, and interdependent. Quantitative growth has plateaued, and even technological, cultural, and social development have hit a ceiling. This section covers the entire content of "Electromagnetic Fields and Energy" by H. A. Haus and J. R. Melcher, which serves as the primary textbook for the first half of the course. The materials included here are the dedication, table of contents, and preface. The chapter topics range from Maxwell's laws in free space to various applications of electroquasistatics and magnetoquasistatics, including superposition and boundary value point of views. This section also delves into electroquasistatic and magnetoquasistatic fields, polarization, conduction, and electroquasistatic charge relaxation. Additionally, it covers magnetoquasistatic fields, magnetization, magnetoquasistatic relaxation and diffusion, energy, power flow, and forces, as well as electrodynamic fields from different perspectives. The final chapters focus on one-dimensional wave dynamics and an overview of electromagnetic fields. The appendixes provide further supplementary materials.
A recent study analyzed Eagles fans using an adjective rarely associated with them: stable, not referring to loyalty or dependability, but emotional stability and lack of extreme mood swings. Researchers from Emory University, Mike Lewis and Manish Tripathi, measured fan happiness, sadness, and stability based on Twitter data from the 2012 NFL regular season. Eagles fans were found to be the third most emotionally stable, after Cowboys and Colts fans. New Orleans Saints fans were the happiest after wins, while Pittsburgh Steelers fans were the saddest after losses. Interestingly, Eagles fans had fewer mood swings than expected, considering their 4-12 record last season. This might have been offset by their happiness after some losses, such as the departure of head coach Andy Reid. Emory researchers concluded that a more fitting description of Eagles fans in 2012 might be 'resigned', as they seemed to focus on the next season and potential changes rather than dwelling on recent losses.

Twitter has finally allowed users to view GIFs directly on its website and mobile apps. To watch a GIF, you simply click the play button that appears on top of it, and it will start playing. Unlike some other platforms, GIFs won't automatically start playing, likely to prevent users from downloading unnecessary files. However, if you open a tweet in a new window, the GIF will start playing immediately. The addition of GIF support makes it easier to scroll through Twitter feeds, as you don't need to open a separate window each time you want to view a GIF. Notably, Facebook has refused to add GIF support, possibly due to concerns about how it would affect the appearance of its News Feed. In contrast, Twitter seems to have embraced GIFs, recognizing their entertainment value.

Barbara Loden was a standout figure in American independent cinema. Before directing her only feature film, she had won a Tony on Broadway and appeared in a few Hollywood productions. However, none of these endeavors seemed entirely fulfilling. Thankfully, she was driven to helm her own project, which turned out to be one of the most significant independent features ever made. Her film, Wanda, released in 1970, is perhaps the most poignant story of a woman adrift in the world that I've ever seen. The real world that the film portrays is deeply intertwined with my understanding and love of it. At its core, Wanda is loosely based on the true story of Alma Malone, who endured a kidnapping and robbery ordeal that ended with her imprisonment. Loden poured her entire life into interpreting this story, stating that it was largely based on her own experiences. The film's bittersweet tone is also noteworthy, as it was Loden's only feature and she passed away ten years later without completing another project. Wanda is a powerful and tragic story that explores themes of divorce, outlaws, in-laws, cheap motels, life on the run, and the sad fate of the prettiest girl in town. As a pioneer, Loden's film is a landmark achievement that I hope will inspire you to watch it.
For British mobile users, the need to curb their data enthusiasm doesn't appear imminent thanks to a forthcoming 'super WiFi' trial. The trial, spearheaded by Microsoft and supported by the UK's largest television providers, will operate in the 150MHz spectrum band that is currently avoided by terrestrial television. This unused bandwidth is also utilized in other countries and the US to minimize signal interference, with Microsoft's Dan Reed describing the spectrum as a "finite natural resource." Since there are limited options for operators, this trial represents a viable alternative. The test site is Cambridge, chosen for its dense cluster of old and new buildings, which will allow the trial to effectively test its capabilities. The trial will also offer faster speeds of up to 16Mbps, outperforming existing mobile networks. A similar rollout by British Telecom is already underway in Scotland, suggesting that this technology is poised for an imminent future in the UK. Unfortunately, the US is struggling to overcome regulatory hurdles to deploy a similar system.
In the early 17th century, American colonists at Jamestown, Virginia, were driven to extreme measures to survive the harsh conditions between 1607 and 1625. According to recent findings by scientists at the Smithsonian's National Museum of Natural History, some of these colonists resorted to cannibalism. Archaeologists discovered the remains of a 14-year-old girl whose skull was brutally split open after her death and later discarded in a trash pit with slaughtered animals. The bones were dated to the winter of 1609-1610, a period known to historians as the "starving time" when hundreds of colonists died. Archaeologist Bill Kelso said that the discovery was unusual due to the visible damage to the skull and marks on the bones. "We approached the Smithsonian's National Museum of Natural History for further research because of their proven understanding of the contextual history in this part of Virginia." Kelso stated. The girl, now named "Jane," had sustained severe injuries that suggested the colonists had indeed practiced cannibalism. Historical documents from the period spoke of widespread cannibalism, but this latest finding provides the first solid confirmation of the historical record. According to Dr. Douglas Owsley, a statement from the Smithsonian, the desperation and overwhelming circumstances faced by the James Fort colonists during the winter of 1609-1610 are reflected in the postmortem treatment of Jane's body. The recovered bone fragments showed unusual patterned cuts and chops that suggested tentativeness and a lack of experience in butchering animal remains. However, it is unclear what killed Jane, and her facial reconstruction will be displayed at the National Museum of Natural History on May 3. An image of what she might have looked like was published on the Smithsonian's website, created using forensic facial reconstruction techniques.
Hi everyone, today we're putting the new C/C++ compiler, zapcc, from Ceemple Software to the test. This innovative compiler is based on a modified version of LLVM/clang and comes with a novel caching system, making it available on Mac OS X, Linux, and Windows. It's compatible with Unix projects using CMake and Autotools. To give you an idea of its capabilities, I'll be testing it with some popular software that requires significant compilation time, such as LMMS, the professional music production platform. The tests will be conducted on Linux Mint with an i5-5200U CPU and 8GB of RAM. Let's start with the unmodified clang, which is provided by the system. The output from the make command with 5 jobs is as follows. Next, I'll increase the default memory cache in the zapccs.config file from 1200 to 2000. Additionally, we can specify which files are not to be cached. The executables are named zapcc and zapcc++, and their "inheritance" from clang is evident in the output. As you can see, there's a noticeable decrease in compilation time. To further test the performance, I'll use another source code, OpenCV, which typically takes longer to build than LMMS. The output from the make command with clang and zapcc is shown below. The time decrease is again quite significant. Zapcc would be highly beneficial for projects like Qt4/5, which can take several hours to compile all components. In a professional setting, the value of zapcc is evident, as C++ projects often involve large sizes, allowing for more efficient use of time. However, it's essential to consider the memory usage increase due to caching, so the settings should be balanced according to the target hardware. Given that zapcc is based on clang/LLVM, it's possible that it could be ported to FreeBSD, a platform gaining popularity in the professional world, making it worth considering this possibility.
A high school soccer coach and several players in Pittsburgh are under investigation after allegedly duct taping a 16-year-old autistic boy to a goal post. The incident occurred at Highlands High School's soccer fields on Sunday night. Austin Babinsack, who is autistic, claims he was left tied up for about 15 minutes while his classmates returned to the school to bring in another student. "I was really shaken, I thought I was going to be stuck there for a long time," Austin told a local news station. An off-duty state trooper found him and helped free him. "He was terrified, he could have died, he could have had a heart attack from being so stressed out, he was screaming at the top of his lungs," Austin's mother, Kristy, said. Austin's mother confirmed that her son is autistic and that the duct tape was applied to his hands, legs, and even his waist. She also claimed that the students attempted to take a photo of him while he was restrained. The school district has suspended the coach, Jim Turner, and two players for at least five days. "I'm just disappointed with the Highland soccer team, they should have been run better," Turner said in a statement. The school district released a statement saying that it takes hazing and bullying very seriously and will take disciplinary action against students who engage in such behavior. Austin's mother is trying to help him cope with the trauma, saying that she's trying to reassure him that things will get better. "Watching my kid go through this, and knowing there's nothing I can do right now to make him feel safe," she said. The mother believes that the coach knew about the incident and failed to stop it, and that adults must be held accountable before students are. Austin plans to return to school tomorrow.

I'm crowdsourcing this book, and it will feature 100 examples, each focusing on a different theme. Every few weeks, we'll tackle a new category of problems that Guile can solve. For each theme, I'll need you to write a new script within a couple of weeks. Don't worry if you're not an expert - these challenges should be manageable. What's more, you'll have the chance to earn a bounty of up to 100 USD for completing each task. This project is ambitious, and I'm not sure what the outcome will be, but I'm excited to see where it takes us.
A Utah-based electric vehicle manufacturer, the Nikola Motor Company, recently unveiled its first two products. The first is a functional, four-wheeled vehicle called the Nikola Zero, boasting a claimed range of 125 miles. Although it's decent, the real excitement lies in the Nikola One, which I believe is the most visually stunning big rig I've ever laid eyes on. Given its design to haul trailers, the Nikola One is somewhat compromised by necessity, with its shape dictated by the need to attach something to the back. However, this truck looks genuinely impressive. The claimed specifications are astonishing: it utilizes a turbine to maintain the 320 kWh battery pack, providing 1,200 miles of range between fill-ups. Buyers can choose from multiple versions powered by different turbine fuels, and the interior features a massive, Tesla-like touchscreen at its center for controlling various functions. Ultimately, what truly matters is that this truck bears a striking resemblance to a futuristic cargo pod, with its wraparound windshield being a particular standout feature. Unfortunately, none of this is based on actual products, as Nikola Motor Company merely announced renders of the Zero and One, both of which can be reserved on the company's website for $750 and $1,500, respectively. The Zero will retail for $42,000, while the One will cost a whopping $375,000. If they ever make it to market, that is – the journey of creating electric vehicles is fraught with failures.
A Surrey man's long-standing struggle with the Insurance Corp. of B.C. over wearing religious headgear in his driver's license photo has reached a critical point. OBi Canuel, 36, was told on Friday that the public insurer would not allow him to drive unless he submits to a photo without a head covering. "I was content to wait for them to change their mind," he said. "In the meantime, they never hesitated to give me paper interim licenses. This was never a problem until today." Canuel, a Pastafarian minister in the Church of the Flying Spaghetti Monster who wears a colander on his head for religious observance, claims ICBC's demands are an attack on his rights under the Canadian Charter of Rights and Freedoms. "In Canada, we have a right to express ourselves religiously, and those rights weren't put in place for familiar religions," he said. "They were meant for cases like mine that are unusual." Canuel's fight began nearly a year ago when he tried to wear headgear in his license photo, believing it was in accordance with ICBC's policy, which states: "ICBC affirms your rights to religious expression. You won't be asked to remove any headgear that doesn't interfere with facial recognition technology as long as it's worn in conjunction with religious practice or is needed for medical treatment." However, the insurer disputed this, saying in a later letter that there is no religious requirement that prohibits removing the colander. Adam Grossman, a spokesman for ICBC, refused to disclose whether the provincial insurer has a list of recognized religions or how it makes decisions on accepting religious headgear. He stated that "we will always try to accommodate customers with head coverings where their faith prohibits them from removing it." Canuel claimed that he couldn't provide proof that his faith prohibits removing the colander. Grossman noted that Canuel doesn't wear his headgear in videos posted on his YouTube account.
Three years ago, a major setback occurred for economic policy, both in this country and across Europe. Although the worst of the financial crisis had passed, economies on both sides of the Atlantic were still struggling, with sky-high unemployment rates. Despite this, the policy elite in the West inexplicably decided that unemployment was no longer a pressing concern, and that cutting budget deficits should be the top priority. In recent columns, I've argued that concerns about deficits are greatly overstated – and have documented the frantic efforts of deficit critics to keep the issue alive. Today, however, I'd like to discuss a different but related kind of desperation: the search for examples of austerity policies that have actually succeeded. Proponents of fiscal austerity, known as austerians, have made bold claims about the benefits of their approach: they argue that austerity can both prevent crises and lead to prosperity. And let's be honest, austerians have been on a quest for years to find evidence that supports their views – and they've had their fair share of romantic notions along the way. Their search began with a passionate partnership between austerians and the Republic of Ireland, which quickly turned to severe spending cuts after its housing bubble burst. Ireland was initially hailed as a model of economic virtue, with European Central Bank chief Jean-Claude Trichet declaring it a role model for all debt-ridden nations. American conservatives even went so far as to claim that Ireland's policies could serve as a template for the United States.

According to a report by Barran Liebman, the Portland City Council has established proposed rules for its new Sick Leave Ordinance. The city will review written comments from the public before finalizing the rules by November 1, 2013. Beginning January 1, 2014, employers with six or more staff members will be obligated to provide up to 40 hours of paid sick leave to employees working at least 240 hours per year. Smaller businesses with fewer than five employees will need to offer 40 hours of unpaid sick leave. This time-off is available for reasons such as: personal or family illnesses, caring for a sick family member, or dealing with a public health crisis, which may include school closures. Additionally, employees can take time off for domestic violence, harassment, or other forms of abuse. For more details on proposed rules, please visit the Barran Liebman website.
Major negative economic news was released by the International Monetary Fund on October 4, but the broadcast networks failed to cover it on the same night. The IMF released new estimates for global economic growth and specific countries, which included a cut in U.S. growth forecasts to 1.6 percent for the year and 2.2 percent in 2017. This represents a 43 percent decrease from the IMF's forecast for 2016, which had predicted a 2.8 percent growth rate. Throughout the year, the IMF had downgraded its economic predictions each quarter, citing factors such as the backlash against globalization and the rise of protectionism. The networks, including CBS Evening News, NBC Nightly News, and ABC World News Tonight with David Muir, ignored the announcement that night. Furthermore, the networks have largely overlooked U.S. government reports on the country's economic growth, including the Commerce Department's estimate of second quarter GDP growth, which was released on July 29 but not reported on by the networks between July 29 and 31. A report on September 29 found that the U.S. experienced relatively weak second quarter growth of 1.4 percent. CNBC noted at the time that this represented the weakest pace of expansion since 1949. If the IMF's forecast is correct, the U.S. may be experiencing a prolonged period of low-growth stagnation, similar to that seen since at least 1949.
Google and Yahoo's currency converters don't include Bitcoin, despite its massive popularity, with Microsoft's Bing and online currency converter Oanda being the only major search engines to include it. Neither Google nor Yahoo is willing to discuss the omission, with Google's Jason Freidenfelds stating they have no plans to add it and Yahoo simply saying they can't comment on future plans. In contrast, Microsoft and Oanda were more than happy to discuss their inclusion of Bitcoin into their currency converters, citing the need to respond to emerging trends and showcase their agility. Bing added Bitcoin to its converter in February, while Oanda added it last spring, citing its interest in financial innovation and responding to customer demand. Oanda's Courtney Gibson hinted that they may consider adding other cryptocurrencies to their converter in the future, citing their history of introducing new technologies to the market.
A recent Canadian court ruling has revealed that BlackBerry is holding onto an employment contract for Sebastien Marineau-Mes, the former SVP of Software, preventing him from leaving the struggling company after he was hired by Apple last year. The company is determined not to let a former employee break their agreement, despite the unfortunate necessity of taking this step. BlackBerry expects its employees to uphold their commitments, just as they expect the company to honor its own agreements. The Ontario Superior Court of Justice has ruled in BlackBerry's favour, confirming that the employee contract and its terms are valid. The court's decision notes that Marineau-Mes had discussed leaving BlackBerry in September 2013, was offered the VP of Core OS position in December, and accepted the role before submitting his resignation on December 23. BlackBerry sued Marineau-Mes for breaching contract terms that required at least six months' notice before leaving the company. The court agreed that Marineau-Mes must stay on for the full six months. In a statement, BlackBerry said that it's unclear if Apple will wait for Marineau-Mes to fulfill his notice period and what Marineau-Mes will do at BlackBerry during the interim period.

Xlevus, an active member of the /r/VFIO community and developer, has successfully moved his virsh-patcher to The Passthrough POST's GitHub repository, marking a significant milestone in our VFIO automation project. For those interested, virsh-patcher makes it easy to apply common adjustments, such as error 43 fixes or hugepages, through the command line interface. This tool is available on all distributions via pip and can be installed from our GitHub repository: `pip install -U https://github.com/PassthroughPOST/virsh-patcher/archive/master.zip`. To get started, you'll need a valid installation of Python and pip. Once installed, you can run the tool using the following command: `virshpatcher [FLAGS] [/path/to/target/VM/xml.xml]`. The `[FLAGS]` parameter can be set to --error43, --hugepages, --host-passthrough, or --patch, followed by the path to your XML patch file. The patches will be applied to the XML file. As we continue to develop the tool, we'll add more native patches, including performance optimizations, automatic hook-helper registration, and a "trimmer" that removes unnecessary default devices. We'd like to thank Xlevus for contributing this tool, as it helps us avoid duplicate effort and brings us closer to building a fully automated VFIO deployment system. His contributions will greatly benefit the community. You can find him on GitHub or in our Discord server. If you're interested in testing our VFIO-Tools projects, including hook-helper, vfio-select, or virsh-patcher, don't hesitate to try them out and let us know what you think. Our long-term goal is to create a user-friendly, graphical tweaking and configuration frontend, and a guest-side client that allows for seamless changes to setup, inputs, and other settings. However, this requires significant effort and time to bring large projects to fruition. If you like what we're doing and want to support us, consider becoming a Patron. This will grant you access to exclusive content on our site, private channels on our Discord, and more. The images used in this project were courtesy of Pixabay.
A hiker named Paula Reuter, 21 and from Seattle, was rescued on Thursday afternoon after being missing for several days. Reuter had been hiking with her two dogs when she got lost near Snoqualmie Pass. She was found about six miles from her car and 10 miles from a trailhead, and was spotted by deputies in a helicopter. According to the sheriff's office, Reuter was disoriented and didn't know where she was going. Reuter told police she had eaten mushrooms and built a fire to survive. Despite being missing for several days, she was said to be "pretty healthy" by the sheriff's office. Reuter was last heard from on Monday, when she posted a photo of a hillside on Instagram, and was reported missing the following night. The search for Reuter resumed on Thursday morning, with searchers concerned she had been outside for three nights. The sheriff's office said Reuter was described as a casual hiker who probably wasn't equipped to camp out, as temperatures were dropping into the 40s. A deputy found her car at the trailhead for Mason Lake and Ira Springs trails, and ground searchers returned to the area. A helicopter was also deployed, weather permitting, to continue the search for Reuter and her dogs.

Nintendo's Shigeru Miyamoto has confirmed that the Wii U is limited to one touch-screen controller per console. However, if a game requires more, a Nintendo 3DS can be used instead. According to Miyamoto, the idea is that one Wii U controller can be used with the system, and if more controllers are needed, it might be more convenient for people to use their 3DS and a way to connect it. This concept has already been seen in some multiplayer Wii U games that use up to four Wii Remote Plus controllers for additional players. Limiting the Wii U to one controller would significantly reduce costs for gamers, as each console comes with a tablet-sized touch screen. However, Nintendo is still researching whether the Wii U is technically capable of streaming to two controller screens at once. Miyamoto explained that the company is currently investigating whether it would be possible for someone to play together on Wii U with their controller at a friend's house.

It's a universal truth that all writers face writer's block at some point. When the inevitable happens, should we succumb to despair or muster the courage to push through? One effective strategy is brainstorming, which leverages the non-linear nature of our creative brains. The most conducive environment for brainstorming is indeed chaos, where lines are blurred and the mind can wander freely. I'll leave it to you to experiment with your own non-linear approach, but one technique is to write down ideas and words as they come to mind, rather than crafting complete sentences. Another way to overcome writer's block is to draw inspiration from other authors, but the question remains: where do we draw the line between homage and copyright infringement? Researching copyright law online yields a multitude of conflicting answers, but in reality, the courts often struggle to provide clear guidance. One straightforward way to avoid potential issues is to draw from history, which is replete with fascinating characters, cultures, and stories. From the enigmatic figures of history to the bizarre customs of ancient civilizations, studying the past offers a wealth of inspiration without the risk of copyright infringement. History is a treasure trove of human experience, allowing us to explore our shared nature, our triumphs, and our struggles throughout the ages. And the best part? History is vast, free, and accessible to all, making it an ideal resource for writers seeking to tap into its riches without worrying about legal repercussions. As the saying goes, those who don't study history are doomed to repeat it – but so are those who fail to draw from its rich tapestry of stories and experiences.

As antibiotic-resistant bacteria continue to rise, scientists are on the hunt for new sources of drugs. Researchers have now discovered a potential new antibacterial agent in the tiny freshwater animal Hydra. The protein, known as hydramacin-1, was identified by Joachim Grötzinger and his colleagues at the University of Kiel. This protein is unique, and its clinical value lies in its similarity to virtually no other known antibacterial proteins except for two found in the leech. In laboratory experiments, hydramacin-1 proved to be extremely effective against a wide range of bacteria, including those that are resistant to multiple drugs, such as Klebsiella oxytoca, a common cause of hospital-acquired infections. The protein works by binding to the bacterial surface, causing nearby bacteria to clump together, then disrupting the bacterial membrane. Grötzinger's team also mapped the 3-D structure of hydramacin-1, revealing that it closely resembles a superfamily of proteins found in scorpion venom. Based on this, they propose that hydramacin-1 and the leech proteins form a new family of macins.

Twitter has officially announced the demise of its TweetDeck mobile apps, which will cease to function on May 7th. Initially, the company had given a vague timeline, but they've now confirmed the exact date. As of then, the apps will be removed from the respective app stores and their Facebook integration will be disabled. However, TweetDeck for Mac and PC will continue to exist and will follow the feature updates of the web-based and Chrome versions. The web editions will be the primary experience and will receive new Twitter features first. TweetDeck was acquired by Twitter in 2011 for $40 million in cash and stock, following its founding by Iain Dodsworth in 2008. The company had received $3.8 million in funding prior to its acquisition. As a result, users are likely to switch to popular Twitter apps like Tweetbot on iOS and Carbon and Hootsuite on Android.

Hi everyone, I wanted to touch base with you about the latest update and what's coming next. We've just released the 2.04 minor update and we're currently working on another minor update to address some stability issues, including problems with barnacles and certain settlements not always appearing. We're also continuing to develop 2.1, with a focus on delivering more stability and fun gameplay. This week, I'd like to talk about some changes we're making to NPC behavior in 2.1. One of the key areas we're focusing on is AI control of ships. We want to strike a balance between making the experience fun and challenging for lower-ranked players, while also providing a decent challenge for higher-level opponents. In 2.1, we're adding a range of changes and fixes to improve the AI of NPC ships. First, we're adjusting the overall balance of skills for AI pilots. This means that lower-level pilots will have a more gradual improvement in their abilities, but higher-level opponents will see a significant boost. We're also changing how they use missiles and torpedoes, so they'll wait until the target's shields are down before using them. This will make their tactics more effective, especially at higher ranks. We're also introducing a wider range of tactics for AI pilots, depending on their skill level. Higher-ranked pilots will try to avoid damage and make fewer mistakes, while lower-ranked pilots will be more willing to take risks. We're also reducing the use of energy weapons when the AI is running hot, and limiting the use of chaff to only when necessary. Another key change is that higher-ranked ships will now flee when their shields are down, allowing them to recharge before re-engaging. They'll also keep out of range until their shields are back up, then turn to attack once more. We're also working to address some known AI issues, including getting stuck in space and misusing pips. Finally, we're overhauling the different states that AI pilots can be in, to ensure they behave more appropriately in various situations. Overall, these changes should make for a more enjoyable and challenging experience for players of all skill levels. Thanks!
A widely accepted theory to explain the pay gap between men and women unfairly shifts the blame to women themselves, suggesting they aren't actively seeking raises. However, a recent study conducted by the Cass Business School in London, the University of Warwick, and the University of Wisconsin - Oshkosh has found that Australian women were just as likely as their male colleagues to ask for salary increases. The study revealed that when comparing men and women working similar hours, men were more likely to receive a raise, with 20% of men asking for a raise resulting in one, compared to 16% for women. Around 70% of both men and women in the sample reported having asked for a raise. Although the study didn't provide solutions, Dr. Amanda Goodall, one of the authors, stated that it did help narrow down the possible explanations for the pay disparities.
Six years ago, conservative commentator Ann Coulter stood by her decision to call President Barack Obama the "r word" in a tweet, even questioning whether the term was indeed offensive. In a recent interview on CNN's "Piers Morgan Tonight," she suggested that Vice President Joe Biden might be more upset with her than people with actual disabilities. Coulter was addressing a tweet she sent after the third presidential debate between Obama and Mitt Romney, in which she praised Romney for being kind and gentle to someone with Down syndrome. She claimed she used the term because it's a synonym for "loser." Among the backlash to her post was a viral blog post by Special Olympic athlete John Franklin Stephens, who criticized Coulter for using the word as an insult. Stephens, a 30-year-old man with Down syndrome, argued that the term is offensive and that Coulter's use of it perpetuated negative stereotypes about people with intellectual disabilities. Stephens emphasized that he is not "dumb and shallow" but rather processes information more slowly than others. Coulter responded by saying that "language police" were dictating what words could and couldn't be used, and that she didn't find the term offensive. She pointed out that similar terms, such as "moron" and "idiot," were once used to describe people with mental disabilities, and that changing the word wouldn't change its definition. Coulter also suggested that those who are offended by the term have little ground, as it is not used by people with disabilities themselves. When pressed on whether the term bore a stigma similar to the "n word," Coulter said she wouldn't use it because it's a curse word.

The Armored Spherical Cabin Electric Patrol Vehicle, showcased at the Security China exhibition in Beijing, is designed for anti-riot operations by police and paramilitary forces. Its distinctive feature is the spherical cabin, providing officers with a 360-degree view of their surroundings. Unfortunately, this vehicle cannot transform into a robot or a car. The Armored Spherical Cabin Electric Patrol Vehicle is armored to Level-A, a standard set by the Chinese Ministry of Public Security, and can withstand most pistol bullets but not machine gun fire. The doors and windows are thick and heavy, resembling those of an Aston Martin, while the interior is basic and black-only. The vehicle seats two officers and is powered by an electric motor. Although the manufacturer wouldn't disclose the battery's power, it has a capacity of 15kW/h and a top speed of 80 kilometers per hour, with a range of 120 kilometers. Its curb weight is 1200 kilograms, with a maximum load of 300 kilograms and a turning radius of 4.5 meters. The vehicle can be equipped with smoke grenade launchers on the front fenders and officers can aim their guns through a hole in the window. However, the camera on top of the roof appears small and vulnerable to damage. The Armored Spherical Cabin Electric Patrol Vehicle is manufactured by Suzhou Zijing Qingyuan New Energy Vehicle Technology Corporation, a company that also produces electric vehicles for law enforcement services. The company, which is jointly owned by the automotive department of Tsinghua University and the Suzhou Automotive Research Institute, had three other interesting vehicles on display. Staff mentioned that police had expressed interest in the vehicle, but no sales had been made yet. Later that day, a police delegation visited the booth, suggesting that Zhijing Qingyuan may soon receive an order for this unique vehicle.

Researchers at Case Western Reserve University School of Dental Medicine and the Lerner Research Institute at the Cleveland Clinic have made a groundbreaking discovery about the process that releases cancer cells from the original tumor and allows them to spread and develop into new tumors in other parts of the body. They identified a specific protein called Dab2, which plays a crucial role in this process. This process is known as epithelial-mesenchymal transdifferentiation (EMT), and it has been linked to the progression of various types of cancer, including breast, ovarian, pancreatic, and colon-rectal cancers. To understand how EMT begins, the researchers worked backwards from the process to find its trigger. They found that the compound TGF-ß triggers the formation of the Dab2 protein, which in turn activates the EMT process. In experiments, they knocked out the Dab2 protein and found that EMT was not triggered. According to Ge Jin, the lead researcher, this discovery is a major breakthrough in cancer research. Most tumors originate from epithelial cells, which have specific markers on their surface. EMT occurs when some of these cells dislodge from the surface and undergo a transformation into fibrous mesenchymal cells that can migrate. Jin emphasizes that EMT is the key step in this process. As part of a six-member research team led by Philip Howe, Jin studied the biological processes that lead to cancer spread using animal models. He notes that the process is complex, involving multiple signaling pathways. If they can understand how to modulate the EMT signaling pathway, they can design drugs to delay or halt EMT cells and control tumor progression. The researchers also believe that their discovery may have implications beyond cancer, potentially shedding light on how other diseases progress.
A new report from Katz Media Group reveals that digital subchannels outperform top cable brands in terms of audience coverage and household ratings. As more people cut the cord and shave their subscriptions, the gap between digital subchannels and cable channels widens. The report found that digital subchannels, which include networks such as MeTV, Grit, and Antenna TV, offer more widespread availability than leading cable channels like the Food Network, CNN, and Discovery Channel. In fact, MeTV ranked 20th out of all cable and broadcast networks in Nielsen live-plus-seven household ratings, beating out channels like A&E, FX, and Lifetime. According to Stacey Schulman, executive VP of strategy and analytics for Katz Media Group, people tend to view digital networks as a lower-tier option for advertising investment, when in reality, they are performing at or above the level of many cable networks. The report also found that digital networks have seen significant growth in audiences during a time of relative stagnation for cable and broadcast networks. From 2016 to 2017, digital networks saw a 42% increase in average Nielsen live-plus-seven household share, while cable networks only grew 2% and broadcast networks declined 4%. Digital subchannels emerged after the conversion of broadcast TV to digital transmission in 2009 and are typically managed by local broadcast stations, which lease airtime to digital networks. These networks often draw audiences with library programming, such as classic series like "Alf" and "The Andy Griffith Show," which are geared towards specific genres.
This article offers a thorough analysis of the incentives-access dichotomy in copyright debates. The prevailing view in copyright theory is that copyright strikes a balance between encouraging authors to invest in creating cultural works and making these works accessible to the public. However, this article asserts that the dominant copyright framework conceals the possibility of developing an alternative model that can support authors without sacrificing access to creative works. The internet's abundance suggests that increasing access to cultural works to facilitate learning, sharing, and creative play should be a core objective of copyright policy. This article delves into models of supporting and coordinating cultural production without exclusivity, including crowdfunding, tips, levies, restitution, and service-based models. While these models currently fall short of providing a unified vision for copyright's two primary functions – funding cultural production and ensuring fair compensation for authors – they offer potential avenues for future research. Three potential avenues for investigation are proposed: refining our understanding of fairness in copyright rewards, conducting more empirical studies on commons models of cultural production, and critically examining the non-economic harms that exclusivity in copyright can cause.

LAS VEGAS—The Virtuix Omni is poised to bring immersive virtual reality to users' feet, much like the Oculus Rift brought it to our eyes. This 360-degree treadmill lets users walk and run in virtual reality while staying safely in place. Originally crowdfunded, the Omni has garnered significant attention over the past few years and is set to arrive at early backers later this year. I had the chance to test it at CES 2015. The Omni's design can be likened to a scene from the movie Hackers, where a user walks in a shallow, circular space within a body cage, surrounded by arms that form a waist-high ring. Users wear slip-on shoes with sensors that glide across the surface, while a harness keeps them centered and upright. During a demo, I was taken through a shooting gallery with the Oculus Rift, where I had to run through corridors and shoot targets with a gun-shaped controller. Although I used a pre-production model with only forward-walking capabilities, the final version will enable movement in any direction. At first, walking on the Omni felt awkward due to the lack of friction between the shoes and the floor. However, I eventually found a comfortable pace, but initially had to run like I was being chased, using my legs to wheel in place while bongos played in my head. This is where the cumbersome harness came in, keeping me centered and preventing me from flying forward or backward. It did its job, and I found a way to move quickly without looking like I was flailing. The sensation of skating on a waxed floor in my socks persisted, and despite the slippery movement, I ran forward with reliable control in the demo. Unfortunately, the sensors occasionally flew off my feet, so I couldn't compare my score to others. However, those minutes of shooting felt realistic. I doubt that physically running through levels in first-person shooters is feasible, given the inhuman speed and stamina of game characters. Nevertheless, virtually walking through a fantasy landscape or exploring a museum seems achievable. The Virtuix Omni is available for pre-order at $499, but will increase to $799 when it starts shipping.

Analyzing Amazon's Buckets I ran my Bucket Finder tool with a list of names, which I assumed would be the most common names people give their buckets. To avoid overwhelming the tool, I used a list of common names from Packet Storm, containing 2268 names. The results showed that 5% of the names are public, which I found to be a good hit rate. This suggests that if Packet Storm's 100,000-word list is used, we could be looking at over 5,000 public buckets to investigate. Files in Public Buckets When analyzing public buckets, I found that around one-third of users still store private files in them. This could indicate that users are intentionally choosing what files to share and what to keep private, or that certain applications require public buckets but create private files. The most common file types in public buckets were images (7,086), followed by web files (1,377), and videos/music (436). In my exploration of public buckets, I discovered that most people use S3 to store images, which often contain personal photo collections, including many photos of babies. I also found personal data, such as SSNs, company accounts, and documents that shouldn't have been online. Videos were mostly training and motivational content, while music files included large mp3 collections shared by individuals. Overall, the data was a mixed bag, with some valuable gems hidden among the large amounts of data. While there are some interesting finds, it would require significant human hours or advanced automation to identify the most valuable insights. If anyone has ideas on how to automate this process, I'd be happy to explore building it.
Serious errors in obtaining search warrants have led to innocent people being mistakenly targeted in investigations into sexual offenses, according to a report. The police have made critical mistakes in obtaining search warrants for suspected sex offenders, resulting in the wrongful targeting of innocent people and children being separated from their parents. An official report has highlighted the errors, which were revealed by the interception of communications commissioner Sir Stanley Burnton in his annual report to the prime minister. The consequences of these errors have been severe, with "appalling" outcomes related to the most intrusive powers the state can wield against its citizens. For example, two children were separated from their parents for a weekend while the parents were questioned as suspects in a child sexual exploitation case. It was later discovered that police had raided the wrong address due to an error in the documentation, and the parents were found to be innocent. Innocent people's digital devices were also subject to forensic examination by the police. The errors identified were mainly due to incorrect details being entered into software used by police to determine the location of specific IP addresses. However, internet protocol addresses are frequently reassigned by internet providers, rendering them unreliable. Burnton warned investigators not to rely on these addresses when trying to identify those hiding behind the anonymity of the internet to commit crimes. He noted that these errors were more common than acceptable, especially in cases of child sex exploitation. The impact on some victims of these errors was "appalling." Burnton reported 29 serious errors by those entitled to use intrusive powers, accounting for 0.004% of the total number of applications. In some cases, people were arrested for crimes related to child sex exploitation, their children were taken into care, and they had to inform their employers. Innocent people were treated as suspects and detained or had their homes and devices searched on seven occasions. In other cases, two innocent people were arrested and interviewed under caution by officers investigating child sex exploitation due to similar IP address errors. An investigation into a paedophile ring saw the wrong person detained and interviewed after communications data was wrongly interpreted. In a murder investigation, officers obtained a telephone number but recorded it incorrectly in a database and contacted the wrong person.

A Canadian university is now offering a comprehensive course on growing and selling marijuana, as the North American industry continues to boom. Metro Vancouver's Kwantlen Polytechnic University is launching a 14-week course that covers everything from cannabis cultivation to navigating the complex regulatory landscape of Canada's marijuana industry. Led by Experchem Laboratories' business development manager Tegan Adams, the course is titled "Introduction to Professional Management of Marijuana for Medical Purposes in Canada" and will be divided into two parts: one focusing on plant production and facility management, and the other on marketing, sales, and drug development. The course, which starts in the fall, comes with a hefty price tag of $3,000. According to the university's executive director Jim Pelton, the course takes a production-oriented approach, similar to that found in the manufacturing industry, rather than focusing on the nuances of individual plant cultivation. The Canadian National Medical Marijuana association played a key role in developing the course. Canada has 25 licensed marijuana growing establishments, and the country is not alone in incorporating marijuana education into its academic system. The Northeastern Institute of Cannabis in Massachusetts is set to launch in July, offering students a chance to learn about the rapidly growing American marijuana industry. Upon completion, students will be eligible for a "cannabis industry certification." In February, Anne Arundel Community College in Maryland launched a course focused on the business opportunities in the emerging marijuana-related industry, titled "Entrepreneurial Opportunities in Emerging Markets: Marijuana Legalization."
Apple's CEO, Tim Cook, has announced a $1 billion fund to support the creation of advanced manufacturing jobs in the US. In an interview with CNBC on Wednesday, May 3, Cook stated that this move will help the company become a "ripple effect" in the job market, as manufacturing jobs lead to more employment opportunities in the service industry. This initiative comes after repeated calls by President Trump for American companies to bring manufacturing jobs back to the US. However, Cook emphasized that Apple will not be setting up a large manufacturing operation in the US to produce its products. Currently, the company only produces the Mac Pro in the US, while the majority of its products are manufactured in China at significantly lower operating costs. The $1 billion fund may be seen as a strategic move to improve Apple's relationship with the Trump administration, potentially granting the company more leverage to advocate for concessions that benefit its business operations. Apple and other corporations are currently concerned about issues such as the suspension of expedited H-1B visa processing and the Trump administration's efforts to encourage US firms to bring back revenue held in foreign banks. Notably, Apple holds 93% of its $256.8 billion cash reserves overseas. In the interview, Cook also discussed Apple's plans to expand its workforce, saying the company is "deeply" exploring ways to grow its employee base, including developing initiatives to get more Americans coding and creating software. He stated that a company's values are essential, as they define the people who work there, and therefore, it is a company's responsibility to give back. Cook cited Apple's efforts in environmental sustainability and renewable energy as examples of how the company gives back. He also mentioned that job creation is another way for companies to give back. The details of Apple's $1 billion fund will be announced later this month. This is not the only tech company to invest in creating jobs in the US; Amazon has promised to create 100,000 new full-time positions by summer 2018, and Intel is investing $7 billion in an Arizona chip plant.

Nestle, the giant confectionery company, is planning to slash almost 300 jobs and shift the production of its iconic Blue Riband chocolate biscuit to Poland, according to unions. The job losses will primarily affect sites in York and Newcastle, as well as a few positions in Halifax, West Yorkshire, and Girvan, Ayrshire. Both the GMB and Unite unions strongly criticized the news, with the GMB calling for Nestle to "export chocolate, not jobs." The company is proposing 143 job cuts in York, 110 at Fawdon, 15 in Halifax, and seven in Girvan. GMB general secretary Tim Roache said: "It's unacceptable for Nestle to shift the production of a beloved British brand like Blue Riband to Poland. They're putting people's lives and families into turmoil for the sake of increasing profits. These factories should be exporting chocolate, not jobs. The government needs to intervene before it's too late and reassure millions of workers across the country that this isn't just the tip of the Brexit iceberg." Nestle stated: "These proposals affect four sites: York, Fawdon, Halifax, and Girvan, and may result in 298 job losses, mainly at York and Fawdon, through 2017 and 2018. We expect these to be achieved through voluntary redundancies. The proposed changes include new shift patterns and the transfer of Blue Riband production to a Polish Nestle factory, allowing us to simplify and focus Fawdon's operation. We're making these proposals to ensure these sites operate efficiently and remain competitive in a rapidly changing environment. A 45-day consultation with trade unions and employee representatives will begin as soon as possible. Nestle UK appreciates the uncertainty for employees and will work hard to support them during this difficult period." Unite national officer Julia Long said: "We'll be campaigning to save as many jobs as possible and pressuring Nestle to reconsider these plans. Rather than abandoning its UK workforce, Nestle should be investing in its UK operations and keeping production here. We'll scrutinize the company's business rationale and explore alternative approaches." Valerie Scott, a GMB northern region officer, said: "Pillaging our jobs and shipping them overseas is the worst kind of Brexit imaginable. Under this government, it's more like the Northern Poorhouse than the Northern Powerhouse."
